INDIA
ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        Eastern India, including Orissa, is the country's "rice bowl".
Despite its high potential, foodgrain yields are low, and production has
barely kept pace with population growth. During discussions between GOI
and IDA in November 1974, it was agreed jointly to review the Eastern
States, starting with Orissa, to identify critical constraints and esta-
blish a short term action program. Orissa, covering 15.5 M ha with about
6.8 M ha of cultivable land, and with a population of about 26 M contains
about 5% of India's land and 4% of its population. Farmers with less than
2 ha represent roughly 2.6 M households, cultivate 75% of all holdings, but
control less than 40% of agricultural land. Farms of 5 ha or more comprise
5% of holdings and occupy 35% of the land. Share-cropping and tenancy are
still widespread, despite land tenure legislation.
ii.       Since the Salandi Irrigation Project (Credit 14-IN) in 1961/62,
there has been no Bank Group support to agriculture in Orissa except indi-
rectly through an Agricultural Refinance and Development Corporation (ARDC)
line of credit. A Command Area Development and Irrigation Project has just
been appraised.
iii.      The stagnation of agriculture in Orissa has been partly due to
the inability of the extension service to give meaningful advice to farmers;
similarly, research has failed to communicate and focus on the majority need
for simple, low-cost improvements.
iv.       The proposed five-year project aims to increase agricultural
production, particularly foodgrains, in Orissa and comprises:
(a) reorganization of the present agricultural extension
service, following the successful pattern introduced in
Rajasthan and elsewhere;
(b) strengthening and reorientation of adaptive agricultural
research by the Orissa University of Agriculture and
Technology (OUAT);
(c) pre-service training for extension workers and
training for cooperative personnel;
(d) improved and accelerated land surveys and land
record system, to establish land ownership and
tenancy as a basis for farm credit;
(e) support for the design, testing, demonstration and
distribution of improved animal-drawn farm implements;



- ii-
(f)  ground water survey and monitoring; research, design,
installation and field testing of alternative types of
shallow tubewell and pumping techniques; and
(g)  support for farm management data collection and eva-
luation by OUAT, and strengthening of agricultural
statistics and data processing by the Orissa Bureau
of Statistics and Economics.
v.        The extension service is already being reorganized along a single
line of command from the Director of Agriculture to the village level workers
(VLW), a vital requirement for the success of the new system. The essence of
the reorganization is systematic training and regular visits, whereby each
VLW receives training and instructions one day in 14 and is given a simple
and timely message to convey to the same group of farmers he visits once every
two weeks on a regular schedule. By selecting ten contact farmers from each
of eight groups of 50-100 farmers, one VLW covers an average of 600 active
farm families. Basic extension training facilities would be expanded and
upgraded, as would training facilities for the cooperative movement.
vi.       OUAT, in close cooperation with the Directorate of Agriculture
and Food Production would embark upon a production-oriented research program.
Four applied research stations would be developed, one in each principal
agro-ecological zone together with 13 Government farms in various locations
that would be used for adaptive research.
vii.      Organization and management would be through existing institutions
which, though strengthened, would not (except for extension) be greatly
changed. The Agricultural Production Commissioner would be in overall
charge, exercising coordination and project control through the Head of a
newly created Policy Planning and Coordination Unit. The Monitoring and
Evaluation Cell in this Unit would use information from the project supported
Bureau of Statistics and Economics and OUAT Farm Management Unit as a manage-
ment tool, and would be responsible for project evaluation in terms of its
original objectives. The Department of Agriculture and Cooperatives would
directly implement most parts of the project. Other institutions involved
would be OUAT; the Board of Revenue (land records and survey); Orissa Lift
Irrigation Corporation (OLIC); and the Bureau of Statistics and Economics.
viii.     Out of the estimated total project cost of US$40 M, about US$18 M
would be for the production-oriented components of extension and research
and related training and farm implement demonstration. These represent
about US$5 per farm family at an annual incremental operating cost of US$0.8
per farm family. The proposed IDA credit of US$20 M covers 50% of total costs.
It would finance the equivalent of 100% of the foreign exchange cost (US$1.6
M) and 48% of local costs. The Government of Orissa (GOO) would provide the
remaining US$20 M.



- iii -
ix.       Main items of project cost involving procurement are:  civil works
(US$13.1 M); vehicles, including motorcycles and bicycles (US$1.7 M); and minor
equipment and furniture for research, drilling, surveying and teaching (US$3.9
M). The balance of project costs (US$21.3 M) would consist of local and over-
seas training, incremental salaries and operating costs, not involving pro-
curement. Purchases (except for motorcycles and bicycles, which would be
available to staff on credit and purchased according to individual preference)
would be bulked into single contracts wherever possible and procurement would
be by local competitive bidding under established procedures which are satis-
factory. Scientific equipment for research (US$0.3 M) would involve small
contracts, and established procurement procedures would be followed, with
local competitive bidding wherever possible, Prudent shopping through normal
trade channels would be permitted for minor equipment and furniture procure-
ment valued at less than US$50,000. IDA funds would be disbursed against 100%
of foreign expenditures and 80% of local expenditure for equipment, vehicles
and civil works, 100% against training and fellowships, and 25% against
salaries of incremental staff.
x.        No attempt has been made to estimate an economic rate of return
for the project in the usual way because of difficulties in forecasting with
any accuracy, the incremental benefits. However, partly because of large sunk
costs, whereby the efficiency of an existing service is radically improved, the
economic rate of return of the project is likely to be well in excess of the
estimated opportunity cost of capital in India (about 12%). Instead, an esti-
mate has been made of the increase in paddy production required to achieve an
economic rate of return equal to the opportunity cost of capital. This comes
to 8 kg/ha statewide from year 7 onwards, which is low and well below what is
likely to be achieved. If only 20% of farmers achieved projected benefits,
yields would still have to increase by only 40 kg/ha to have an economic rate
of return equal to *the opportunity cost of capital, and this would be readily
obtainable. The potential is about Rs 2,000 kg/ha under rainfed conditions
and 3,300 kg/ha under irrigation. At least 0.7 M out of 3.4 X farm families
would benefit from direct and indirect contact with the extension service,
and the improvement in on-farm production is expected to be achieved at
little extra cost to the farmer, who would carry out existing operations
more efficiently.
ix.       Project risks are difficult to forecast, success being dependent
upon both human response and the vagaries of the weather. However, returns
on the relatively small investment should remain satisfactory under most
conceivable conditions. Experience in other States of India indicates that
great enthusiasm is aroused by such developments, and these have been markedly
successful.
xii.      In addition to having a major impact on the target group of small
and tenant farmers, the project would provide additional on-farm employment
to about 400,000 underemployed and landless people by Year 8 and create new
jobs in ancillary activities. The project is of considerable importance to



- iv -
the successful implementation of other agricultural projects in Orissa,
particularly ground water development (Credit 540-IN), and the proposed
surface irrigation projects. The increase in food production would im-
prove nutrition, and as Orissa is traditionally a food surplus State,
contribute to foodgrain supplies in the rest of India. The project is
considered suitable for an IDA credit of US$20 M.



INDIA
ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01        Eastern India, comprising Assam, Bihar, Orissa, West Bengal and
eastern Uttar Pradesh, is the country's "rice bowl", with substantial areas of
relatively fertile alluvial soil, moderate to heavy monsoon rains, and consid-
erable, largely unexploited, surface and ground water. Yet foodgrain yields
are generally lower than elsewhere in India. Rural institutions are weak, and
most social and economic indicators are lower than for the rest of India.
During discussions between the Government of India (GOI) and the Bank in New
Delhi in November 1974, it was agreed to give special emphasis to the develop-
ment of suitable agriculture projects in the Eastern States. The proposed
project was identified in line with this new direction of Bank Group agricul-
ture operations in India. It was prepared by a GOO working group assisted by
Bank staff, the FAO/IBRD Cooperative Program (CP) and consultants. It embod-
ies a new and successful approach to extension which was pioneered in India
and incorporated into the following Bank Group projects with the assistance of
Mr. D. Benor (Senior Agricultural Extension Consultant): Chambal Command Area
Development Project (Loan 1011-IB); Rajasthan Command Area Development (Credit
502-IN); West Bengal Agricultural Development Project (Credit 541-IN).
1.02      This appraisal report is based on the findings of a mission to
India in May/June 1976 comprising Messrs P. Nelson, Y. Elkana, G. Slade, H.
Stier, B. Woods, (IDA); J. Cole (CP); H. Vieilhescaze, B. Babcock, S. Freiberg
(Consultants); and Ms R. Mason (IDA).
II. BACKGROUND
A. The Agricultural Sector in India
2.01      Agriculture accounts for 45% of India's GNP, 70% of employment, and
provides the raw material for major commodity exports. The current ten-year
agricultural growth rate is about 3%. Compared with previous decades, yield
increases have contributed more to growth than cropped area expansion, improved
yields resulting mainly from more intensive use of high-yielding varieties
(HYV), fertilizer, pesticides and irrigation. GOI Ministry of Agriculture
and Irrigation is responsible for agriculture country-wide, but agricultural
development is specifically a State subject.
1/  The CP missions comprised J. Macartney (Mission Leader), J. Cole
(Agricultural Economist), M. Fenn (Marketing), F. Vita (Agricultural
Economist) and I. Mathews (Consultant Engineer).



B. The Agricultural Sector in Orissa (Annex 1)
2.02        Orissa, on the shores of the Bay of Bengal, has a population
of about 26 M and a land area of some 15.5 M ha, 5% of India's total.
Forests cover 32% and other uses account for 24%, leaving about 6.8 M ha
for cultivation; yet agriculture contributes about 60% of Orissa's GDP.
Farmers and agricultural laborers make up about 76% of the total labor force.
Scheduled castes and tribes, concentrated in more backward districts, consti-
tute 38% of the population.
2.03        Data on the agricultural sector in Orissa is generally scarce
and not very accurate. Two GOO agencies are responsible for most agricul-
tural statistics: the Bureau of Statistics and Economics (BSE), primarily
for final estimates of rice area, production and yield; the Statistics Section
of Directorate of Agriculture and Food Production (DAFP), for all forecasts
and final estimates on 33 minor crops. Data for sector planning is not read-
ily available. Except for some work by the Orissa University of Agriculture
and Technology (OUAT), there is little information on costs and returns of
rice production, on which almost all of about 3.5 M farmers depend.
Climate
2.04        Temperatures permit all year cropping, though some crops are
naturally limited to specific seasons. Heavy clouds during the monsoon and
shorter daylight in the cool season, are also limiting factors. However, the
principal climatic constraint in Orissa is rainfall distribution. The average
of about 1,500 mm, is adequate; but about 80% falls in about 60 days during
the June-Sept monsoon (kharif) and variation both in amount and timeliness is
great. Rainfall probabilities and maturation periods of various crops show
that a more assured kharif harvest depends on timely planting, short-term
100-110 day cereal varieties, and supplementary irrigation.
Soils and Topography
2.05      Residual soils of the Orissa uplands, about 47% of the cultivable
land, are predominantly lateritic, highly leached, acidic and low in organic
matter and nitrogen. They tend to be shallow, sandy and erodible, with asso-
ciated low water retention. Transported soils of the alluvial plain (30%)
are moderately fertile, less acidic and generally more productive. Highly
suitable for paddy, the lower lands (23%) are subject to flooding of varying
intensity and duration.
2.06      Rainfall naturally drains from the uplands, which are more drought
prone. The medium lands and non-flooded low lands have acceptable water
control. It is common for farmers to own parcels of all three classes, a
logical reason for fragmentation since this acts as insurance against dis-
trict crop failures. However, such fragmentation is frequently excessive.



- 3-
Land Tenure and Distribution
2.07        Some of the reasons why it is difficult to raise agricultural
productivity in Orissa are: the large number of small farms; the insecurity
of tenancy arrangements; and the persistence of sharecropping. "Small" and
"marginal" farmers (less than 2 ha) represent roughly 2.6 M households. Though
operating over 75% of all holdings, they control less than 40% of cultivable
land, while farmers with 5 ha or more (less than 7% of holdings) control about
35%. Sharecropping and tenancy are still widespread, despite land tenure
legislation.
Yields and Production Patterns
2.08        Recorded yields, among India's lowest, are 1.2 T/ha for rainfed
paddy and 1.5 T/ha for irrigated kharif paddy, about half those of Punjab,
Haryana and Tamil Nadu. The low irrigation response is probably due to
poor water control and excessive flooding, which is known to occur. Even
in West Bengal, with similar weather, yields are 30% higher. Rabi paddy
yields under irrigation reach 2.0 T/ha (2.7 T in W Bengal). Kharif paddy
HYV is only about 10% of total planted area, though most rabi crop is HYV.
Wheat yields, on the other hand, are higher than the all-India average,
because wheat is grown mainly under irrigation, using modern varieties and
quite large amounts of chemical fertilizer. Pulse yields are roughly the
same as all-India.
2.09        Orissa agricultural production has virtually stagnated.  Rice
production in 1973/74, 4.4 M tons, was exactly the same as reported in 1964/65;
considerably lower in some intervening years; all the more puzzling because,
at this time, farmers elsewhere in India were benefitting from HYV, chemical
fertilizers and tubewells. A few minor crops showed large percentage increases;
but their base was small and the overall effect negligible.
Organization
2.10        Orissa is divided into 13 Administrative Districts, each headed
by a Collector; 30 Agricultural Districts, headed by a District Agricultural
Officer (DAO); and 314 Development Blocks, the local rural administrative unit,
headed by a Block Development Officer (BDO). The 30 Agricultural Districts
are grouped into six Ranges, each under a Deputy Director of Agriculture.
These are currently being reorganized and extended into 13 Ranges, correspond-
ing approximately to the Administrative Districts. The Directorate of Agri-
culture and Food Production (DAFP) is headed by a Director responsible to
the Secretary for Agriculture and Cooperation (see Chart 16273).
Agricultural Extension (Annex 3)
2.11        Responsibility for agricultural extension was divided, until
recently, with most Village Level Workers (VLW) employed multi-purpose, attend-
ing many duties unrelated to agriculture. They worked under the Department of
Community Development and Social Welfare, were administratively responsible to



-4-
the BDO and supervised by him, while technically backed by DAFP. The BDO, in
turn, is responsible to the Collector and is also Secretary of the Panchayat
Samiti, a local political grouping. Some VLW were on special crop development
schemes, directly under DAFP. There was little contact between the agricul-
tural officers of DAFP and the staff of OUAT. Lines of command, supervision
and technology flow were complex and diffuse. These problems were compounded
by poor training; severe transport shortage; lack of promotion opportunities;
and difficult living and working conditions.
Agricultural Research (Annex 4)
2.12      Since it was established in 1962, OUAT has had principal responsi-
bility for agricultural research in the State. The Central Rice Research
Institute (CRRI) of the Indian Council of Agricultural Research (ICAR), at
Cuttack, carries out specialized research on rice. Some adaptive research is
also carried out by DAFP. CRRI, established in 1946, develops rice HYV for
improved yields under different agro-ecological conditions throughout India,
and with resistance/tolerance to major pests and diseases. It has a 121 ha
research farm and a professional staff of 120 scientists.
2.13      Research coordination is the responsibility of the Research Dean
of OUAT. He also administers seven research stations, recently transferred
from DAFP. The main station is 246 ha, on the Bhubaneswar campus. OUAT has
produced several rice HYV and also new varieties of millet,_ pulses, oilseeds
and wheat. It has also done work on economic evaluation of cropping systems,
pest and disease control, soil fertility and rainfed agriculture. Most
research is financed by ICAR and most research staff are temporary, depending
on ICAR budgets.
Farm Power and Implements (Annex 5)
2.14      Most Orissa farmers are wholly dependent for energy on human labor
and animal power. About 3.4 M cultivators, 1.9 M agricultural laborers and
an estimated 2.6 M animals work on the land. Nevertheless, there are serious
seasonal shortages of animal power, for early cultivation; and of labor, par-
ticularly for transplanting and weeding, despite pervasive landlessness,
unemployment and poverty. A major constraint to production is failure to
prepare and sow land in time. Before early rains have softened it, soil is
generally too hard to cultivate and then, at a time when bullocks are in poor
condition following the dry season, peak demand results in shortage of draft
power, and consequent late planting, inadequate land preparation and poor
cultivation. The local country plow is also very inefficient, requiring more
animal hours than would be required with improved plows. Mechanization is at
an early stage in Orissa, with only about 1,800 tractors and 450 power tillers
in use, and it will be slow to spread when there are so many small fragmented
farms. Animal power must therefore be better used to improve cultivation.
2.15      DAFP and CRRI have carried out valuable work on improved anitmal-
drawn implements, some of which, particularly the metal plow, are ready for
production and distribution on a commercial scale.



- 5 -
Ground Water Development (Annexes 6 & 7)
2.16        Ground water development in Orissa was negligible until about
ten years ago, though quite substantial progress has been made since the
Orissa Lift Irrigation Corporation (OLIC) was formed by GOO in 1973, princi-
pally to construct and operate lift irrigation with both surface and ground
water. There are currently an estimated 90,000 dug wells and 700 tubewells
supplying irrigation to 50,000 ha in both kharif and rabi.
2.17        Based on a plan prepared by the Agricultural Refinance and
Development Corporation (ARDC), in collaboration with OLIC, GOO proposes
development of ground water to include 200,000 dug wells, 15,000 simple pri-
vate tubewells (Annex 7), and 2,250 public tubewells, resulting in ground
water irrigation of an additional 295,000 ha at an estimated cost of Rs 1,180 M
(US$130 M). This would be financed by ARDC from an existing general line of
IDA credit (Credit 505-IN), a further credit to be negotiated, and from its
own funds.
Input Supply (Annex 8)
2.18        Orissa input use is low (fertilizer averaging 9 kg/ha compared
with all-India 14.8 kg/ha), partly because extension is not organized to
encourage it, partly because supply is not well developed. Agricultural co-
operatives handle an estimated 60% of the small amount of fertilizer dis-
tributed. Villages near a distribution center (about half) are served by
private retailers, but in most outlying areas, the cooperative is the only
source. Cooperatives and private retailers rarely have enough technical
knowledge and training, storage, transportation and working capital for
efficient input distribution. GOI fertilizer price controls, with fixed dis-
tribution margins, are also a severe disincentive to handling of fertilizer
beyond the busier centers. The fertilizer situation in India is currently
under discussion between GOI and the Bank.
2.19        Neither cooperatives nor private traders supply seeds, pesti-
cides or implements on any scale, and few farms have ready access to them.
DAFP is primarily responsible for production and distribution of improved
seeds, the processing facilities for which are rudimentary.
2.20        Fertilizer supply is not an immediate constraint, since Orissa
plants will have capacity well above local demand for some years. The National
Seeds Corporation has arranged to make required quantities of seeds available
until a planned State Seed Corporation is developed and operating. The
private input trade has adequate support, but the cooperative system is weak
due partly to low turnover, partly to inability of members to provide or raise
funds for investment. The National Cooperative Development Corporation (NCDC)
is GOI instrument for helping overcome this weakness, by offering loans, through
State Governments, on special terms, and grants up to 25% of the cost of storage
and, in tribal areas, of transportation. Regional marketing cooperatives chan-
nel fertilizer, farm chemicals and implements mainly through primary coopera-
tives, with the help of their formal and somewhat inflexible credit system.



- 6 -
Regional cooperatives also procure and process paddy and oilseeds and store
commodities and consumables. Distribution problems are compounded by high
credit overdues and consequently reduced turnover as members thus become
ineligible for credit to buy inputs. Rescheduling of some debts and a good
1975/76 harvest should have helped; but the real answer is strengthening of
cooperative membership and staff training and a firmer stand against overdues.
Land Records (Annex 9)
2.21        Many of Orissa's land-holders do not have up-to-date records.
Accurate village maps are rare. This lack of such records and maps is a major
obstacle to development. For instance cultivators, without the security of
tenure provided by a firm title, are reluctant to invest in their holdings.
Credit agencies requiring land as collateral are prevented from lerninig.
Work on land consolidation is delayed. Improvements in water use in irrigated
areas are hampered and land acquisition for development becomes doubly time
consuming. Conversion of share-croppers to full ownership and enforcement of
the land ceiling legislation are almost impossible. In addition, even where
land records do exist, the systems for retrieving information and updating it
is cumbersome and time consuming.
2.22        Two separate Divisions within the Department of Revenue and
Excise are responsible for land records: the Commissioner, Land Records and
Settlement, for land survey every 30 years to update basic records; and the
Inspector-General of Land Registration, for registration of transfers and
mortgages. A third Division takes care of land reform and consolidation.
Agricultural Credit (Annex 10)
2.23      Institutional credit in Orissa is estimated to cover only about 15%
of total need, compared with all-India estimates of about 35%. Most institu-
tional credit (85%) is provided by the cooperative movement; short and medium
term through the Orissa State Cooperative Bank, 17 Central Cooperative Banks
with 84 branches, and about 3,300 Primary Credit Societies; long (and some
medium) term through the State Land Development Bank, with eight branches and
55 affiliated Primary Land Development Banks. However, only about 40% of farm
households are members of cooperatives, and only about 10% borrow from them.
The 14 major commercial banks with 345 branches (half in rural areas) also
provide short, medium and long term loans under a "Lead Bank" System with
responsibility for commercial bank credit in particular districts.
2.24      Short, medium and long term cooperative credit is supported by redis-
count facilities with the Reserve Bank of India and ARDC. Both institutions
also support commercial banks, and both have control and monitoring mechanisms.
2.25        In spite of the wide coverage of the cooperative system, insti-
tutional credit performance has been mixed. A Study Team on Institutional
Credit Arrangements (August 1975) and the ARDC Banking Plan Team (January
1976) recommended remedial measures, notably: vigorous recovery of overdues;
redistribution of primary cooperatives, closing the weakest; deposit mobili-
zation; training; and reinforcement of supervisory staff. IDA credits ARDC
I and II are being used as vehicles for helping to implement such measures.



- 7-
Cooperative Training (Annex 11)
2.26        About 10,000 staff are employed in the Orissa cooperative move-
ment, which has grown rapidly in recent years. The three cooperative training
institutions are all housed in rented accommodation; one at Bhubaneswar for
senior staff; and one each at Gopalpur and Bargarh, where primary cooperative
secretaries, supervisors and inspectors are mainly trained. They are grossly
inadequate for demands now put upon them, let alone expected. Some Central
Cooperative Banks also provide training.
Basic Extension Training (Annex 12)
2.27        About 6,600 are employed at village level in the extension serv-
ices. Training is needed to replace normal wastage of about 5% (330) and to
upgrade poorly-trained staff who could benefit, numbering about 2,500. Up-
dating courses and basic training for contact farmers and rural leaders,
including rural women, are also needed. Basic extension training is provided
at three centers; Bhubaneswar, Gopalpur, Bolangir. Built in the early 1950's
for a much smaller function than now required, each with accommodation for
about 160 trainees, they have insufficient classrooms and overlarge classes;
and they lack practical emphasis. There is urgent need to expand and upgrade
them.
Training of Women (Annex 12)
2.28        Women play an important and increasing role in family decision
making and are becoming progressively more involved in agriculture e.g. in
seed selection, handling of fertilizers and keeping of small animals. Each
Development Block has a women's social education officer, who concentrates on
improved nutrition, health and family planning, child care, home management,
agriculture and animal husbandry. Three out of 13 Revenue Districts have
training teams holding 3-5 day training camps for women's clubs. Each team
comprises a training officer and two demonstrators, with a vehicle for teach-
ing aids and demonstration materials. They have proved extremely effective.
III. THE PROJECT
A. General Objectives and Description
3.01        The project is a package of complementary components aimed at
achieving early and sustained improvement in agricultural production, particu-
larly of foodgrains. This would be accomplished mainly by reorganizing and
strengthening the extension service, to bring improved agricultural practices
to farmers statewide; and by strengthening and reorienting related research.
At the same time, associated input supply would be improved. The base would
be laid for longer term improvements in ground water development and credit
supply through surveys, monitoring, shallow tubewell technology, and new and



-8-
improved land transfer and survey systems, which would also help land consol-
idation to improve farm efficiency. Agricultural statistics and data process-
ing would be strengthened to improve agricultural planning for GOO and GOI.
3.02        The project comprises:
(a) reorganization and strengthening of agricultural extension
and its link to agricultural research;
(b) strengthening and reorientation of agricultural research
by OUAT, including adaptive research and field trials;
(c) pre-service training for extension workers and training
for cooperative personnel;
(d) improved and accelerated land surveys and land record
systems, to establish land ownership and tenancy more
efficiently as a basis for farm credit;
(e) support for the design, testing, demonstration and
,distribution of improved animal-drawn farm implements;
(f) ground water survey and monitoring; research, design,
installation and field testing of alternative types of
shallow tubewell and pumping techniques; and
(g) support for farm management data collection and evalua-
tion by OUAT, and strengthening of agricultural statis-
tics and data processing by the Orissa Bureau of Economics
and Statistics;
B. Detailed Features
Agricultural Extension (Annex 3)
3.03        The extension service would be reorganized and strengthened,
closely following the successful pattern introduced in Rajasthan and else-
where. Based on regular two-weekly visits by full-time VLW on given days to
selected groups of farmers, each VLW receives training and instructions on one
day in the two-week period from his supervisor, an Agricultural Extension
Officer (AEO) supported by Subject Matter Specialists. He is given simple and
timely messages to communicate, finds out what his farmers' problems are and
seeks solutions.
3.04      Each VLW would cover some 400 to 800 active farm families, depending
on local population density and communications. They, in turn, are divided
into eight groups of 50 to 100 families. The messages, conveyed through 8-10



- 9 -
contact farmers in each of these smaller groups, focus on the most important
local crops and on practices that are of immediate relevance; and they relate
to farm size and farmer ability to invest and take risks. VLW also carry out
demonstrations and participate in field trials. Making no formal reports,
they keep a daily diary to record farm visits, major recommendations and
problems to be discussed with the AEO.
3.05      The extension service is now being reorganized with a single line of
command from the Director of Agriculture through to the VLW. This is being
effected by transferring some 2,500 VLW (about 70%) from the Ministry of
Community Development and Social Welfare to DAFP and incorporating another
3,100 from all "special schemes", which will be abolished. All would devote
100% of their time to agricultural extension.
3.06      VLW would thus cover the 3.4 M active farm families in Orissa at
an average ratio of 1:600. Seven hundred AEO would support and supervise
them (at a ratio of 1:8) and would be responsible to District Agricultural
Officers (DAO). DAO would each have two Additional District Agricultural
Officers who with him would each supervise 8-10 AEO. Five Subject Matter
Specialists and a Training Officer would operate in each of the thirteen
agricultural ranges and give support and training to field staff. They would
also actively participate in local field research and form a strong link with
OUAT. Assurances were given that appropriate staffing, acceptable to IDA,
would be implemented (Annex 16, Table 4).
3.07      The project would provide transportation, housing, office accommoda-
tion, demonstration and training equipment and fellowships for the extension
service, together with incremental establishment costs. Details are in Annexes
3 and 16 (Table 3). Eleven cars, 83 four-wheel drive vehicles, 13 pickups and
13 minibuses would be provided. Motorcycles and cycles would be so'ld to staff
on credit, which is the best means of ensuring proper care and maintenance.
Maximum loans allowed are presently inadequate; assurances were obtained that
credit would be made available to extension staff for purchase of suitable
means of transport, on terms and conditions that would be sufficient to create
incentive for purchase of such transport. On purchasing a motorcycle, staff
would attend a short course on motorcycle handling, maintenance, simple
repairs and highway code and insurance laws.
3.08      Housing would be provided for the newly appointed Deputy Directors,
Assistant District Agricultural Officers and Subject Matter Specialists and
for about 35% of the VLW and AEO whose housing is now inadequate (Annex 16,
Table 2). Assurances were obtained that adequate low cost housing would be
provided to enable VLW to live in the area assigned for their operation.
Assurances were also given that (i) within two months from date of signing,
GOO would furnish IDA for its review the design and cost estimates of such
housing, and (ii) within six months of signing, GOO would complete a survey
to determine the exact number and locations of such housing.



- 10 -
Agricultural Research (Annex 4)
3.09      A production-oriented applied and adaptive research program in those
crops of importance to Orissa would be set up. It would concentrate on eval-
uation of improved cultural practices; farming systems and cropping patterns;
and testing of improved and high-yielding varieties resistant/tolerant to the
major pests and diseases in the State. This would be carried out by OUAT and
DAFP in addition to their existing research work.
3.10      The four main agro-ecological zones would be covered by applied
research stations under OUAT at: Bhubaneswar (Coastal), Chiplima (Central
Plateau Tableland), Simliguda (Eastern Ghat-Highland), and Keonjhar (Northern
Plateau). In addition, adaptive research would be conducted at a government
farm in each of the 13 agricultural ranges, with OUAT providing researchers on
secondment to DAFP which would provide the support staff. Assurances were
given that these stations and farms would be included in the project and that
a Central Research Coordination Unit would be established by OUAT in Bhuban-
eswar (see Annex 4).
3.11      At full development by year 3, the four applied research stations
would have 41 professionals and 165 support staff. The adaptive research
farms would have 13 Research Officers and 26 Research Assistants seconded from
OUAT to the DAFP ranges. Assurances were given that OUAT professional and
support staff for these stations would be permanent staff members devoting
full time to research.
3.12      Equipment and facilities at the research stations are generally poor
and they would be strengthened. On-farm development comprising irrigation,
drainage, land levelling, roads, fencing, etc, would be provided at all four
applied research stations and some of the adaptive research farms. Buildings,
staff quarters and a 24-bed dormitory would also be provided at each of the
three outlying stations. The dormitory would accommodate extension personnel
and farmers for training and mutual exchange of information and problems.
Input Storage and Distribution (Annex 8)
3.13      To achieve full project potential, input supply should be strength-
ened so that seed, fertilizer and plant protection chemicals are available on
time. Fertilizer application is forecast to increase, largely as a result of
the project, from a 7.4 kg/ha (nutrient) mean for 1973/74-1975/76 to 14.6 by
1979/80 and 24.6 by 1983/84. The 1974/75 all-India average was 14.8 kg/ha.
Total volume is expected to increase approximately as follows:
1975/76 1976/77 1977/78 1978/79 1979/80 1980/81 1981/82 1982/83 1983/84
Project Year    (1)       (2)     (3)      (4)     (5)
------------------------------Thousand tons ---------------------------
155    185      225     275      335     385      440      515    590



- 11 -
3.14      Seed volume is expected to increase to 20,000 tons by 1979/80 and
30/40,000 tons by 1983/84; and total turnover of low volume plant protection
chemicals to Rs 60 M by 1979/80 and Rs 120 M by 1983/84.
3.15      Increased input would be handled about equally by the private trade
and cooperatives. The private trade would be able to increase its distribu-
tion capacity adequately without project assistance. The cooperative system,
however, needs special help which NCDC offers.
3.16      At appraisal GOI and GOO proposed that IDA finance the cooperative
storage and distribution component of this project using NCDC as the financing
agency. Since NCDC would be a new channel for IDA funds, a special mission
visited India in October-November 1976 to review its procedures and policies.
This mission found that, subject to a number of legal, organizational and
policy changes, NCDC would be a suitable channel. However, because it may
take an extended period to introduce these changes, it was agreed with GOI
and GOO to delete this cooperative component from the present project and to
transfer it to a subsequent all - India project to be prepared by NCDC. Any
delay that occurs in the provision of additional storage as a result of this
transfer should not affect the implementation of the extension and research
components of the present project.
Cooperative Training (Annex 11)
3.17      For cooperative training, a 50 double-room hostel and 4 lecture
rooms (2 X 40 and 2 X 20) would be built at the Bhubaneswar College and,
in addition to similar lecture rooms, a hostel to accommodate 100 and housing
for five staff at the Bargarh Center. The Gopalpur Center would be renovated
and a hostel to accommodate 30 and two classrooms (for 40) added. All three
would be provided with ancillary buildings and services, furniture, kitchen
equipment (except Gopalpur), teaching equipment and visual aids; and a
40-seater bus, particularly to take students for on-farm and other training
visits.
3.18      Training provided by the 17 Central Cooperative Banks would be
strengthened by the addition of simple hostel accommodation for about 20
students at each one, with a classroom, ancillary construction, furniture
and equipment. A full-time instructor would be seconded from the Department
of Cooperatives to each. Disbursement requests would certify that the Coop-
erative Bank, in respect of which the request was made, had submitted to the
Registrar satisfactory evidence of title to land for the training structures
and had a Department of Cooperatives instructor appointed full-time.
3.19      Annual capacity would thus be increased to train about 10% of the
more senior cooperative staff. Central Cooperative Bank training capacity,
gradually phased over three years, would increase sufficiently to enable
replacement of employee departures from the cooperative movement. It would
also provide a brief update for all cooperative secretaries, junior accountants,
storekeepers, etc.



- 12 -
Basic Extension Training (Annex 12)
3.20      The three basic extension training centers would be transferred to
the Ministry of Agriculture and Cooperatives from the Ministry of Community
Development and Social Welfare. They would be expanded and upgraded and a
new one added in the north at Sambalpur on land to be made available by GOO.
Hostel accommodation would be provided at the new center and expanded at
Bhubaneswar. Additional classrooms and other facilities would provide full
teaching and hostel accomodation for the 160 students that can be accommodated
at each of the existing centers and 200 in the new one. Mobile training units
would be provided for rural women in the ten Ranges which do not now have
them.
3.21      The basic training period would be reduced from two years to one,
but intensified under a revised curriculum (Annex 12) supplemented by practical
husbandry. Annual training capacity would thus be increased from a possible
4,880 to 6,160 more effective man-months, enough to enable 80 trainees to
receive annual pre-service training, 40 an eight-month upgrading and 120 a
four-month upgrading at each center.
3.22      Scholarships would be provided for 80 man-months of training out-
side Orissa for Subject Matter Specialists and 20 man-months for lecturers.
Training Establishment Costs
3.23      Incremental costs for the above training over the five-year project
period would be included for lecturers' and subordinate staff remuneration,
travelling and trainee allowances, building and vehicle maintenAnce and ope-
ration, teaching materials and office expenses. The services of a Training
Officer (who would also be provided with a vehicle) would also be included.
Land Survey and Land Records (Annex 9)
Land Survey
3.24      For reasons set out in paragraph 2.21, work on the cadastral survey
of the state and the preparation of a complete Record of Rights would be
speeded up. It is at present only constrained by lack of funds and is not
due for completion until 1984. Under the project, the schedule would be
brought forward to be completed by 1980/81. An additional 362 survey teams
would be provided with six more survey supervisors and 156 assistant survey
(settlement) officers. A further 1,500 trained technical field staff and 4,000
unskilled laborers would be recruited (and are available). An additional six
4-wheel drive vehicles, survey equipment and tents would also be provided.
Land Record Systems
3.25      For similar reasons it is important that searching for land records
to confirm ownership title be made less cumbersome and time consuming. In
Dhenkenal District, an improved system of card indexing has been successfully
introduced, whereby search and issue time has been reduced from six days to



- 13 -
one hour. This system would be extended statewide under the project, by pro-
vision of card index cabinets (646); data forfs and cards; additional tem-
porary staff; and buildings for secure storage and ongoing registration and
certification work, with 14 record rooms at important local centers, 47 rural
record rooms at key towns, and two new registration offices, one each at
Sambalpur and Bolangir.
Farm Implements (Annex 5)
3.26      To help farmers improve cultural practices, animal drawn farm imple-
ments would be developed through: expansion of DAFP production; extension and
intensification of DAFP demonstration; establishment of a research and design
unit at OUAT College of Agriculture and Engineering Technology.
3.27      To increase annual production of improved plows from 5,500 to
14,000, the project would provide a power press, additional workshop equipment
and a 4-wheel drive vehicle with trailer. Staff (one development engineer and
two assistant engineers) and operating costs, including tests on DAFP adaptive
research farms would also be included. Thirteen mobile demonstration vans
with audio-visual equipment would be purchased, one unit per agricultural
range (13), with incremental staff and operating costs. Each would be operated
by an agricultural engineering supervisor (also Range Farm Machinery Snbject
Matter Specialist), and cover about 150 demonstrations, or 30,000 farmers, a
year. For OUAT research and design unit, additional workshop equipment
and one 4-wheel drive vehicle would be provided, together with two research
engineers and two assistants under a chief research engineer and operating
costs over five years. Visits by mobile units would be at request of VLW.
3.28      Demand for improved plows under the project is expected to increase
to about 50,000 a year and, over and above DAFP production, would initially
be manufactured by the industrial Development Corporation, Hirakaud (IDC) to
hANFP specifications. Private entrepreneurs would also be encouraged to take
up manufacture on contract to the Agro-Industries Corporation (AIC), which
would coordinate manufacturing and distribution with market needs. Assurances
were given that a Farm Implements Committee comprising DAFP, OUAT and AIC
representatives and chaired by the Joint Director of Agriculture (Engineering)
would be established to supervise design to distribution of such implements.
Ground Water Surveys and Monitoring (Annex 6)
3.29      Ground water development on the scale referred to in para 2.17 (an
additional 295,000 ha) would increase annual ground water extraction by an
estimated 240,000 ha m from approximately 100,000 ha m at present. To assure
orderly development and avoid local over-exploitation, the project would
include a three-year program of extensive geo-hydrologic investigation and
formulation of systematic monitoring and data collection.



- 14 -
3.30      Two new divisions for monitoring at Balangir and Bhubaneswar would
be needed, each headed by a hydrologist, and six sub-divisions, each with
an assistant hydrologist. A total of 160 additional staff would be required.
Instrumentation for measuring rainfall and runoff data would be installed at
92 locations. Seasonal and annual fluctuation of the water table would be
determined from 65 specially designed monitoring wells. An inventory of
existing wells would be made, and some of these would be incorporated in the
monitoring program. A comprehensive ground water quality monitoring program
would also be included. A central data bank would be established at each of
four regional headquarters. Assurances were given that a separate unit would
be established promptly to conduct groundwater surveys and monitoring with
such terms of reference and composition as may be mutually agreed between
GOO and the Association.
3.31      The major equipment required would be two drilling rigs, one heavy
duty rotary and the other percussion type, with supporting transport. Also
included would be casing, welding equipment and other materials to install
30 tubewells in the coastal plain and some 35 elsewhere. Other items would
be electrical logging equipment, metering and recording devices, laboratory
testing apparatus, surveying instruments, field camp facilities, and office
furniture and equipment.
Shallow Tubewell Technology (Annex 7)
3.32      Scientifically designed hand-dug wells were introduced to Orissa
fairly recently and proved very popular, because initial capital cost is
relatively low and benefit great and immediate. They cost more per ha and per
ha m, however, than most alternatives. Where geological conditions permit,
shallow filter point wells offer an even lower capital cost, with similar or
superior performance. A variation of the conventional tubewell, they may be
hand-drilled to a relatively shallow depth of up to 25 m. They use locally
available materials instead of conventional steel tubes and screens and
comprise a coir filter, made up of jute wrapping round a bamboo frame, and
a fabricated bamboo pipe instead of steel tube. A few have been successfully
installed in Orissa, and in Bihar similar bamboo tubewells have demonstrated
a five year life when kept wet.
3.33      Assurances were obtained that OLIC would implement a three-year
program of research, design, installation and field testing of alternative
types together with low cost alternative systems of irrigation water extrac-
tion and distribution.
3.34      To conduct this program, OLIC would need two additional engineers
and 14 technical and general office personnel. Tubewell and pump components,
transport, hand-boring sets, miscellaneous tools and testing instruments,
would be required, together with materials for prototype manual pumps.
Farm Management Data Unit (Annex 13)
3.35      The project would support farm management data collection and
evaluation by expanding OUAT Farm Management Data Unit (para 2.03) to provide
statistical information for monitoring agricultural performance together with
feedback on costs of production and returns from different farm systems.



- 15 -
Main objectives would be to provide GOO with data both for agricultural
policy and planning, and for use in extension service training as well as for
monitoring and evaluating the extension service and new research technology.
Data would comprise: production cost studies on major crops; cost-benefit
studies of new techniques developed from adaptive research; revision of the
Orissa State Farm Management Handbook; bench mark information for geographical
zones and social groups. A continuing sample of about 1,500 farms would be
selected across the main agroclimatic zones.
3.36      The Unit would be under a Director and staffed by six Farm Manage-
ment Economists, 30 Farm Management Assistants (one to each Agricultural
District), 105 Primary Investigators and supporting analyst staff. The eco-
nomists would be based at the four zonal basic research stations (Annex 4).
Suitable assurances were given.
Agricultural Statistics and Data Processing (Annex 14)
3.37      The Bureau of Statistics and Economics (BSE) would be strength-
ened to undertake the Timely Reporting System (TRS) being adopted India-
wide. BSE Agricultural Statistics and Crop Survey Division would be headed
by a Deputy Director, with a total of 24 staff in Bhubaneswar and a field
organization under a District Statistical Officer in each of the 13 Districts.
3.38      State-wide estimates of areas and average yields of rice and 11
other important crops would be based on annual samples rotated every year from
1976. Gathering of land use data would begin with about 3,100 villages (6%) in
kharif, gradually increasing to 10,400 (20%) over four or five years. There-
after, a complete land-use picture would be available every five years. 460
villages, in intensively irrigated blocks, would be selected for estimating
land use during rabi. TRS would provide: area and yield statistics; irriga-
tion statistics (source, area, frequency, crops); price statistics (farmgate
through retail); supplementary data on soils, labor use, land tenure, input
use, etc.                                                   X
3.39      A large increment of staff would be required (Annex 26), mainly
primary and supervisory workers, since VLW would no longer be available. Suf-
ficient qualified persons are available, and recruitment is expected to be no
problem. Training in statistical methodology and practical field work would,
however, be given by a training team comprising an Assistant Director, three
land registration officers, three instructors and two research assistants.
Suitable assurances were given. The project would provide vehicles; loans for
motorcycles and bicycles; measuring and weighing equipment and crop cutting
devices; office calculating machines, equipment and furniture (Annex 26
Table 2).

4.02      Physical contingencies, averaging 4% on total basic project cost,
have been applied (Annexes 16-27) to civil works, furniture and miscellaneous
equipment and selected incremental operating costs but are not needed for
specific equipment items, vehicles and incremental staff costs. Price
contingencies, totalling 26% on basic cost plus physical contingencies, have
been applied cumulatively as follows:
- Equipment, vehicles and incremental operating costs,
except incremental salaries, 2% for the remainder of
1976; 8% per annum in 1977/79 and 7% thereafter;
- Civil works, 2% for the remainder of 1976, 12% per
annum in 1977/79, and 10% thereafter;
- Incremental salaries, 6% per annum from 1977 onwards.
B. Proposed Financing
4.03      The proposed IDA credit of US$20 M, on standard terms to GOI, would
finance about 50% of total project cost, (52% net of taxes and duties), and
would cover the equivalent of the estimated US$1.6 M foreign exchange cost and
about 48% of local cost. The remaining 50% of project costs would be met by
GOO. IDA funds would be made available to GOO in accordance with GOI standard



- 17 -
terms and arrangements for the financing of State development projects. GOO
would channel funds to implementing units (DAFP etc) as annual budget alloca-
tions.
C. Procurement
4.04      Main items to be financed are:  civil works; vehicles, including
motorcycles and cycles; research, drilling, survey and teaching equipment;
and incremental salaries and allowances. Contracts for civil works would be
awarded on the basis of competitive bidding, following local advertising, under
established procedures. Small and disbursed geographically and over time,
these contracts (US$13.1 M total) would not be suitable for international
competitive bidding. There are numerous qualified local contractors able to
carry out civil works. A Buildings Implementation Unit would be established
to assure efficient implementation of the buildings component (para 5.02).
Construction of VLW housing would be supervised by civil engineers attached to
the Block level administration. Suitable assurances were obtained.
4.05      About 130 motorized vehicles of various types including trucks,
4-wheel drive vehicles, and automobiles (US$0.8 M) would be required by
Government Departments. They would be widely dispersed in rural areas, and
adequate maintenance and spares availability would be of paramount importance.
This is only possible for locally made vehicles of types already widely used
by GOO, and well-known to mechanics, even in remote villages. Procurement
would be by local competitive bidding under existing procedures, which are
satisfactory, except that procurement from GOI of some vehicles and equipment
(such as calculators and filing cabinets) commonly used by all Government
Departments is also possible, where appropriate, through GOI Director-General
of Supplies, who regularly makes "rate contracts" for supply of these items by
competitive tender. Motorcycles and bicycles (US$0.9 M) would be purchased by
individual staff, according to their preferences.
4.06      Scientific equipment for research (US$0.3 M) would involve small
contracts, from different suppliers of specialized items. International com-
petitive bidding would, therefore, not be practical. Most of the world's
leading manufacturers of scientific equipment are represented in India, are
competitive, and could offer local maintenance service. GOO established local
procurement procedures utilizing competitive bidding where appropriate would
be utilized. Other minor equipment and furniture procurement (US$3.6 M) would
follow similar procedures, except where valued at less than US$50,000, when
prudent shopping through normal trade channels would be permitted. Suitable
assurances were given.
4.07      The balance of project costs (US$21.3 M) would consist of local and
overseas training, and incremental salaries and operating costs, not involving
procurement.



- 18 -
4.08      Assurances were obtained that contracts for vehicles and equip-
ment would be bulked wherever feasible and that for contracts in excess of
US$100,000, draft tender invitations and bid documents would be submitted
for IDA review before issue.
D. Disbursements
4.09      Disbursements under the Credit would extend over five years for:
(a) 100% of foreign expenditures for equipment
and vehicles;
(b) 100% of cost of training and fellowships;
(c) 80% of cost for locally procured equipment and vehicles;
(d) 80% of civil works and;
(e) 25% of salaries of incremental staff.
4.10      There would be no disbursement against land aquisition or operating
costs. Disbursements against expenditures for locally procured equipment
costing Rs 20,000 or less (item c), payments under civil works contracts for
one or more progress payment not exceeding Rs 50,000 (item d), and salaries
(item e) would be against a certificate of expenditure, the documentation for
which would not be submitted for review but would be retained by GOO. It
would be available for inspection by IDA during the course of Project Super-
vision missions. Disbursements against all other items would be fully docu-
mented. Certificates of expenditure would be audited at least once every six
months and a report submitted to IDA promptly thereafter. Suitable assurances
were obtained. A schedule of estimated disbursements is in Annex 29.
V. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
5.01      Organization and management of the project is complex, in the sense
that it covers a wide range of components (para 3.02). On the other hand, its
main thrust is through existing institutions which would not, except for the
Extension Service, be greatly changed.
5.02      Chart 16979 shows the relationship between project implementing
units. The officer principally responsible, in overall charge of the project,
would be the Agricultural Production Commissioner. He would exercise coordi-
nation and overall project control through the Head of a newly created Policy
Planning and Coordination Unit, an already appointed Officer on Special
Duties. The establishment of this unit is a condition of effectiveness.



- 19 -
The Unit would assist GOO with agricultural policy planning and coordination
sector wide, not only for the project, and would have three cells: Project
Preparation (to identify and prepare projects); Monitoring and Evaluation (to
coordinate activities and oversee implementation); Policy and Planning (to
prepare agricultural development programs and strategies and advise on policy).
Each cell would have a small staff transferred from similar duties with the
Commissioner and DAFP. Terms of reference of the Unit have been agreed (Annex
30). In order to assure efficient implementation of the buildings component
of the project and timely disbursement of the credit, the Public Works
Department, or such other department as the Association may agree, would
establish promptly a special Buildings Implementation Unit for the duration of
the design and construction stage. A qualified and experienced director of
the Unit would be appointed. Suitable assurances were obtained.
5.03      The Department of Agriculture and Cooperation would directly imple-
ment most components, particularly through DAFP and the Registrar of Coopera-
tives. Other institutions would be: OUAT; the Board of Revenue (land records
and survey); OLIC (ground water monitoring and tubewell technology); and the
Bureau of Statistics and Economics.
5.04      A high-level Adaptive Research and Extension Advising Panel, has
been recently formed. This Panel will evaluate seasonal research results;
define a basic program for additional trials; formulate seasonal extension
programs; propose annual field demonstration programs for practices already
proven; assess requirements for further coordination of research and extension
and related needs (eg, input and credit supply). It comprises: Agricultural
Production Commissioner (Chairman); Secretary, Agriculture and Cooperation;
Commissioner, Command Area Development; Director, DAFP; Director, CRRI; Vice-
Chancellor, OUAT; Head of Project Planning and Coordination Unit; Additional
Director (Extension) (Member Secretary). District Agricultural Officers,
Subject Matters Specialists, and cooperative staff are co-opted as necessary.
The Panel meets twice yearly, at least two months before each season. The
Secretary prepares agenda from data supplied by DAFP and OUAT.
5.05      Changes in the extension organization are described in Annex 3
and paras 3.03-3.06. During negotiations GOO confirmed that extension staff
had been transferred to DAFP, and a competent and experienced person appointed
full-time to head the extension service.
Project Monitoring and Evaluation
5.06      The Monitoring and Evaluation Cell (para 5.02) would be primarily
responsible for checking on project progress and effect. It would be headed
by an economist, supported by an agriculturist and a systems analyst. With
regard to Extension and Research, the Cell would use information flowing from
BSE (Annex 14 and paras 3.37-3.39) and from the Farm Management Data Unit in
OUAT (Annex 13 and paras 3.35-3.36). BSE would also provide the Cell with an
estimate of the uptake of practices introduced by the extension service. The



- 20 -
Farm Mlanagement Data Unit, through its fully costed farm samples, would provide
a measure of extension and research success, in terms of farm profitability.
Data from both these sources would also be analyzed and provided to the Panel
referred to in para 5.04, and thence to the Departments concerned.
Accounts and Auditing
5.07      Assurances were given that:  all institutions using project funds
would keep separate accounts for the project and these would be audited in
accordance with appropriate auditing principles acceptable to IDA; accounts
would be prepared annually for audit and these would be submitted to IDA
not later than six months after the end of each fiscal year; quarterly summary
draft accounts of the project would be submitted to IDA promptly; as would
quarterly project progress reports in a form acceptable to IDA.
VI. PRODUCTION, MARKETING AND FARMER BENEFITS
Yields
6.01      Yields of rainfed and irrigated crops are projected to increase:
firstly, by widespread adoption of improved cultural practices and second,
through improved seeds and increased use of fertilizer and plant protection
chemicals. Yields of the major crop - rice - at increasing rates of adoption
of practices (Annex 2), are projected as follows:
A           B               C
Improved      A plus          A Plus
Present    Cultural       Fertilizer      HYV plus more
Yields     Practices      20 kg N/ha       fertilizer
and plant protection
----------  Rice Yield in Tons per Hectare  /a -----------
Rainfed
Kharif          0.8       1.0              1.3              1.5 - 2.0
Irrigated
Kharif          1.0       1.3              1.7              2.0 - 2.3
Rabi            1.3        1.5             2.1              2.6 - 3.3

- 21 -
6.02      These yields are well within field results already achieved in
Orissa. Based on projected uptake rates (Annex 2, Table 2) and expected
yield under each practice (Annex 31, Table 2), average yields for all food-
grains in tons/ha are projected to increase from 0.8 at present to 0.9 by
1980 (year 4) and 1.1 by 1984 (year 8).
Marketable Surplus
6.03      As a result of increases in production, from the project and
otherwise, the net surplus of foodgrain, pulses and oilseeds for export to
other states is expected to rise substantially. Precise forecasts, depending
on many variables, are difficult; but it would be disappointing if the present
200,000 tons surplus were not multiplied several times. Assuming about 30% of
lowland and 15% of upland farmers adopt the simple package of practices (A),
and another 30% of lowland and 10% of upland add, in addition to (A) a small
amount of fertilizer (B), and less than 10% of lowland and 5% of upland adopt
the full package (C), foodgrain production could exceed 7 M tons by Year 8
and marketable surpluses could exceed 1 M tons.
Storage and Processing
6.04      Storage, processing and distribution facilities were stretched to
the limit in 1975/76, when a total of 200,000 tons of rice were estimated to
be procured. Total estimated storage available, at 300,000 tons, is suf-
ficient to handle the export surplus. The total marketed surplus, allowing
for both public and private trade within Orissa, is higher and could be as
much as 1.2 M tons by 1980.
6.05      While India is short of foodgrains, pulses and oilseeds and there
is thus a large nationwide market for production from the project, there
are considerable organizational and distributional constraints and GOO will
require much greater support from the Food Corporation of India (FCI) than it
has received so far. Once the results of the all-India foodgrain storage
study are available, 1/ GOI, GOO and FCI are expected to tackle the planning
and creation of institutional procurement facilities to handle the additional
production.
Prices
6.06      Between 1974 and 1976 the annual average wholesale market price
for rice has fallen from Rs 1,500 - Rs 2,100 per ton (US$170-250) to Rs 800
(US$89) per ton, or about the procurement level paid by GOO. This fall
reflects the unusual increase in production due to very favorable weather
conditions. The economic price derived from the Bank's projection for the
1980 world market, in 1976 constant prices, with appropriate adjustments
for transportation and grade, is Rs 1,490 per ton and has been used in eval-
uating project benefits (Annex 34). In financial projections of farmers'
benefits, a price of Rs 1,000 per ton has been used.
1/   Financed under IDA Credit 267-IN and being evaluated.



- 22 -
Farmer Benefits
6.07      On the typical 0.75 ha rainfed farm, which at present has a net
farm income 1/ of about Rs 515 (US$57) and no cash surplus after meeting sub-
sistence needs, net farm income would be expected to rise to about Rs 1,290
(US$145) at full development (between Years 6 and 10). Many small farmers,
averaging about 0.75 ha rainfed, now produce less than total family food
needs and have to rely on off-farm employment to maintain a livelihood.
Adoption of improved technology, particularly fertilizer and HYV seed,
would be slower on these farms due to the higher risk involved under
purely rainfed conditions. Where extension is associated with irrigation,
e.g. from a dugwell, production would rise above family needs and leave a
cash surplus of about Rs 800 a year. In purely rainfed conditions, contri-
bution to subsistence needs would rise from about 50% to 80%. The project
would provide additional on-farm employment to about 400,000 people by year
8 and create new jobs in ancillary industries.
VII. BENEFITS AND ECONOMIC JUSTIFICATION
7.01      Project costs of the main production oriented components of exten-
sion and research and two related components of extension training and farm
implement demonstration, amount to about US$18 M or US$5 per benefitting farm
family. The project would reach a minimum of 0.7 M out of a potential 3.4 M
farm families who cultivate a total of some 6.8 M ha statewide. Almost all
farmers, except in the more remote areas, would benefit from direct or ['idirect
contact with extension workers, who would convey practical and profit-oriented
advice for crop production, based primarily on rice.
Economic Rate of Return
7.02      The design of the project, its timing, scope, relative size of
project components and organizational set-up are based on past experience
with similar successful agricultural extension and research reorganization
in India. Budgetary and organizational constraints have been taken into
account. No attempt has been made to estimate an economic rate of return
for the project in the usual way because of difficulties in forecasting with
any accuracy, the incremental benefits. In the first place, it would be
virtually impossible to differentiate that part of the total benefit which
would be the result of irrigation development and other farming inputs (r.     t-
lementary but not financed under this project) from the benefits from exten-
sion and research (financed under this project). In the second place, there
is not enough experience in Orissa to estimate acceptance rates with any
degree of accuracy. However, partly because of large sunk costs, whereby
the efficiency of an existing service is radically improved, the economic
rate of return of the project is likely to be well in excess of the estimated
opportunity cost of capital in India (thought to be about 12%).
1/   Net Farm Income is defined as value of total production at farmgate
prices less inputs, hired labor, bullock and other costs, but exclud-
ing family labor.



- 23 -
7.03      Instead, an estimate has been made of the increase in paddy pro-
duction required from participating farmers to achieve an economic rate of
return equal to the opportunity cost of capital (Annex 34). This comes to 8
kg/ha state-wide from year 7 onwards, which is low and well below what is
likely to be achieved. However, not all farmers would benefit equally from
the project, and if only 20% of farmers participated, yields would have to
increase by only 40 kg/ha in order to have an economic rate of return equal to
the opportunity cost of capital. The potential is about 2,000 kg/ha under
rainfed conditions and 3,300 kg/ha under irrigation. Average annual statewide
yields, with the project, are expected to rise by 100 kg/ha by Year 4 and a
further 200 kg/ha by Year 8. In the IDA financed Chambal and Rajasthan
Command and Development Projects (Reports 430-IN and 447-IN) rice, wheat and
mustard yields increased by over 50% as a result of improved extension of the
type this project would provide. These yield increases were achieved within
two years of the introduction of the new extension system. This project is
expected to provide similar rapid increases in yields.
Project Risks
7.04      Project risks are exceptionally difficult to evaluate, being depen-
dent on so many factors. The most important factor for success is wholehearted
acceptance by GOO and by farmers. Experience elsewhere in India is encouraging.
The system is designed to be thorough, dependable and reach all manner of
farmers without discrimination. However, expected benefits are so great that,
barring a natural disaster or acute mismanagement, the return on the relatively
small investment would remain satisfactory.
Employment Aspects
7.05      Annual incremental on-farm employment due to the project would rise
to about 55 M mandays by Year 4 and 100 M mandays by Year 8, reflecting addi-
tional employment generated by improved practices. By Year 8, through better
utilization of existing labor plus incremental job opportunities, total addi-
tional on-farm employment would be equivalent to about 400,000 man-years. New
jobs would also be created in implement manufacture and distribution; supply
of inputs; handling of an increased marketable surpluses; and in farm manage-
ment data collection and processing. Increased agricultural activity would
also have a multiplier effect, generating further employment.
Project Role Relative to National and Other Projects' Objectives
7.06      The project is of considerable importance to the success of other
agricultural projects in Orissa, particularly the planned ARDC ground water
development program, composite CAD project (medium and minor surface flow
irrigation) and major CAD.
7.07      The increase in foodgrain, pulse and oilseed production would raise
the level of nutrition in Orissa and provide an increased surplus for the food-
grain deficit areas in the rest of India.



- 24 -
7.08      In the past, agricultural development strategy has been aimed at
achieving production increases mainly through investment in irrigation and
through the use of "optimal" packages of practices, involving HYV with sub-
stantial doses of fertiliser and pesticides. These improvements are fre-
quently financed by credit, often not available to small and tenant farmers
with recommendations that are unsuited to the majority of farmers. This
project provides an opportunity for small and marginal farmers to enjoy the
benefits of increased productivity. Apart from generating high economic
benefits at low cost, the project would, therefore, also have considerable
impact on income distribution.

ANNEX 1
