INDIA
ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agriculture in Orissa
A. Background
General
1.        Agriculture is dominant in Orissa's economy contributing about
51% of total domestic product. About 80% of the nearly 26 M population
depends directly or indirectly on agriculture and about 75% of the work
force is engaged in it. Of a total area of about 15.5 M ha, 6.8 M ha are
cultivated. Of this, 16% is irrigable, but less than 4% of the area receives
water in the dry (rabi) season.
2.        Paddy is the most important crop occupying 70% of all cropped area.
Over 95% is grown in the kharif season, June to December, when moisture is
available from the SW monsoon. In the coastal areas, paddy is virtually the
only crop grown in kharif; in upland areas the cropping pattern is slightly
more diverse, small grains, pulses and oilseeds being grown in addition to
paddy. About 20% of the area is double cropped, most of the second crop
coming after kharif paddy on residual moisture. Statewide, in both the kharif
and rabi seasons, other cereals (wheat, maize, millets and barley) take up
another 7% and pulses 14% of the cropped area. This leaves about 9% of the
cropped area for oilseeds (6%), fibers (1%), vegetables and spices. Rice
yields in Orissa, as in other states of Eastern India, are low in relation
to the rest of India and similar areas in Asia. Peak achievement has never
exceeded about 1.0 ton of rice or 1.5 tons of paddy per hectare (Table 1).
Yields are about twice this in Punjab, Haryana and Tamil Nadu.   In neighbor-
ing West Bengal, with similar climatic patterns, yields are 30% higher, though
a higher proportion of the crop is irrigated, about 40% as compared to close
to 15% in Orissa.
3.        Agricultural production in Orissa has stagnated in the past decade,
despite increased area under cultivation (about 350,000 ha) increased irri-
gated area (close to 500,000 ha), and introduction of HYV rice and wheat.
With a quickly growing population, per capita production has actually de-
clined. There is thus both need and potential for increasing production
through intensified action.
The Rural Setting and Economy
4.        Orissa has substantial natural resources, including industrial raw
materials. In particular there are large deposits of coal, iron, limestone,



ANNEX I
Page 2
manganese and chromite and many other valuable minerals, including dolomite,
bauxite, graphite, nickel ore and quartzite. This wealth is still largely
untapped and the State will probably continue to be heavily dependent on
agriculture for many more years. Agriculture and allied activities account
for about 60% of State income at current prices, while the corresponding
figure for all India is less than 50%. Agriculture accounts for about 57%
of income in Orissa compared with 30% in West Bengal, 46% in Bihar and 47%
in Assam.
5.        The pressures of population and poverty in rural Orissa are consi-
derable. Among Indian States, Orissa not only has the highest percentage
living in rural areas (over 90%) but is also probably the poorest state (with
64% of the population below the poverty line). The population is also strati-
fied along caste, class and tribal lines, and is caught in a web of social-
economic relationships and land tenure systems which further hamper agricul-
ture development and modernization. The scheduled tribes and castes (23% and
15% of the population respectively) are economically the weakest sections of
the community (Table 2). Overlapping with the scheduled tribes and castes, but
recognized separately for administrative purposes, are the "small" and "mar-
ginal" farmers, with holdings of less than 2 ha, who represent roughly 2.6 M
out of a total of 3.4 M cultivators' households (Table 3). Although these
farmers operate over 76% of all operational holdings, they control less than
40% of total cultivable land, while farms of 5 ha or more (representing less
than 7% of holdings) account for about 30% of the land. Adding to the skewed
distribution of income in the rural areas are the landless and agricultural
laborers, who constitute an additional 1.8 M households. Complicating the
problem of size of holding is fragmentation, each holding being comprised,
on average, of 3 plots.
6.        Agricultural production can be considerably influenced by ownership
and tenancy patterns. The bulk of agricultural land in Orissa is owned by
its operators; about 5.9 M ha, or 92% of the total area is recorded as fully
owned and operated, the remaining 8% rented from others. From the statistics,
the problem of share croppers does not appear really severe with 66% of the
rented area (330,000 ha) recorded as under share cropping. However, in
practice, a much larger but unquantified area is believed to be under
share-cropping, although under recent land reform legislation the position
is likely to improve.
7.        The mass of farmers in Orissa are almost wholly dependent on human
labor and draft animals for power to cultivate their land and to perform all
other farm operations. Although there are 1.9 M agricultural laborers,
in addition to 3.4 M cultivators, there is a sharp seasonal shortage of labor
at critical peak periods such as when transplanting and weeding rice. Simi-
larly with animal draft, there are some 2.6 M working animals in Orissa; yet
under rainfed conditions, timely cultivation is only possible on part of the
land.
8.        A serious obstacle to an immediate surge in agricultural production
is the quality of what may be called the "delivery system", which explains



ANNEX I
Page 3
perhaps some of the paradoxes evident in this sector. Orissa carries out
important rice research which has produced a large number of improved varie-
ties and technology; yet few farmers use them. Orissa is a major producer of
nitrogenous fertilizers, yet uses only about 50,000 tons on its own land.
The Goverm-nent stresses the importance of credit as a catalyst for development,
yet the majority of the farmers depend on the merchant and money lender or use
no credit because it is hard to get. The extension service is not short of
competent and motivated workers, but yet it completely lacks the ability to
deliver. Extension, research, input supply and credit must improve greatly
if advance is to be made.
Climate
9.        The climate of Orissa is suitable for cropping throughout the year
with irrigation in the winter. Some crops and varieties, however are limited
to specific growing seasons. Temperatures are lowest in December-January,
with an average minimum daily of 120 - 15 0C , and average maximum daily of
27 - 28 C. The corresponding temperatures for the hottest months, April,
May and June, are minima 260 - 28 C and maxima 370 - 39C.
10.       Rainfall is the dominant climatic factor.  The yearly mean for the
State is about 1,500 mm, varying from 1,300 to 1,650 for the 13 districts
(Table 5). However, from one year to the next, variations in a district might
be as high as 50%. 76% of the rain falls in about 60 days during the four
months June-September, the main SW monsoon season.  But at both ends of the
monsoon there are periods of highly variable precipitation. High rainfall
intensities, typical during the monsoon, lead to much runoff, and flooding of
lowlands. The following figures show the rainfall distribution pattern:
Jan  Feb  Mar  Apr  May  Jun  Jul  Aug  Sep  Oct  Nov  Dec  Total
Rainfall-mm  14  26   24   37   73   215  352  338  239  136   40   6   1,500
Rainy days  0.9 1.6  1.5  2.5   4.4  10.1 15.6 15.5 12.2  6.2  1.6  0.4  72.5
11.       This pattern dictates that intensive cropping in rabi is only
possible with irrigation. The effect of the variable precipitation at either
end of the monsoon is more complex. With the available implements and draft
animals, unless there are sufficient pre-monsoon showers, farmers are unable
to prepare their land for cultivation before the monsoon breaks. Typically
this is towards the end of June (unless some limited source of irrigation is
available, they are not even able to start nursery plots before this date).
With perhaps two weeks required for land preparation, this implies planting
often as late as mid-July. Miost of the rice varieties in common use then
require about 100 days before the critical growth stage of grain formation.
By then, however, the peak, predictable portion of the monsoon has passed.
While average monthly data indicate that there should just be sufficient
rainfall to prevent the paddy from drying out, in fact the weekly analysis of



ANNEX 1
Page 4
data shows that there is an increasing probability of damaging moisture stress
after mid-September. Most of the kharif varieties planted (90%) are local
photosensitive types which, irrespective of the date of planting, will mature
late November to December. Under many of the soil and topography conditions
in Orissa, the effects of the vagaries of the monsoon can be extremely damag-
ing. Implications are that increasing the reliability of a kharif crop
depends on earlier planting or on the substitution of current long-maturity
varieties (145 days) with short maturing photo insensitive varieties (95-105
days). In soils of low water-holding capacities, i.e., the higher uplands, a
change from paddy to drought-resistant course grains would be desirable.
Physiography
12.       Orissa State lies between 17 031' - 22027' North latitude and 81 027' -
87 030' East longitude and covers an area of 156,000 sq km. It is bounded in
the southeast by the 300-mile coastline of the Bay of Bengal, in the north
by Bihar, in the northeast by West Bengal and in the southwest by Andhra and
Madhya Pradesh. Given the physical features and agro-climatic conditions,
Orissa may be divided roughly into four zones: 1) the Northern Plateau,
2) the Central Table Land, 3) the Eastern Ghat Region, and 4) the Coastal
Plain.
13.       The Northern Plateau is a continuation of the Chotanagpur plateau
of Bihar and includes the districts of Mayurbhanj, Keonjhar and Sundargarh,
ard parts of Dhenkanal, Cuttack, Balasore and Sambalpur (see Map). About 23%
of the total area of the State is in this region. There are a number of hill
ranges rising to elevations of 2,000' to 3,000' above sea level. About 45% of
the area is covered by forest, and 36% is under cultivation.
14.       The Central Table Land consists of the district of Bolangir, the
southern part of Dhenkanal district, and parts of Phulbani, Cuttack, and
Sambalpur districts. The region, as large as the northern plateau, is
generally flat with undulating and folded topography. It consists chiefly
of the Mahanadi basin. The table land gradually rises from east to west
and rarely is the elevation more than 1,000'. The western part of the
region is intersected by small rivers at almost regular distances. With
the commencement of irrigation from Hirakud Project, agricultural production
in the area has been greatly increased. Among the four agroclimatic regions
it is the most suitable for wheat and, next to the coastal region, for paddy.
15.       The Eastern Ghat Region mostly consists of hill ranges which belong
to the main line of Eastern Ghats along with some plains and valleys lying
between the hill ranges. These hill ranges also contain extensive plateau,
most of which have elevations between 1,000' to 1,500'. This is the largest
of the four regions, covering 36% of the State, and consists of the dis-
tricts of Koraput, Kalahandi, Phulbani and some of Ganjam. Forest is pre-
dominant, covering some of Kalahandi, more than half of Koraput and almost
all of Phulbani. The southern portion of Koraput is a plateau with elevation



ANNEX 1
Page 5
of more than 2,000 ft. A large part of Kalahandi district with an elevation
of about 900 ft. lies between the river Tel in the north and the Jeypote pla-
teau in the south and is the area most suitable for rice. Most of the other
areas of Koraput district are characterized by scattered and sharp isolated
hills, sometimes with thin forest but mostly bare. The hills in the area are
intercepted with small meandering streams having flood plains terraced and
cultivated with rice.
16.       The Coastal Plain runs from north to south, a strip 15 to 60 miles
wide in the districts of Balasore, Cuttack and Puri, and the eastern part of
Ganjam. The coastal plain is characterized by a number of deltas. This
zone covers about 18% of the total. Being influenced by the sea, the climate
differs from the other three regions. The relative humidity in the plain is
high throughout the year. For crop production this is the most important
region of Orissa.
Soils
17.       The soils of Orissa can be grouped into residual and transported
soils. The residual soils of the uplands are predominantly red and yellow
laterites which are typically highly leached, acidic (pH 5.0-6.50), and low
in organic matter and nitrogen, with high phosphorous fixation.   Although
lateritic, most do not contain lateritic masses within their profiles. They
tend to be shallow, sandy and erodible on the steeper slopes. Their pro-
ductivity is therefore limited both by fertility and water holding capacity.
The uplands also contain smaller areas of "black cotton" and brown, forest
soils, which are deeper, finer in texture, and generally more fertile, but
are much less permeable and more erosive than the red and yellow soils.
They have a high water holding capacity, but are slow to dry and since they
cannot be worked when very wet, seedbed preparation and land cultivation are
difficult without adequate water control.
18.       The transported alluvial soils found in the lowlands are moderately
fertile, slightly acidic, deep and more productive than the residuals. These
soils are ideal for paddy production. Residual moisture in the winter is not
enough for a second (rabi) crop of paddy, but pulses can be widely grown. The
potential of the alluvial soils of the coastal zone depends primarily upon
elevation and therefore the intensity and duration of inundation. In the low-
est areas, flood control measures are necessary to increase productivity. Al-
though the transported soils cover only 6% of the State's area, they are the
most important agriculturally. Along the coast there are problems of salinity.
Leaching, drainage and construction of embankments to check encroachment of
seawater are needed to improve fertility.
Topography and Land Use
19.       Microtopography is extremely important and traditionally plays a
major role in cropping patterns and practices. It is closely related to water
availability during the growth season, and crops are planned accordingly.



ANNEX 1
Page 6
Cultivable area is thus divided into what are traditionally termed high,
medium and low lands. The high lands are usually planted to upland, short
duration paddy or, in the lighter soils and slopes, to millets, maize, oil-
seeds and pigeon pea (arhar). They are the first to suffer in drought.
Medium lands are planted to medium duration paddy and lowlands to long dur-
ation, relatively flood resistant paddy, harvested in December and even
in January. Many farmers own parcels of land in all three categories, pro-
viding them with some form of insurance under varying climatic conditions.
Of the 6.9 million ha under cultivation, about 47% are upland, 30% medium and
23% lowland.
B. Agricultural Production
Performance
20.       Agricultural productivity has been virtually stagnant in Orissa
over the past ten years (Table 4). Yields of the major crop, kharif rice,
have fluctuated between 750 and 1,000 kg/ha. This during a period when,
elsewhere in India, farmers were learning about and reaping benefits from high
yielding varieties, chemical fertilizers and tubewells. There have, however,
been some large percentage increases in the production of a few minor crops.
Rabi rice production had increased six-fold by its peak in 1972/73 over its
1964/65 base. By 1973/74, production of wheat was 12 times and of maize 3
times the 1964-65 level; but the aggregate increase in annual production of
all cereals was about 400,000 tons during the 10-year period, rising from
3.9 M tons yearly average for 1964-67 to 4.3 M tons for 1972-75. Production
of most other crops has registered modest increases, with the exceptidn of
oilseeds which increased about 100% by 1973/74 over the 1964/65 base. To-
gether however, these crops occupy less than 10% of the total cropped area
in Orissa.
21.       The foodgrain production increase over the last 10 years is
disappointing. During this period 350,000 ha of new land has been brought
into cultivation, which, with average yields, should have produced 1.0 ton/
ha. With a further 500,000 ha newly irrigated, and 400,000 of this planted
to HYV, a yield increase of at least 1.0 ton/ha should have resulted, not
including the increased cropping intensity made possible. With an increase
of 600% in fertilizer use to about 63,000 tons of nutrients, it would be rea-
sonable to project production increases in the range 850,000 - 1 M tons. Yet
only 400,000 tons was recorded.
22.       In Orissa, the economy, income and nutrition are heavily dependent
on rice production. Total population grew by 24% from 1965 to 1975; using
three-year averages centered on 1966 and 1974, total foodgrain production has
increased only 11%; using the same three-year averages, per capita gross pro-
duction declined 7%. This decline obviously means farm families either have
less to consume at home or, more likely as Orissa is an exporting state, less
to sell in the market, inducing a decline in real income. In all but two of



ANNEX 1
Page 7
the last ten years, Orissa has produced enough foodgrain to consume at the
all-India per capita average and export. Official export figures show about
50,000 tons a year, but actual exports in good crop years are estimated to
reach several hundred thousand tons.                                          (

24.       About 15%, or 900,000 ha of the land is irrigated.  However, most
irrigation is limited to the rainy season and depends directly on the run of
the river; only about 250,000 ha can be fully irrigated in the dry season
(rabi) as well. There are plans for developing the tremendous potential of
ground and surface water in Orissa, but surface irrigation cannot be a signi-
ficant source of accelerated production in the short term.
C. Potentials and Constraints
25.       The potential for increasing agricultural production in Orissa
will be discussed briefly in the following paragraphs; they are treated
more fully in annexes on the related project components.
Natural Resources
26.       The favorable climate for plant growth and the relationship of
soils and their topography to rainfall utilization has been reviewed in
Part A. Limited additional land for cultivation is available, mainly land
now left fallow, estimated at 1 M ha, but wtdely scattered and varying from
year to year. Technology and resources (fertilizer, and labor -- animal
1/   For detailed area and production data see Table 4.  1974-75 data are
not presented here because drought made that year atypical.



ANNEX 1
Page 8
and human) are needed to release this potential. By contrast, however, there
are large areas in the hill region, under shifting cultivation by tribal
farmers, which should never be cultivated. They do not appear in the stat-
istics. Irreparable damage may be caused, not only to the forest but to lower
lying agricultural land below. Such misuse of forest area is widespread in
Koraput and Phulbani. GOI is presently studying the problem.
27.       Water sources and irrigation potential are large.  It is estimated
that at full development, perhaps as much as 4 M ha, compared with about
1.3 M ha now irrigated, could receive kharif irrigation; and about one-half of
that be irrigated in rabi as well. Developing this potential is a long and
costly process. However, there is much scope for increasing production on
existing irrigated land by overall improvement of water distribution and
drainage systems, and by better cultural practices, seed varieties, use of
fertilizers and water management. Yields could be at least doubled by these
means, if fully and effectively carried out.
Infrastructure and the Agricultural Economy
28.       Transportation, supply and marketing in Orissa are not highly de-
veloped. Railroads serve the eastern, northern and coastal districts of the
state, and a network of paved roads covers the major producing areas. The
interior, especially the hilly areas, is poorly served, however. Given im-
proved organization of institutions serving agriculture, and an increase in
storage and trucking capability, roads and railways should not pose a
major constraint in the short term, as is proven in good years, when
large quantities of grain are shipped.
Human and Social
29.       The poverty of most of Orissa's farmers is a major constraint to
development. Special programs to reach the small, marginal and tribal farmers
are gaining momentum, though they have welfare-political motivation. The
project, as planned, would reach many of these non-tribal farmers. Hence,
the importance of developing and extending low and medium cost, labor-intensive
technology, and of strengthening the service institutions.
30.       The leadership of Orissa is committed to increasing agricultural
production, giving it high priority in a selective "core sector" approach to
the 1975-76 Annual Plan. A definite policy has been formulated for rapid
increase in foodgrain production, to be achieved by more effective use of
water, increasing irrigated area, fuller use of fertilizer, improved seed and
other inputs, improved cultural practices and multiple cropping.
31.       The officers and workers of the Directorate of Agriculture (DAFP)
demonstrate much enthusiam and willingness to bring this about. At the core
of the project is the farmer of Orissa. He is hard working, intelligent, and
knowledgeable. He has shown ability to survive with meager means under



ANNEX I
Page 9
difficult conditions. He is quick to adopt and adapt when he is certain of
the benefits of the new method or technology. The rapid adoption of rabi
rice and wheat using HYV, the use of the mouldboard plow in many villages,
the tremendous enthusiasm for dugwells, and the recent rapid spread of
vegetable growing in Phulbani are examples of this.
32.       Tribal people, close to one quarter of Orissa's population, are
the main occupants of the hill regions of Orissa (Table 1). Though depending
largely on forest produce, many cultivate land, often shifting from one site
to another as fertility declines. There is little information on their crop-
ping patterns. It is known, however, that their yields are extremely low,
as is their use of inputs of all kinds. In the short-range there [s leed to
work on increasing yields in these areas, bearing in mind the special social
and geographical setting; viz, very small villages, often of 5-6 families,
widely dispersed in forest areas with only footpath communication. The longer
range solution for these regions will probably require a shift to a tree-
crop--forest economy combined with other crafts and occupations.
Farm Structure
33.       Land tenure, and especially the small size and scattered location
of holdings, are serious constraints to production. Basic changes in land-
holding patterns are both socially and politically very difficult to achieve.
In fact, landholdings will become smaller in years to come as districts
in Orissa, which were Princely States with relatively large holdings until
Independence, effect further land distribution. Small landholdings by
themselves need not inhibit production increase as proven by great yield
advances achieved in Japan, Taiwan and Korea. By developing messages
appropriate for small, marginal and sharecropping farmers and ensuring that
the "delivery system" is geared to reach them effectively, the project expects
to have an impact on this large sector, little reached heretofore.
Institutional
34.       Research  is the responsibility of the Orissa University of Agri-
culture and Technology (OUAT), in Bhubaneswar. It has but few, ill-equipped
regional research facilities and is poorly staffed. It is not, on the whole,
sufficiently field oriented and although it has achieved some good results in
rice, it has had little impact on total production through lack of ability to
adapt its results locally. The Central Rice Research Institute (CRRI) of ICAR
is at Cuttack, near Bhubaneswar, covering all aspects of rice production on an
all-India basis. It has produced superior modern varieties used in many
states.  The main effects of the CRRI program have not, however, been felt Ln
Orissa and the other eastern States where acceptance of kharif HYV has been
small and where the yield potential of rabi HYV has not been exploited. There
is much scope for strengthening OUAT's applied and adaptive research program
through the project, and developing in it, jointly with DAFP, the capability
for locally testing varieties and practices before final recommendations are
arrived at and extended (Annex 4).



ANNEX 1
Page 10
35.       Other supporting services, input supply, credit and marketing, are
supplied either through private channels or through the primary and regional
cooperative societies. (Seeds and plant protection chemicals are generally
supplied through DAFP.) In spite of its weaknesses, the cooperative move-
ment in all three service areas, is an essential factor in meeting the require-
ments of increased agricultural production in Orissa, to be achieved by
millions of farmers as envisaged. The cooperative sector must therefore
be utilized and will need considerable support (Details in Annexes 8 and 10).

ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agricultural Practices
1.        DAFP has made serious attempts to tackle the problems of low kharif
yields and has achieved moderate success in the limited areas of concentrated
effort. DAFP strategy for r4ce production in kharif published as "a guide-
line to extension", covers all aspects necessary to maximize production. The
package is comprehensive and complex and is carried out through a series of
supervised State demonstrations. While these trials have been valuable in
demonstrating that there is a great potential for increasing paddy yields up
to 3.5 tons/ha, the technology is impractical for wide acceptance, being far
beyond the farmers' financial resources and present technical capability.
Under the project, therefore, the approach would be modified by gradually
introducing simple improved cultural practices (para 21) which, with little
financial outlay, would give significant improvements in yields. This would
act as a catalyst to encourage farmers subsequently to change to short-term
HIYV and the use of nitrogen, which would follow the introduction of improved
cultural practices.
2.        A sequence for the introduction of improved practices has been
drawn up for paddy, ragi, wheat, maize, groundnuts and pulses. Concentra-
tion for all crops would be first on land preparation, increasing plant pop-
ulations through the introduction of the improved plow, high germination
capacity dressed seed of existing varieties, improved nursery techniques
and the introduction of line seeding or transplanting to permit efficient
weeding. Also some plant protection measures would be introduced. Begin-
ning with the existing practices, termed 0, progressively complex packages
of practices, termed A, B and C are presented in Appendix 1, for the major
crops.
Paddy
3.        Experience elsewhere has shown that the impact of these cultural
improvements alone on paddy production can increase yields by 30%, but in
Orissa the farming standards of the cultivators at present are such that
increases in yield of only 15-20% are projected. For the next stage, com-
prising the introduction of sub-optimal doses of fertilizer on local vari-
eties, increases of about 500 kg paddy/ha can be expected, based on nitrogen
response. The package of practices proposed in kharif includes a phased
increase in fertilizer from 30 to 60 kg/ha. No mention has been made of
phosphate or potash application to paddy, because crop responses are not



ANNEX 2
Page 2
yet known; but work on this would be a priority of the adaptive research
program (Annex 4) and recommendations would be introduced if responses proved
economic.
Ragi (Eleusine corocana)
4.        Ragi, a grain millet, due to its ability to adapt to shallow soils
and its short growing season, is currently being introduced in the upper
upland areas which are unsuited to paddy. The initial package of cultural
practices would be followed by the introduction of available improved early
maturing varieties. Fertilizer would be limited to 20 kg N/ha.
Pulses
5.        In addition to better cultivation of pulses grown on residual mois-
ture, quick improvements can also be expected by the introduction of already
tested, higher yield potential, mosaic tolerant varieties. But further
significant yield increases will only be achieved through improvements in
the moisture status of the crop by earlier planting, which can only follow
the introduction of shorter maturing paddy varieties. Improved varieties of
pigeon peas can be introduced in areas considered suitable and they can be
intercropped wih the millets.
Projected Uptake
6.        Yield assumptions for the different crops under the various stages
of practice uptake are presented in Annex 2, Table 1.
7.        The rate at which farmers adopt these practices would determine the
rate of increase in production. Table 2 presents a projection of the percen-
tage of farm area, after Year 4 and Year 8, which is expected to be under each
of the stages of practices for the various crops. Based on these, the mean
yields of foodgrains and oilseeds are conservatively expected to rise from the
present 0.8 tons/ha to 0.9 tons/ha in Year 4 and to 1.1 tons/ha in Year 8.
8.        Based on the projections (which cover paddy, wheat, maize, ragi,
pulses, groundnuts) the expected percentages of farmers who would adopt dif-
ferent packages of practices for paddy are:

ANNEX 3

INDIA
ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agricultural Extension
A. Introduction
1.        The development of appropriate crop and farm technology and an
effective system for its delivery are both essential if Orissa's agricultural
production is to be substantially increased within the next few years. Some
technology for increasing production is already available, and some farmers
have adopted it. Although the institutions carrying out research and provid-
ing agricultural extension are contributing to production, the potential for
improvement is great. However, in order to realize this potential, they
must be reorganized, their staff must be trained, their efforts intensified
and new methods of work employed. Improved agricultural extension and provi-
sion of supportive research are thus crucial components of this project
(for research, see Annex 4).
B. Background
Rural Administration
2.        At present, agricultural extension work forms an integral part
of the administrative system of the Government of Orissa (GOO). The State
of Orissa is divided into 13 Revenue Districts, each headed by a Collector
who is usually from the Indian Administrative Service and is the highest dis-
trict level administrative officer. He also heads a District Development
Board through which the development plans of all GOO departments are funneled
and coordinated. The local rural administrative unit is the Development
Block, of which there are 314 in Orissa, 20 to 30 in each district. Each
Block covers 100-200 villages and is administered by a Block Development
Officer (BDO). He is in the Community Development and Social Welfare Depart-
ment, but is responsible directly to the Collector. With the BDO, either
directly under him or on secondment, is a staff of technical people from the
various GOO Ministries and Departments, including Agriculture. The funding,
execution and supervision of rural development programs is through the BDO.
3.        Parallel to the Government administration, there is an elected local
government system. Each village elects a representative (Ward Member) to
a local committee (Panchayat) which incorporates about 8-10 villages. The
15-20 Sarpanch (elected chairmen of these committees) within the Block,



ANNEX 3
Page 2
together form an executive body or rural district council, the Panchayat
Samiti. The BDO is a member and the secretary of the Panchayat Samiti.
All budget sums allocated to the Block are placed in a fund which belongs
to the Panchayat Samiti, and is operated by the BDO. The BDO (and all other
GOO staff at the Block) are, in a sense, controlled by the Panchayat Samiti,
which has an important say in how budgets are to be spent. At the same
time, since there is no higher local government body at the district level,
the BDO, backed by the Collector, has strong authority. Yet this system
enables and ensures active local participation in development work.
C. The Recent Agricultural Extension System
4.        Agricultural extension responsibilities at the farmers' level are
divided between the District Administration, the Department of Community
Development and Social Welfare (CD & SW) and the Department of Agriculture
and Cooperatives (Table 1). Most Village Level Workers (VLW), while receiv-
ing technical guidance from the Directorate of Agriculture and Food Production
(DAFP), are administratively responsible to the Panchayat Samiti and to the
BDO; most of them are CD & SW employees, some DAFP employees seconded to the
Block. Under the Block Development Program in operation for the past 20 years,
each of the State's 314 Blocks had 10 VLW from CD & SW; more recently, 6 addi-
tional VLW have been seconded to many of the Blocks by DAFP. Similarly, each
Block has one or two Agricultural Extension Officers (AEO) from CD & SW, with
one other seconded from DAFP. On technical matters they report to and receive
support from DAFP, but they are administratively responsible to and receive
pay from the BDO. They guide and supervise the work of the VLW in agricul-
ture, yet have no administrative control over them.
5.        Thus agricultural extension at the local level is in the hands of
several different bodies. Most VLW are multi-purpose workers, unable to
concentrate on production, while some work under various schemes on a single
crop only. Most VLW and AEO have two if not three masters. Few formal or
informal relationships exist between the agricultural professionals of DAFP
and those of the Orissa University for Agriculture and Technology (OUAT). The
lines of command, supervision, and technology flow are complex and diffuse
(see Figure 2), and many staff, however capable and competent, cannot be fully
effective, resulting in few and diluted farm level contacts and activities,
with low impact.
6.        Compounding these difficulties, which are primarily organizational,
are difficulties inherent in Orissa and its general state of development:
large tracts of hilly terrain, sparsely inhabited mainly by primitive tri-
bal people; generally adequate but unstable rainfall which results in both
drought and flood; poorly developed irrigation (often poorly maintained,
where it is developed); large areas of unproductive soil; low level of train-
ing; poor transportation; and finally, lack of incentives and difficult liv-
ing and working conditions for a majority of the staff. All considered, the



ANNEX 3
Page 3
achievements of Orissa in agriculture (Annex 1) are to be appreciated, and
underscore the potential that can be realized when many of the constraints
will have been removed with the help of this project.
7.        Although intended to spend 80% of their working time on agricultural
extension, VLW in fact spend probably as little as 25%, and this usually on
agricultural work which is of a distributive or reporting nature, so that
few farmers receive extension advice in their fields. The remainder of the
VLW's time is taken up by a large variety of duties such as public health,
family planning, small savings, school affairs, elections, and famine re-
lief. It is the local ward members who direct the VLW to particular fami-
lies in the village, and the priorities are probably not often based on
problems of agricultural production. The initial training of most VLW, a
two-year course in a Community Development training center, and that of the
AEO who were recruited from colleges of agriculture, was probably good and
sufficient. But little in-service training has been given them since.
8.        In an attempt to remedy the situation, various types of special
schemes have been established by DAFP, usually at Government of India ini-
tiative, in which additional AEO and VLW and less trained workers called
Field Man Demonstrators were recruited and employed for particular crops and
in specific areas. Intensive Rice Cultivation, Cotton and Sugarcane, the
Intensive Agricultural District Program (IADP) and the Drought Prone Area
Program (DPAP) are examples. Other departments also have programs which
closely touch upon agriculture, such as the Small Farmers Development Agency
(SFDA) and the Integrated Tribal Development Project (ITDP).
9.        Responsibility for all DAFP programs in the district and for AEO
technical backing lies with the District Agricultural Officer (DAO). The
State is divided into 30 agricultural districts, each headed by a DAO. Al-
though he should be directing the agricultural production program, and is
active in setting the yearly production targets, he has little staff under
his control. The DAO is also in charge of the seed production and distribu-
tion programs (each has several small seed farms) and has some responsibility
for, though little control over fertilizer distribution.
10.       From the DAO upwards, the hierarchy is clearer:  the 30 DAO are each
responsible to one of 6 Range Deputy Directors of Agriculture (DDA). Each DDA
is supported by three Subject Matter Specialists in agronomy, plant protection
and soils. The ranges and districts do not coincide with the administrative
boundaries as there are two to three Revenue Districts in each range, and each
of the 13 Revenue Districts contains 2-3 agricultural districts. The DDA are
directly responsible to the senior agricultural technical officer of the
department, namely the Director of Agriculture and Food Production. He
reports to his administrative seniors, the Secretary for Agriculture and
Cooperatives and the Agricultural Production Commissioner, under the Minister
of Agriculture and Cooperatives. The agricultural headquarters is supported
by 8 Joint Directors (JDA) and 10 DDA each acting as a specialist in a narrow
discipline and reporting directly to the Director, DAFP. Most of these JDA
and DDA have their own small unit subordinate staff.



ANNEX 3
Page 4
11.       The GOO, having analyzed the situation, and having been made aware
of achievements in production obtained under World Bank supported projects
in Rajasthan and Madhya Pradesh by changing extension along lines set by Mr.
D. Benor, World Bank consultant, asked that revitalization of its agricultural
extension system be included as part of the Agricultural Intensification
Project for World Bank financing.
D. The New Agricultural Extension System
12.       For the project, the agricultural extension service would be revi-
talized under a new system now to be introduced State-wide. A well-trained,
highly motivated, and technically competent single organization would be
created, making optimal use of existing staff and with very few additions.
The organization would be given the means to reach the majority of Orissa's
farmers with relevant and practical technical guidance aimed at increasing
production and income. The available manpower, except for trained subject
matter specialists, is sufficient and of excellent potential to achieve re-
sults, provided there is redeployment, a concentration on extension per se,
and a continuing and frequent training element. The new system would take
over all agricultural extension work and all "special schemes" of extension
would be incorporated.
13.       The new extension system would be spread over the entire State be-
cause, even though it may encounter initial difficulties, farmers would
benefit more from early partial coverage than by. waiting for full coverage
until all staff are fully retrained and qualified. Orissa would be the
first Indian State to adopt the new system statewide, without previous
trial; but GOO prefers not to have different systems operating concurrently
and is confident that the proposed system is correct and that the change-
over can be swiftly made.
14.       In agricultural extension, the project would provide for:
(a) Reorganization of the entire extension staff in a single
line of command under DAFP.
(b) An increase in the number of Ranges from 6 to 13, and with
it the number of professional supervisors and Subject Matter
Specialists.
(c) A method of field and village visits which ensures regular
coverage of most farms.
(d) Effective and continuous applied in-service training.
(e) Strengthening of VLW pre-service training to ensure a continu-
ing supply of well-trained workers.



ANNEX 3
Page 5
(f) Facilities and equipment for improved training and extension.
(g) Staff and facilities for field trials and demonstrations.
(h) Vehicles and travel allowances for greater mobility.
(i) Housing for field staff.
(j) Viable extension-research-farmer coordination.
Reorganization in a Single Line of Command
15.       Almost all VLW would remain in their present locations, but 70%
(about 2,500 out of 3,500) would be transferred to DAFP from CD & SW control,
leaving 3-4 VLW per Block with the BDO to cover all non-agricultural work.
Together with all 3,100 VLW level workers on DAFP special schemes, including
Field Demonstrators, 5,600 VLW, or equivalent, would be made available for
redeployment to full-time agricultural extension work. With 3.4 M active
farm families in Orissa, there would thus be, on average, one VLW for every
600 farm families.
16.       One AEO would supervise eight VLW.  The 700 AEO thus required would
be drawn from the 650 employed in the Blocks (see para 4) and more than 100
already in DAFP. The AEO would be directly responsible to the DAO who, in
turn, is responsible to the Range DDA.
17.       This organizational structure would enable a rational selection
of objectives and goals to be made, concentrating efforts on high priority
areas and allocating human, physical and financial resources accordingly.
All special schemes and programs would be incorporated within the one system.
Flow of technology information would be streamlined.
Strengthening of Staff
18.       Because of the added supervisory load and work responsibilities in
DAFP, additional managerial and supervisory staff would needed. A senior
staff member would be appointed at DAFP headquarters as Additional Director
of Agriculture, Extension (ADA Extension) to head the Extension Service. He
would be principally responsible for the reorganization. The number of agri-
cultural ranges would be increased from 6 to 13, to coincide approximately
with the 13 existing revenue districts. This would improve coordination with
other aspects of rural development. The additional 7 ranges would need to be
staffed, each to be headed by a Deputy Director of Agriculture who would be
transferred from existing duties. The thirty District Agricultural Officers
(DAO) (see para 9), in turn, would need further support if enough supervision
and guidance are to be given to AEO. Sixty Additional District Agricultural
Officers (ADAO) would therefore be posted, averaging two to a district, and
each would closely supervise and guide 8-10 AEO in the field, the DAO directly
supervising 4-6 AEO.



ANNEX
Page 6
19.       A viable field extension program needs strong technical support by
Subject Matter Specialists. At present, there are only three at each of
the six ranges (see para 10) and many more would be needed under the proposed
system. Since they are not readily available in all fields and in sufficient
numbers, their posting would be phased as they became available. Each range
would have, by the end of Year 3, one SMS in each of these fields: agronomy;
plant protection; farm machinery; water management; and farm management.
Staffing would thus be approximately (depending on location):
Range        District        Block
DDA             13             -              -
DAO              -             30             -

/a   from Year 3
20.       SMS in agronomy, plant protection and farm machinery are available,
but in water management and farm management, though there is excellent poten-
tial, not enough have been trained to the standard required. Positive action
will be needed (starting at the Extension Training Institutes - see Annex 12)
to select and train specialists in these subjects. Plans should also be made
to increase the number of SMS from Year 3 so that they can be made available
at the agricultural district level, with one SMS in each discipline in each
district as extension, and the demand for its services, develops.
21.       The search for suitable SMS should already have begun and, from the
start of the project, suitable AEO and OUAT graduates would be selected to be
trained up in SMS positions. In the meantime, the training and information
officer (TO), one posted permanently to each range, would assist a team of
five SMS to support and train a field staff of about 54 AEO and 430 VLW per
range (on average, though varying according to farm population per range and
other factors). They would work closely with the researchers, particularly
through the 13 adaptive research farms (see Annex 4).
22.       Figure 1 illustrates the proposed agricultural extension organiza-
tion and staffing.
The New Extension Methodology
23.       Under the new agricultural extension system, an intensive farm visit
program would be introduced. The farming areas and the estimated 3.4 M farm
families of Orissa would be divided into 5,600 units of 400-800 families each,
to which VLW would be allocated. Each unit, in turn, would be divided into 8
more or less equal groups of 50-100 farm families each. Each of these groups



ANNEX 3
Page 7
would be visited by its VLW, on a fixed day of the week, once every two weeks.
Thus the VLW would cover 4 groups in one week and 4 groups the next. The day
and location of visits for each VLW would be well publicized and documented
both among farmers and in the Block and district offices. The VLW would
spend the full day with the scheduled farmers' group; during the morning
hours he would visit the farmers in their fields and, in the afternoon,
hold an open meeting in the village, at a predetermined hour and location
to be proposed by the group.
24.       It is not possible, nor effective, to attempt to reach all farmers
in direct extension work in the field. Many are part-time farmers, others
not accustomed to contacts with outside agencies, and yet others are best
influenced by neighbors or relatives. Under the guidance of their superiors
and in consultation with the Panchayat and the farmers themselves, therefore,
the VLW would select 10 contact farmers from each group. Contact farmers
could be from all levels of village society, including sharecroppers, and
would be selected for their potential influence and willingness to collab-
orate with extension in following recommendations. Changes in contact
farmers could be made, especially between seasons, if shown to be ineffec-
tive or if they so desired. The VLW, in his field visits, would make spe-
cial efforts to reach at least all the contact farmers' fields, and they,
in turn, would share the responsibility of organizing and attending the
afternoon meetings. Their fields may be used as demonstration plots and
for carrying out some field trials. They would have priority in farmer-
training programs.
25.       With 700 AEO, each would supervise 6-10 VLW and, at least once every
two weeks, would participate in part of each VLW program of visits to farm-
ers, following such a pattern as would enable him to reach every farmers'
group at least twice each season. He would check that the VLW visits and
meetings were being carried out on schedule and would assist both VLW and
farmers in technical matters. He would also assist the VLW in carrying out
demonstrations and simple trials. This close ongoing relationship between
AEO and VLW should relieve the latter of the need for any formal reporting.
He is expected, however, to carefully conduct a diary record to be scruti-
nized and signed by any supervising superior visiting him.
26.       The AEO's supervisors up to the highest level, having access to
the VLW and AEO visits schedules, would be able quickly and easily, by random
check, to find out whether visits are being made as planned, not only by
making surprise visits to the areas in which VLW and AEO are scheduled on
particular days, but also by questioning farmers about the visit days in
their villages, the names of the VLW and AEO, the messages learned, etc.
Experience elsewhere in India has shown that, once farmers become aware of
the fixed visit schedule, they themselves become effective monitors by their
complaints if visits are not maintained regularly and in a satisfactory
manner.



ANNEX 3
Page 8
full day every two weeks, learning in a practical and applied manner, the
relevant messages which are to be delivered to the farmers in their next
round of visits. The advice given must be such that can be immediately used
by them.
28.       One two-weekly VLW training session would be given at the Block
level, by the AEO to their 8-25 VLW as a group. This training would be
completely practice oriented, with time devoted to actually carrying out
field operations, such as sowing and spraying. VLW would be free to raise
and discuss any problems they had encountered during the preceding field
visits.
29.       The second two-weekly training session would be organized and led
by the ADAO or DAO with his 8-10 AEO and all his VLW (48-80). SMS from the
range office would participate in this session. The Training Officer would
organize the plan for the entire range, in which approximately 7-8 such train-
ing sessions would be held every four weeks (90 in all 13 ranges). These
sessions should, if possible, be held at the range adaptive research farm.
30.       The training of the AEO, ADAO and DAO would be held once every four
weeks at range level, preferably for two days. The Training Officer and
SMS, together with researchers from OUAT, would provide the training. It
is at these sessions that the details for all the ongoing training and pro-
grams of work for the next four weeks are to be learned and carefully
planned. For this purpose, the staff of each district would work separately
during part of the second day and come up with district-wise plans. Repre-
sentatives of other related organizations would be invited to participate
in these sessions to ensure coordination of activities.
31.       Twice a year, once before each crop season, the entiie staff of each
district would gather for a four-day course to be given by OUAT and range
staff. It is in this course that more basic and theoretical knowledge would
be taught, to give the field workers an overall view of the major crops and
innovations which would be recommended in the coming season. These would be
based upon the previous year's results which would be presented and analyzed.
Each range would hold 2-4 such courses every season, one per district.
32.       In addition to these various training activities, special short
courses, for specific subjects, would be prepared for SMS, DAO and ADAO,
and AEO as needed, to be decided upon by the ADA Extension and his senior
staff.

34.       In addition to participation in special courses, which would be
planned for the senior technical staff, the SMS would maintain close, on-
going relationships with researchers at the regional stations and at the
range adaptive research farms, where they would also participate actively
in conducting field trials. This link is extremely important.



INNE' -
Page 10
35.       The Training Officer would be responsible for advance planning and
monitoring of all training sessions in his range, for obtaining relevant
training publications from the Information Unit at Headquarters, and for
obtaining or producing leaflets which summarize the subject matter and im-
pact points of each session. He would ensure that these materials were
ready in good time and distributed to AEO and VLW at each training session.
The multiplicity of training activities in the range, district and Block
requires careful and accurate pre-planning, well in advance, and ensuring
that the equipment and facilities are available and prepared in time.
36.       Provision is made in the project for 20 man-months a year of train-
ing outside Orissa, especially to enable SMS to specialize in particular
fields best developed in other Indian States. Training Officers and DAO
may also visit other States to learn new methods. Several fellowships for
training abroad would be provided.
37.       With high emphasis placed on the timely delivery of information of
immediate value to the farmer, the SMS have a major role in the whole train-
ing system proposed. Situated at the range offices and aided by OUAT re-
searchers (mainly those of the regional stations and range experimental
farms), the SMS teams would have a heavy training load. Each team would be
expected to participate in 7-9 one-day VLW training sessions conducted by
the DAO/ADAO, in addition to a 2-day training session for AEO and others at
the range, together about 10 days during a 4-week period. In the two pre-
season periods, with 2-4 four-day courses in each range, the SMS would be
working only in training. This heavy load would remain until additional SMS
begin to be posted in the districts in the third year of the project, when
they would gradually assume a growing share of the training responsibilities,
aided by new Training Officers.
38.       This makes it all the more important that the training program be
well planned and executed, and that the DDA gives his Training Officer all
the support he needs. It must also be coordinated closely with the VLW
visit program. The two-weekly training sessions should be held, for each
unit, on a regular day of the week, e.g., in a given district, Friday for
one ADAO, Saturday for the second. Also, the two-weekly sessions would be
held one week in one district, the next week in another, alternating. The
VLW visit days have to be planned accordingly, four per week.
Strengthening Basic Training
39.       In addition to the intensification of the ongoing training, which
would play a vital role in the new extension methodology and organization,
much strengthening of the programs and facilities for pre-service and basic
in-service upgrading training is needed. Also, there is room for the up-
grading of training for women extension workers (gram sevikas), not included
otherwise in the extension reorganization. These training components are
presented in Table 2 and a separate annex (Annex 12).



ANNEX 3
Page 11
Equipment and Facilities Supporting Extension Work and Training
40        Equipment and operating costs would be provided for publications,
preparation of audio-visual aids, duplicating machines and photographic
services needed for effective extension work with the farmers. Initially
district offices would act as the ranges, subsequently the district offices
would be equipped beginning from year three. Materials and equipment such as
flip charts and blackboards, needed by AEO and VLW in their ongoing training
and extension work, would also be provided.
41.       Since heavy emphasis is to be placed on the ongoing training of all
field staff, funds would be provided for incremental operating costs in-
volved, such as preparation of materials and aids for the training sessions,
renting space, honoraria for outside lecturers and transporting extension
workers on field visits. The costs for the supporting equipment and facili-
ties are presented in Annex 16.
Demonstrations and Field Trials
42.       The demonstration method in India's extension work has been widely
used, with varying results. It is therefore proposed not to place special
emphasis on a demonstration program, but rather to utilize the demonstra-
tion effect in fields of contact farmers, where new practices would be em-
ployed, and in simple field trials which would be carried out on both adap-
tive research farms and farmers' fields. These would form an ongoing part
of the work of every VLW and AEO. Supporting materials and funds for signs,
markings, group tours, etc. would be provided.
43.       A wide series of simple field trials would be carried out as one in-
tegrated program, both on the 13 adaptive research farms and on selected
farmers' fields. Thirteen of 85 existing DAFP seed farms, one for each range,
would be turned into adaptive research farms of about 20 ha each. A research
officer would be seconded by OUAT to head each of the farms, and to guide and
supervise the entire field trial program in the range. He would be respon-
sible directly to the range DDA and, working closely with the SMS and other
technical field staff, would form the all important link between research
and extension. Recommendations would be tried out on a large enough scale
before being delivered to the farmers to ensure their validity under local
conditions and, at the same time, extension workers would receive practical
training and gain confidence in their recommendations through active partici-
pation in field trial work. The researchers from the central and regional
OUAT research stations, through work with the range adaptive research farms
and field trials, would be pulled into closer contact with farmer's actual
problems and with the extension people.
44.       The present managerial and working staff of each farm would be
sufficient. The project would provide for improvement of the farm and its
facilities--irrigation, drainage, machinery, buildings and equipment (for
details, see Annex 4 on research).



ANNEX 3
Page 12
Transportation and Mobility
45.       A key feature of the new extension methodology and its organization
is the requirement that the entire staff spend most of their time in the
field. VLW would spend four days on pre-planned visits and a fifth day for
field trials, demonstrations and special requests each week. AEO would be
four days a week with VLW. SMS would be expected to devote two days to field
visits and two days to field trials each week. ADAO and DAO would be at
least three days a week in the field. Transportation is thus crucial to
the success of the program, and is a serious constraint to extension today.
The project would provide for:
11 - cars
83 - 4-wheel drive diesel light vehicles
13 - pickups diesel
13 - minibus diesel
700 - loans for motorcycles
2800 - loans for bicycles
Since similar vehicles are in operation in Orissa and in DAFP, their main-
tenance should not pose a serious problem. Loans for motorcycles, however,
should be increased and conditions changed in order to make it attractive
for AEO to purchase them. AEO mobility must be assured. The motor vehicles
are incremental to the existing, or replace such vehicles as should be (but
without the project probably would not be) taken out of service. About
one-half the VLW own bicycles already.
46.       Incremental vehicle operating and maintenance costs for the project
period (5 years) are provided for, based on 25,000 km/year. A motorcycle
allowance would be given to AEO based on 10,000 km/year. Basic travel allow-
ance for incremental staff is provided for. However, it is felt that greatly
increased demands would be made of the entire staff, in terms of time and
distances of travel and length of stay away from home, justifying an additional
travel allowance to all staff. This show of concern and appreciation for
hardship and effort to be made by the staff, otherwise relatively lowly paid,
would have important motivational and incentive effects and would contribute
to the effectiveness of the extension service (Annex 16).
Offices and Residence
47.       Efficient management and administration of as vast and widespread
organization as the extension service requires reasonable office quarters and
facilities. Twenty-four of 30 districts have modest offices, sufficient
for the project period. Only three ranges have office buildings of their
own. The project would provide modest buildings, office furniture and
equipment needed to cover the entire State.
48.       Housing for field staff should be located where they do their work.
Many of VLW and AEO now occupy houses at Block headquarters. However, some
will have to vacate them, and for additional staff to be posted in most



ANNEX 3
Page 13
rural areas there is no accommodation available for rent. It is proposed,
therefore, to provide housing for 35% of VLW and AEO as a minimum. In addi-
tion, housing would have to be provided for new DDA, ADAO and SMS. In homes
of AEO and VLW, provision for office space and equipment would be made.
For costs, see Annex 16.
Coordination, Program Planning and Evaluation
49.       Under the project, both agricultural extension and agricultural re-
search would be strengthened, the interdependence between the two increased,
and their commitment to the farming community made greater. There is need
for a systematic approach for the development, delivery and ultimate use of
effective and beneficial technology. It is proposed that a program planning
process take place twice each year, before the kharif and before the rabi
seasons.
50.       The existing top level Extension and Research Policy Advisory Panel
would convene twice each season: the first meeting would take place 2-1/2 to
3 months before the start of the season, when the general results of the for-
mer season would be already known. The purpose of this meeting would be to
come out with broad guidelines of agricultural production policy for the next
season. The second meeting, to be held 1 to 1-1/2 months before the start
of the season, would evaluate, amend and approve the detailed plans on the
basis of extension district and range discussions, and of research stations
and OUAT talks (para 55). The Advisory Panel would be chaired by the APC,
and members would be Secretary, Agriculture and Cooperation; Commissioner
Command Area Development; Director, CRRI; Vice-Chancellor, OUAT; Director,
DAFP; the Officer on Special Duty, Agriculture (who heads the Project Plan-
ning); the Dean of Research Unit, OUAT; the ADA Extension, and top level
representatives of input agencies and the cooperative movement.
51.       After the first seasonal panel meeting guidelines would be conveyed
to each of the two organizations--OUAT and the Extension Service of DAFP--by
their respective representatives, starting in both organizations an intensive
process of summarizing results and developing recommendations and plans at the
different levels, in light of the policy guidelines. The process for research
is described in Annex 4.
52.       The Director of DAFP and the ADA Extension would convene all the
JDA, DDA and DAO in Bhubaneswar for one day, inviting the APC to present the
policy guidelines. These would be discussed as to their relevance, in broad
terms, to the various ranges, districts and agricultural disciplines. The
schedule for district and range meetings would be drawn.
53.       Following this meeting, each DAO helped by his ADAO, would hold meet-
ings in the various Blocks with the AEO, VLW, BDO and members of the Panchayat
Samiti to develop, based on last season's experiences, a plan and recommen-
dations for the coming season. This round of meetings should take no more
than three weeks, at the end of which a district-level summary meeting would



ANNEX 3
Page 14
be held with the DDA, DAO, ADAO, SMS, 2-3 AEO, 2-3 Sarpanch (para 3) and BDO,
and the researcher of the adaptive research farm. Specific recommendations
for crops, and practices would be spelled out.
54.       The ADA Extension would be responsible for this planning process.
When the districts would have completed their plans, he would convene four
meetings, one at each of the applied research stations. DDA, DAO, SMS, TO
and range researchers from 3-4 ranges of the agro-climatic zone represented
by the station would participate, various JDA from headquarters, and the
Research Director and researchers from OUAT. They would form work groups
for the major crops and develop extension and adaptive research recommenda-
tions.
55.       Armed with the plans from the four regions, based on statewide work,
the ADA Extension and the Director of Research would present to the recon-
vened Extension and Research Advisory Panel, their recommendations which,
after discussion and amendments, would be approved for action, completing
the cycle started 1-1/2 months earlier.
56.       Each range and each adaptive research farm would then finalize its
seasonal plans, and translate them into training to be transferred at the
4-day pre-seasonal sessions to the entire district staff.
57.       The process described appears at first to be complex.  During the
first year of the project, the planning process would go down to the dis-
trict level only, where only some representatives from the Blocks would
attend. As staff is recruited and trained, the local planning capability
would increase. The importance of this element of the project cannot be
overemphasized, for the key to the success of the project is a technically
competent and highly motivated staff. The planning process is the best way
to arrive at both technically sound recommendations and plans, and a deeply
involved staff to carry them out. The early communication of information
needed by the supporting services is also assured, as is the contribution
of the farmers' experience through their representatives. The BDO would
be in an excellent position to contribute his important coordinative role.
Lastly, adaptive research results would feed into the system, and field
trial plans for the coming season would develop from a joint research-
extension effort.
58.    The Advisory Panel would also serve as an evaluating body.  It would
receive the analysis of field-crop cuttings, research results and the survey
results of the Farm Management Units, and would decide on any special evalu-
ative studies that need be done. A small committee headed by the Officer on
Special Duty, Agriculture, would prepare the evaluation materials and pro-
posals for the Panel. Members would be statistician, research (see Annex 4);
statistician, DAFP; Head, Farm Management Unit; and evaluation specialist,
Extension Education Department, OUAT.

INDIA
ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agricultural Research
I. BACKGROUND
1.        Orissa has considerable scope for increasing crop production and
extending cropped area, both rainfed and irrigated, at present lagging behind
other Indian States in yield of many crops. Rice yield is about 85% of the
all-India average and only about 50% of yield in the four most progressive
States. Adoption of improved cropping systems has been inhibited by a number
of factors, one of which has been a limited extension effort and another, a
lack of a viable research effort to supply extension and farmers with needed
information.
Agricultural Research Institutions
2.        Two major institutions conduct basic and applied research in Orissa;
the Orissa University of Agriculture and Technology (OUAT) and the Central
Rice Research Institute (CRRI) of the Indian Council of Agricultural Research
(ICAR). The Department of Agriculture conducts some adaptive research; and
the Regional Research Laboratory of the Council of Scientific and Industrial
Research (CSIR) conducts some research at Bhubaneswar on medicinal, aromatic
and other plants, which may have economic value to the pharmaceutical
industry.
CRRI
3.        CRRI was established in 1946 and, since 1966, has been a part of
ICAR. It is involved in all aspects of basic and applied research on rice
to develop high-yielding varieties (HYV) under a range of different agro-
ecological conditions for use throughout India and with resistance or tol-
erance to the major pests and diseases. It is located at Cuttack near
Bhubaneswar, the capital, on a 121-ha research farm with a professional staff
of 120 scientists of which 60% have PhD degrees and most of the remainder,
Masters degrees. All major disciplines are represented and the research
effort is on a commodity-oriented multidisciplinary team approach. Its total
annual budget was Rs 8 M in 1975/76. It is hoped to finance certain aspects of
CRRI activities under a National Research Project presently under preparation.
4.        There is some cooperation between CRRI and OUAT and the Department
of Agriculture. The Director of CRRI is on the Board of OUAT, and the Dean of
Research of OUAT is a member of CRRI's management. There are joint programs



ANNEX 4
Page 2
whereby selected varieties developed at CRRI are tested with Extension on
State research farms and in "minikit" (small packet of seed and fertilizer)
trials on a small portion of cooperating farmers' fields.
5.        CRRI has produced a number of HYV of rice for irrigated areas, and
these, though used in Orissa, have increased production most notably in Pun-
jab and Haryana. It is also intimately involved in the All-India Coordinated
Rice Improvement Project (AICRIP) in cooperation with State rice research pro-
grams. AICRIP has produced a number of varieties which are being grown in
the more humid high rainfall areas of Tamil Nadu, Kerala, Mysore, Andhra
Pradesh and West Bengal.
OUAT
6.        OUAT was established in 1962.  At its inception, the main research
station at Bhubaneswar and two small substations were transferred to OUAT from
the Department of Agriculture. The primary function of the University has
naturally been teaching, with research mainly supporting higher degree
studies in Agriculture, Botany, Agronomy, Economics, Extension, Horticulture,
Mycology, Pathology, Nematology, Statistics, Physiology and Chemistry. OUAT
graduates about 125-130 students to BSc level and about 65 graduates to MSc
level each year. The Veterinary College graduates about 42 BSc and 24 MSc
annually and about 20 BSc graduates from the Agricultural Engineering College.
7.        Research at OUAT is under a Dean of Research who has responsibility
for coordination of research at the University and for administrative direc-
tion of the research stations. Research coordination is accomplished, not
very effectively, through meetings with the Deans of the different faculties.
They have full responsibility for their research programs, which reinforces a
disciplinary approach to crop problems; but there is some collaboration be-
tween certain disciplines, such as between Entomologists and Plant Pathologists
working with Rice Breeders to produce varieties resistant to serious pests
and diseases.
OUAT Research Stations
8.        At present, OUAT has seven research stations transferred from the
Department of Agriculture. The main station on the University campus at
Bhubaneswar, is a research farm of 246 ha, of which 20 ha is flooded during
the monsoon season. The farm has 8 tanks, 4 tubewells and 5 tractors, and
facilities for conducting research on various agronomic and horticultural
crops as well as livestock. Most of the research program at the main station
and at the outlying stations is currently financed by ICAR in all-India coor-
dinated projects. The present annual budget for the main Research Station is
Rs 1.72 M of which 45% is involved in ICAR shemes. At the outlying stations
the percentage is much higher, with 92% involved in ICAR schemes at the
Chiplima and Pottangi Research Stations out of annual budgets of Rs 0.59 M and
Rs 0.14 M respectively. The majority of the staff in these ICAR schemes have
temporary appointments with the University, which do not lend much permanence
to the research effort.



ANNEX 4
Page 3

Of the above outlying research stations, Chiplima is the largest with a 93-ha
farm, and 10 professional staff and 77 supporting staff devoted full time to
research and without teaching responsibilities. In addition to the research
stations, OUAT also conducts research on some Government farms as follows:
