Research Programming and Funding - OUAT
10.       OUAT has a Board of Management, and a Finance Establishment Commit-
tee which approves all budgets and programs. The Finance and Establishment
Committee consists of the Vice-Chancellor of the University, who is Chairman,
GOO Secretary of Finance, Secretaries of Agriculture and Cooperation, Animal
Husbandry and Forestry, Finance and Community Development, Director of Agri-
culture and a Member of the Legislative Assembly. This group scrutinizes all
budget requests prepared by the University Controller, in consultation with
Deans and Heads of Departments. The Department of Agriculture appears to have
sufficient authority on this Committee to prevent approval of certain bud-
getary items of the University which it does not favor, and this has created
friction in the past. All budgets and programs approved by the Finance and
Establishment Committee are then taken to the University Board of Manage-
ment for approval. The Board has no less than 19 members, including the Pro-
Chancellor, Vice-Chancellor, Chief Secretary, Secretaries of Agriculture,
Animal Husbandry, Finance, Cooperation and Forestry, Community Development,
Chief Engineer, 2 members of Legislative Assembly, 3 nominees of Government,
University Teacher Representative, Registered Graduate Representative,
Directors of Agriculture and Animal Husbandry and Farmers' Representative.
The University can go back for supplementary budgets if the need arises before
the annual budget is approved.
Research Accomplishments - QUAT
Rice
11.       Several HYV of rice have been produced by OUAT.  Three semi-dwarf
selections through hybridization were released as Hema, Kumar and Rajeswari,
have high yield, and are suitable for red and laterite soils - the major
soil types of Orissa. Subsequently varieties which are early maturing,
adaptive to low land conditions and resistant to major pests and diseases
have been found. These include the recently released varieties Parijata and
Suphala which have gained acceptance by cultivators because of early maturing
and high yield (4-6 tons/ha). One of the varieties, Parijata as OR34-16,
ranked first in AICRIP trials during the 1974 rabi season. A number of
promising drought resistant varieties have been selected in preliminary
trials, including DR-92, OR45-61-23 and CR-125-12-17.
Millets
12.       One variety of ragi, the most important millet in Orissa, has
been released as Divyasingha. Two new varieties, not yet released, ranked
first in All-India Coordinated Trials for the early group (T 27A-P4 = IE 273
Mutant) and for the medium group (IE 642).
Pulses
13.       Three new varieties of greem gram have been evolved through mu-
tation breeding (TT 8E, 9E and 18E) and are superior to Pusa Baisakhi and
Koppergan in yield and tolerance to yellow mosaic.



ANNEX 4
Page 5
Oilseeds
14.       A number of HYV of groundnut have been produced at Chiplima.  One
of them, OG 73-2 gives 15% higher yield in rabi than the popular variety,
AK 12-24.
Wheat
15.       Four new varieties of wheat have been produced, which are early
maturing, high yielding and tolerant to Helminthosporium and Alternaria
blights.
Farming Systems
16.       Economic evaluations of different cropping systems have shown
that, in irrigated highland light soil areas, three crops per year of maize/
cauliflower/groundnut, or rice/potato/maize can be grown profitably. In-
creased net income can be accomplished by cropping patterns which include
more vegetables, permitting growth of as many as 5-6 crops per year. Im-
proved water management practices have been determined for rice, maize and
potato. Mixed cropping trials in rainfed regions during kharif have led to
recommendation of maize/ragi, red gram/ragi, black gram (on ridges)/rice (in
furrows), or cotton/rice (in medium lands).
Pest and Disease Control
17.       Biological control of the coconut caterpillar has been achieved by
release of two larval parasites and a pre-pupal parasite and these are in
great demand by coconut orchard growers. Various chemicals have been tested
and recommended for control of nematodes, gall midge and stem borer, and rice
weevil. Other chemicals have been identified for control of false smut disease
and udbatta disease in rice and common scab of potato. Strains of rice,
moderately resistant to blight (xanthomonas oryzae), have been isolated in
cooperative trials with rice breeders.
Soil Fertility and Dryland Agriculture
18.       Most red and laterite acid soils of Orissa have been found to
contain high amounts of free oxides of iron and aluminum, causing high
phosphorus fixation and unavailability to plants. In order to minimize
phosphate fixation, various phosphates have been tested and raw and partially
acidulated rock phosphates, dicalcium phosphate and a mixture of rock phos-
phate and superphosphate are now recommended for these soils, depending on
degree of acidity. Various optimum fertilizer applications, for the improved
HYV of rice have been worked out. By-products of the paper mill industry have
been found useful in liming acid soils in dryland areas during the kharif
season. Paper mill sludge at the rate of 3 tons/ha gives high production of
black gram, groundnut, ragi, and cotton.



ANNEX 4
Page 6
Constraints
19.       Despite the accomplishments outlined above, much more effective ap-
plied and adaptive research is needed to overcome the constraints to crop
production in Orissa. As an example, HYV cover 80-90% of the area under rice
in irrigated rabi season, but only about 10% during the rainfed kharif season.
Assurance of water source, through irrigation, in the rabi season contributes
to farmers' willingness to use HYV and inputs of fertilizers and pesticides.
During the kharif season the uncertainty of rainfall, fluctuating water lev-
els, greater susceptibility to pests and diseases and reduced solar radia-
tion, all contribute to reluctance in using HYV with their costly inputs.
There is little doubt that the use of HYV during kharif season, using nitro-
gen fertilizer, could substantially increase rice production in Orissa; but
it is understandable that farmers are unwilling to take risks - such as
applying expensive nitrogen fertilizers which may be washed away by heavy
rains - unless some way can be found of reducing them. One way is to develop
improved cultivation practices during kharif. Only through the use of such
measures, developed in applied and adaptive trials convincingly demonstrated
to farmers by the extension service in contact farmers' fields, will it be
possible to overcome the reluctance of farmers to use HYV during kharif.
20.       Major constraints, preventing OUAT from conducting effective re-
search, have been the severe shortage of funds for research, and lack of ade-
quate facilities, staff and effective cooperation with Extension. The problem
is compounded at the main research station because all staff have dual re-
sponsibility for teaching and research. Since lecturing commitments are
high, it seriously affects the amount of time which can be spent on research,
which tends to be discipline-oriented and related to higher academic degree
requirements. At the outlying stations, staff are devoted full time to re-
search, but most of them have temporary appointments with restricted operating
funds severely limiting what they can do. There is also inadequate coordina-
tion of research between the disciplines in the Agriculture Faculty, as well
as between the research stations, and with CRRI and other national and inter-
national institutions. Emphasis needs to be given to regional problems, with
proper linkage between applied research, conducted at the research stations,
and adaptive research on selected farms in each of the 13 Districts of the
State. The establishment of a Coordination Unit for this purpose within
OUAT is needed, which could also collect and generate research information.
II. AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH PROGRAM
A. General Description
Program Goals
21.       The project would strengthen applied and adaptive agricultural re-
search to provide practical answers to farmers' problems so that they can



ANNEX 4
Page 7
increase agricultural productivity in Orissa. It would focus on the key
commodities, which include rice (above all) and other cereals, pulses, oil-
seeds, fibers, sugarcane and important horticultural crops. It would concen-
trate on developing improved cultivation practices with high-yielding vari-
eties, which would be acceptable to farmers in kharif under different agro-
climatic regimes of the State. It would also involve research on improved
farming systems and cropping patterns to increase farmers' income. Research
would be conducted on a multidisciplinary team approach. All staff would be
permanent appointees of OUAT devoted full time to research, with only incidental
teaching. Four applied research stations would be supported in different agro-
ecological regions which are in the four main agricultural areas of the State.
In addition, 13 adaptive research farms would be used, one for each District,
to test out agronomic practices in each locality with new varieties developed
at the applied research stations of OUAT or elsewhere. Through this research
effort, in support of and combined with Extension, crop production should
increase substantially, and in five years' time kharif rainfed rice should
increase from the present (1975) average yield of 0.76 tons/ha to 1.5 tons/ha
and irrigated rice from the present 1.28 tons/ha to 2.0 tons/ha.
22.       The four applied research stations would include two existing ones
at Bhubaneswar and Chiplima and two new stations as follows:
Area (ha)    Station                              Region
246      Bhubaneswar                 Coastal Zone
80      Chiplima                    Central Plateau - Table Land
100      Simliguda                   Eastern Ghat - Highland Zone
117      Keonjhar                    Northern Plateau - Undulating Land
23.       The adaptive Research Farms in the 13 districts would be:
Farm Area

1.  Barchana                   Cuttack                    15
2.  Sakhigopal                 Puri                       26
3.  Balia                      Balasore                   16
4.  Golanthara                 Ganjam                     16
Central Plateau Table Land

6.  Chakuli                    Sambalpur                  22
7.  Mahisapat                  Dhenkanal                  40
8.  Phulbani                   Phulbani                   26
Northern Plateau - Undulating Land
9.  Samakhunta                 Mayurbhani                 28
10.  Keonjhar                   Keonjhar                   20
11.  Sundargarh                 Sundargarh                 20



ANNEX 4
Page 8
Eastern Ghat - Highland Zone
12.  Simliguda                 Koraput                   804
13.  Arkabali                  Kalahandi                  75
Program Components
24.       In order to achieve the goals outlined above, the program would
have the following components:
(a) civil works at three outlying applied research stations
including staff quarters and a 24-bed dormitory;
(b) research farm development at all applied research stations
and some adaptive research farms;
(c) equipment, furniture and vehicles;
(d) increase in permanent professional and support staff
at stations and farms; and
(e) establishment costs of the project during a five-year
period.
Details of the investment to be made are in Annex 17 Tables 1-6.
B. Detailed Features
Applied Research Stations
25.       The four stations would work on those crops most important in their
respective agroecological regions as follows:
(a) Cultivation Practices. Techniques which improve crop
productivity would be emphasized for study. These
would include land preparation, time of planting in
relation to rainfall patterns, spacing, alternative
farming systems and cropping patterns, soil amend-
ments (organic and inorganic), pesticide and herbicide use.
Improved water management practices and more effective
utilization on dryland farming would also be studied.
(b) Varietal Improvement. Plant breeding, screening, iden-
tification and selection of varieties useful to farmers
would be intensified for factors relevant to the different
agroclimatic regions, seasons, water levels, soil types
and resistance to various pests and diseases.



ANNEX 4
Page 9
(c) Pest and Disease Control. Studies woud be accelerated
on pests and diseases which seriously affect crop pro-
duction in Orissa. Particular emphasis would be placed
on rice pests, especially gall midge but also stem bor-
ers, leaf hoppers, blast and tungro virus. The epidem-
iology, prophylactic measures for control in nursery
beds and rice fields, chemical control where necessary,
and screening for resistant or tolerant varieties would
be undertaken in joint programs by the entomologists, plant
pathologists and crop breeders at the stations.
(d) Economic Studies. Economists-Statisticians at each of
the stations would evaluate the various cultivation prac-
tices, alternative farming systems and cropping patterns
developed in the studies outlined above. The economic
impact of the various recommendations would be assessed
before extension personnel were given the improved
practices and/or new crop varieties to present to
farmers in the region.
Adaptive Research Farms
26.       The main function of the Adaptive Research Farms would be to test
out, under the existing local conditions of each of the 13 districts, the rec-
ommendations from the Applied Research Stations before recommending their use
to Extension and farmers. Recommendations would include improved cultivation
practices, alternative farming systems, cropping patterns and improved vari-
eties. Results obtained would be evaluated by the Economist-Statistician at
the Research Coordination Unit in Bhubaneswar.
C. Organization and Management
27.       The research programs outlined above would be the responsibility of
OUAT Dean of Research. To assist him in ensuring a multidisciplinary ap-
proach and to effectively coordinate research at the applied research stations
and adaptive research farms, a Research Coordination Unit would be established
at Bhubaneswar. Each of the three Applied Research Stations in the outlying
regions would be headed by an Associate Director of Research, directly res-
ponsible to the Dean of Research.
28.       Technical programs for each of the stations and adaptive research
farms would be formulated for the kharif and rabi season in consultation with
the concerned scientists. An agreed program would then be discussed in a
working group, headed by the Dean of Research and the heads of each major



ANNEX 4
Page 10
discipline and the Associate Directors of Research. The research programs
would then be discussed at State level in half-yearly meetings, presided over
by the Dean, with representatives of Extension. Programs adopted at these
meetings would then be put into practice. The results of research for each
year would be compiled in a technical report and circulated to all concerned
and would serve as the basis for recommendations by Extension to farmers.
29.       Each of the Applied Research Stations and Adaptive Research Farms
would have adequate field, laboratory and staff quarters facilities to carry
out a viable farmer problem-oriented research program.
Extension Relationships
30.       Relationships between research and extension would be substantially
improved. In order to foster closer linkages between these two important
groups, and so benefit the farmers, the adaptive research farms would be on
Government-operated farms. The Research Officer and Senior Research Assistant
supplied by OUAT to each farm would be responsible to the Range Deputy Director
of Agriculture. The Research Officer would design, lay out and operate the
field trials on the farm. He would also supervise any trials in the district
and advise extension staff on their layout. He would, however, look to the
Research Coordination Unit for guidance and supply of information and materials
regarding trials on the adaptive research farm.
31.       Research workers at the Applied Research Stations and Adaptive Re-
search Farms would be actively associated with training sessions held at the
State, Range and District levels. The three outlying Applied Research Sta-
tions would each have a 24-bed dormitory to permit periodic visits of
extension personnel to provide for a mutual information exchange of the
latest findings and farmers' problems with crop production.
Collaborating Institutions
32.       The Applied Research Stations would maintain close collaboration
with national research organizations, such as the Research Institutes of ICAR,
and especially CRRI at Cuttack, AICRIP, and ICRISAT at Hyderabad, and with
key research programs at Universities and Departments of Agricultures con-
ducted in other States of India. Collaboration would be encouraged with such
international research organizations as IRRI in the Philippines, Asia Vegetable
Research Center at Taiwan and CIMMYT in Mexico, and with research in countries
such as Australia, USA, Japan, Israel and USSR.
D. Staffing
33.       The staff at the four Applied Research Stations at full development
would comprise 41 professionals and 165 supporting staff. Proposed staffing



ANNEX 4
Page 11
at the four stations is shown on page 12. The 13 Adaptive Research Farms
would have a total of 13 Research Officers, 13 Senior Research Assistants
and 13 Junior Research Assistants supplied by OUAT, with the support staff
supplied by the Department of Agriculture.
III. COST ESTIMATES
34.       Estimated costs of the agricultural research component excluding
contingencies, would be approximately Rs 44.1 M (US$4.9 M). Details are given
in Annex 17.

ORISSA - AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Farm Implements
A. Background
Introduction
1.        Much of the early benefit from an intensified agricultural extension
program would stem from the adoption of improved cultural practices, particu-
larly techniques to achieve more timely planting, increased plant population,
better moisture conservation and effective weed control. The use of improved
farm implements, particularly replacement of the wooden country plow with an
iron or steel plow, would help successful adoption of these practices.
2.        The country plow requires several passes to achieve even partial
soil inversion which results not only in poor weed control, but also inefficient
use of man and bullock hours. While a case can be made for selective mechan-
ization, the vast majority of farmers (93% of holdings are below 5 ha and 43%
below 1 ha) will continue for many years to rely on bullock power to achieve
any improvement in the cultivation system.
3.        A considerable amount of valuable work has been carried out by Central
Rice Research Institute (CRRI) and the Directorate of Agriculture and Food Pro-
duction (DAFP) in research, development testing and production of animal drawn
equipment. As a result, proven and competitively priced implements are avail-
able on a limited scale, manufactured by the DAFP Agricultural Engineering
Section in Bhubaneswar. The biggest impact has been made by the steel plow
and at present there are estimated to be about 80,000 steel and cast iron
plows, the latter manufactured privately - in use in the State, or about 3% of
the total.
4.        Other implements which have been produced and distributed on a
limited basis include a disc harrow, two-row seed-drill, three-tyne culti-
vator with seeding attachment, hand weeders, and low lift hand pump. How-
ever, though these improved farm implements, particularly the plow, have
been under development and limited production for some years, many farmers
away from the main centers have not even seen an improved plow. At present,
these implements are primarily in use in the coastal areas and in small
pockets where limited demonstrations have been run by DAFP.



ANNEX 5
Page  2
Development, Manufacture and Demonstration
5.        Under the Joint Director of Agriculture (JDA) Engineering, the
DAFP implement manufacturing unit handles development and manufacture of
implements. In addition the Agricultural Engineering Section has a unit for
demonstration of farm implements and water management techniques, operating in
25 of the 314 administrative blocks. Section staff includes three executive
engineers, thirteen assistant agricultural engineers and forty-four mechanical
or agricultural engineering supervisors.
6.        In 1975/76, the DAFP manufacturing unit, managed by an assistant
engineer and run on two shifts, produced implements valued at Rs 0.36 M in-
cluding about 5,500 mould-board plows. The unit about breaks even financially.
Annual capacity could be increased on the present site, with a small addition
to equipment, to a turnover of about Rs 0.9 M, including 14,000 plows. Through-
put has been constrained by lengthy government requisitioning procedures for
materials, but these are being streamlined to avoid undue delays in the
future.
7.        The Industrial Development Corporation (IDC) Hirakaud Industrial
Workshop, in Sambalpur District, has also satisfactorily produced a small batch
(500) of mild and carbon steel plows to DAFP specifications. The workshop is
concerned mainly with fabrication of items such as electric transmission
towers, but has the capacity to expand production of plows in its foundry. One
of several private entrepreneurs in Orissa sold about 6,000 cast iron mould
board plows in 1975/76. These sell at about Rs 22 compared with Rs 32 for the
steel plows; but they wear more quickly.
Distribution
8.        Distribution of the small number of improved implements manufactured
each year is presently handled primarily by DAFP through Block offices, but
also through village stores and by the Agro-Industries Corporation (AIC),
(Appendix 1) which distributes through its branches and agro-service centers.
Private entrepreneur sales are concentrated in the coastal areas and, overall,
improved farm implements are not readily available in the State nor is there
an established distribution chain.
9.        Prices of the plow and main items of DAFP manufactured equipment ex-
works are presently: steel plow Rs 32; three-tyne cultivator Rs 91; seeding
attachment for country plow Rs 7; blade weeder Rs 16; two-row seed drill Rs
247; seed treating drum Rs 146. Prices include 6% sales tax and at present
all farmers receive a subsidy of 10% on purchases of the plow; small and
marginal farmers benefttting under the Small Farm Development Agency are
able to receive subsidies of 25% and 33-1/3% respectively.



ANNEX 5
Page 3
B. Role of Farm Implements in Improved Agricultural Practices
Contribution to Improved Cultural Practices
10.       Improved implements provide the key to better weed prevention
through better initial soil inversion, more timely land preparation operations,
and simplified line sowing, which facilitates weeding.
11.       Land Preparation.  Use of the improved steel plow for land prepar-
ation leads to lower cost per hectare and improved timeliness of opera-
tion. Time for land preparation under dry land conditions is reduced by 50%,
one plowing with the steel plow and two disc harrowings (or use of the
wooden plow) replacing 4 to 5 operations with the wooden plow. Similarly,
under wet land conditions one pass with the steel plow, followed by two
passes with a comb harrow, will replace five passes with the wooden plow. As
a result, time is reduced from 16 to 8 days per hectare, cost is reduced by
about 40% and sowing or transplanting date can be advanced as much as 20
to 25 days with consequent important improvements in yield:

12.       Sowing.  Introduction of a simple seed tube and funnel attached to
the wooden plow provides a means for line sowing, enabling easier weed control
and achieving considerable saving in seed of 20% to 30%. Apart from the
agronomic advantages of line sowing, there is also a considerable saving in
time. Use of the two row seed drill presently under development will provide
further savings, and increased efficiency:

13.       Weeding.  The combination of effective plowing and line sowing
should lead to the abandonment of the present practice of cross plowing
("behushoning") broadcast rice, which results in a serious reduction in plant
population. Hand pulling of weeds is often avoided at present but avail-
ability of simple hand weeders, although requiring some labor input, shouild
encourage weed control where presently not undertaken.
On Farm Costs and Benefits
14.       Total investment in improved farm implements per farm is small in
comparison to expected benefits; the annual cost, including capital repayment
and interest and repairs and maintenance, being equivalent to 130 kg of paddy
on a one or two-hectare farn and 510 kg on a five-hectare farm:

15.       Farm budget projections for farms taking up the package of practices
including purchase of farm implements demonstrate that there will be a sub-
stantial incentive for farmers to participate. Medium term (three years)
loan facilities would be required to assist farmers purchase the full range
of equipment, though initially most sales would be plows alone and the small
amounts required could be financed as part of short term credit.
16.       In the case of a 2 ha rainfed farm after all debt servicing, net
farm income would r[se from Rs 1,500 to about Rs 4,000 at full development.
After home consumption, cash income would rise steadily from Less than Rs 200
to over Rs 2,000 per year (Table 1).



ANNEX 5
Page 6
Expected Demand
17.       The main requirement for improved animal drawn implements will be
on the 1.6 M holdings in the 1-4 hectare range and the 0.3 M holdings between
4-10 hectares. Above this, farmers are likely to take up full mechanization.
The ultimate requirement for improved farm implements has been estimated
crudely from farm census data. Estimates of crop areas categorized according
to elevation, sowing method and whether irrigated or rainfed, have been
compared with data for implement capacity and then adjusted with farm size
distribution data. On this basis it is estimated that there is a potential
demand for some 700,000 improved mould-board plows, 1.4 M hand weeders,
630,000 simple seeding attachments, 300,000 three-tyne cultivators with
seeding attachments, 40,000 seed drill and 340,000 disc harrows (Table 2).
In addition, some export demand from other States would be expected. It is
difficult to say, however, at what rate actual demand would develop.
18.       Within four years some 43% of kharif paddy land could be under im-
proved cultural practices. After allowing for land area under full mechani-
zation and a 50% uptake of improved plows by "eligible" farmers some 40,000
plows a year would need to be sold to meet this requirement (Table 3).
C. Project Actions
19.       The project would support the design, development, manufacture and
distribution of the animal drawn farm implement industry to meet farmers'
needs in improving cultural practices through:
-    establishment of a research and design unit at the College
of Agriculture and Engineering Technology, OUAT;
-    expansion of production at the DAFP manufacturng unit;
-    intensification of the DAFP demonstration activites in
support of the reorganized extension service;
-    creation of a sound distribution network under the aegis
of AIC which would also contract with corporate (e.g. IDC)
or private entrepreneurs for the manufacture of implements
required in excess of DAFP capacity.
Research and Design (OUAT)
20.       Objectives and Responsibilities.  With improved cultural practices
to be stimulated on a State-wide basis, there is urgent need to establish a
research and design unit which will provide a pipeline of improved implements
suitable for a wide range of soil conditions. At the same time, there is a
need to develop equipment for a range of other jobs such as foodgrain harvest-
ing, water lifting development of structures for irrigated area development,
and low volume, sprayers for herbicides.



ANNEX 5
Page 7
21.       The College of Agricultural Engineering and Technology, OUAT, would
be responsible for fundamental research and design of new implements, in close
liaison with DAFP and CRRI. Responsibilities would cover:
(a) Improvement of implements being recommended for large-scale
adoption, particularly: design improvements to achieve better
performance in the mould-board plow, under heavy land conditions,
and selection of materials for improved performance at lower
cost.
(b) Testing and development of other implements in design and
prototype stage, particularly: evaluation of disc harrow
for secondary cultivation under dry conditions and for puddling,
development and modification of seeding devices; further work
on paddy weeders; development of grain and pulse winnowers;
water lifting devices; prefabricated structures for use in
water management works, particularly for the Irrigation Com-
mand Area Development (CAD) program under which structures
such as channel lining, drops and diversion boxes are required.
(c) Evolving alternative machines and implements suitable for
field needs, particularly: design of equipment for fertilizer
placement; design of a steel plow for conditions where inversion
of soil is not important and of alternative cultivators; design
of implements for crops such as sugar cane, potatoes, cotton
and maize; development of suitable threshers.
22.       Organization.  A research and design unit would be set up under a
Chief Research Engineer with two Research Engineers and two Assistant Engineers.
The unit would be located at the OUAT, College of Agricultural Engineering
and Technology in Bhubaneswar. The Chief Engineer would be responsible to
the Dean of the College.
23.        Project Cost.  Under the project, support would be provided for:
-     purchase of workshop equipment required over and above
the present complement and a 4W-D vehicle for use in field
testing; total cost Rs 265,000 (US$29,000); Annex 22 table 1.
-     five years' staff and operating costs estimated at Rs 1.43 M
(US$159,000); Annex 22, table 2.
Manufacture and Demonstration (DAFP)
24.       Objectives and Responsibilities.  DAFP would retain responsibility
for, initially, a major share of production and development and demonstration
of implements across the State. Once private entrepreneurs take up production
of bulk items, DAFP would concentrate on manufacture of more sophisticated
equipment.



ANNEX 5
Page 8
Manufacture and Demonstration (DAFP)
25.       Manufacture.  Throughput of the existing DAFP manufacturing unit at
Bhubaneswar would be stepped up to about 14,000 mould board plows per annum,
which is about the capacity of existing buildings and management. A 120-ton
power press would be provided to improve manufacturing efficiency.
26.       The DAFP manufacturing unit would be strengthened by setting up a
sub-unit to develop implement manufacturing processes. In addition, proto-
types would also be manufactured for distribution to the adaptive research
farms, where field tests and trials would be conducted. This unit would be
headed by an experienced agricultural engineer and would have two assistant
engineers. The project would also include additional testing equipment, to
supplement existing workshop facilities, and staff and operating for five
years.
27.       It is expected that demand under the project would build up to the
order of 50,000 plows a year. Requirements over and above DAFP production
would, initially, be manufactured by IDC to specifications produced by DAFP.
Ultimately, private entrepreneurs would be expected to take up manufacture.
AIC, which would assume responsibility for distribution (para 33), would
place contracts with IDC and private entrepreneurs and would be responsible
for quality control.
28.       IDC is financed by commercial banks refinanced by the Industrial
Development Bank of India (IDBI) and would require no additional financing
under the project. Private entrepreneurs would be able to obtain finance
through the State Finance Corporation (SFC) which has adequate funds
available.
29.       Demonstration.  The DAFP mobile demonstration units would be ex-
panded and regrouped to cover the 13 agricultural ranges proposed under the
project. The demonstration units would provide support to the extension
service in demonstrating to farmers the role of the implements in the im-
proved cultural practices recommended as part of the package of practices,
see Annex 2. The demonstration units would follow a systematic program in
close liaison with the extension service, and each unit would be expected to
conduct 150 demonstrations a year, covering about 30,000 farmers per agri-
cultural range per year.
30.       Each of the 13 units would be equipped with a demonstration van and
with a complete set of basic implements, as well as sophisticated implements
in the first phase of introduction. The demonstration personnel would consist
of one Agricultural Engineering Supervisor (a graduate in Agricultural Engineer-
ing), one fitter and one helper, in addition to the driver of the vehicle.
Audiovisual aids consisting of a film projector, speech equipment and accesso-
ries would be included. The unit would work closely with the Subject Matter
Specialist in farm machinery, at range level, to backstop extension workers
and participate in field training.

Page 9
31.       Project Cost.  The project would include the following:

32.       Additional short-term working capital finance, estimated at
Rs 537,000 (US$60,000), of which about 70% would be for raw materials, would
be provided from GOO funds.
Distribution (AIC)
33.       AIC would take primary responsibility for marketing and distribution
of the implements and would, according to regularly updated estimates of
demand, place contracts with manufacturers for production. Specifications
would be prescribed by DAFP, and AIC would impose quality control.
34.       Sales outlets.  Sales would be made through a number of different
outlets including:
-    DAFP; block headquarters and demonstration units
-    AIC; ten branch offices and agro-service centers
-    Cooperatives; 59 regional cooperative marketing societies
and 3,300 primary service cooperative societies
-    Private retailers
Some 40% of sales would be expected to be handled through DAFP and AIC outlets
and 60% through cooperatives and the private sector.
35.       Organization.  AIC would appoint a qualified engineer with production
and marketing experience to place manufacturing contracts and exercise quality
control. He would be assisted by an implement engineer. Existing AIC sales
staff would be strengthened with three assistant commercial managers appointed
to be based at the three regional offices -- Cuttack, Berhampur and Sambalpur.
Existing Regional Managers would supervise distribution within their zones
with the help of these assistant Commercial Managers to the various outlets.



ANNEX 5
Page 10
36.       Implements would be transported from Bhubaneswar (DAFP), Hirakaud
(IDC) and Cuttack (private entrepreneurs) by AIC and hired trucks. Working
capital required for setting up the distribution organization would be pro-
vided by AIC equity and commercial bank loans.
37.       Pricing.  Incremental costs of AIC procurement, quality control and
distribution is about 5% of annual ex-factory cost (about Rs 5 M at full
development). A total mark-up of 15% would be charged to cover distribution
cost and commission to final outlet. Accordingly, prices for commercial
distribution would be increased, the plow retailing at Rs 37 compared with
Rs 32 at present (Table 4). Demand is expected to expand rapidly as demons-
tration develops and revised prices are not likely to restrict uptake.
Organization and Management
38.       There is already close liaison between the three institutions to be
concerned with implement design, manufacture and distribution, but to ensure
formal coordination a committee would be set up to oversee the farm implement
component. This committee would be chaired by the JDA, Agricultural Engineering
and would comprise: from OUAT, the Dean of the College of Agriculture and
Chief Engineer, Design Unit; from DAFP the Engineer in charge of Manufacture;
from AIC, the Production/Marketing Engineer.

Ground Water Surveys and Monitoring
Short-term Action Program
1.        A joint exercise between the Government of India (GOI), Government
of Orissa (GOO) and a team of World Bank and FAO/IBRD Cooperative Programme
staff in September 1975 identified a Short Term Action Program (STAP) with
the primary objective of increasing Orissa foodgrain production and rural
incomes. With a view to ameliorating the adverse effects of erratic rainfall
and extending multiple cropping, the largest STAP component was ground water
development. This provided for construction of 105,000 dug wells, 15,000
simple private tubewells and 3,000 public tubewells and would result in
ground water irrigation for an additional 105,000 ha, beyond the approxi-
mately 50,000 ha now irrigated in this way. The approximate cost would be
Rs 895 M (US$98 H1).
2.        A subsequent Working Group formed by the Agricultural Refinance.
and Development Corporation and GOI to appraise Orissa ground water resources
proposed to change the program to 200,000 dug wells, 15,000 simple private
tubewells and 2,250 public tubewells. This would result in ground water irri-
gation of an additional 295,000 ha, beyond that now so irrigated, at an
estimated cost of Rs 1,180 M (US$130 M). An integral part of STAP, though
not included in the Agricultural Intensification Project, this development
would be financed by ARDC under a general line of credit currently available
from IDA (Credit 540-IN).
3.   Ground water development on this scale would increase the annual extrac-
tion of ground water by an estimated 240,000 ha m (1.95 M ac-ft). The present
level of development results in an annual extraction of approximately 100,000
ha m (800,000 ac-ft). Longer term projections contemplate an annual draft
approaching two million hectare meters.
4.        To assure the orderly development of the ground water resource and
avoid over-exploitation in local areas, a five-year program of investigational
surveys and monitoring has been included in the Intensification Project.
Objectives
5.        The Orissa Lift Irrigation Corporation (OLIC) has made reconnaissance
level surveys of ground water resources available in the State of Orissa. More



ANNEX 6
Page 2
detailed investigation is proposed, under the project, to implement proposed
ground water development so as to maximize the use of the resource and facili-
tate long range planning. A more immediate objective is to gain specific know-
ledge of ground water conditions in local areas in order to provide for an
orderly and phased development. Finally, there is a need to monitor the
effects of such development. The scope of the proposed survey and monitoring
activities to be conducted over a five-year period, and the objective of the
proposed program, are described in the following paragraphs.
Surface Hydrology
6.        Collection and evaluation of rainfall and runoff data are essential
to assess the availability of ground water recharge. Instrumentation would
be installed at 92 stations throughout the State by which measurements of
rainfall and runoff would be obtained and recorded.
Ground Water Levels
7.        No comprehensive program of ground water level measurements is
presently being implemented. It is proposed to install 65 specially de-
signed monitoring wells through the State (Map 12339) to determine seasonal
and annual fluctuations of the water table or, in the case of confined
aquifers, the pressure surface. It is proposed to install automatic water
level recorders in many of these wells to facilitate the compilation of con-
tinuous records. Monitoring well site selection is based upon preliminary
identification of ground water storage units which individually exhibit sim-
ilar subsurface geological conditions and water level fluctuations.
Well Inventory
8.        An inventory of existing wells is essential to provide a basis for
evaluation of the effects of project implementation. Data to be compiled
would include well locations, dimensions, drilling logs, pump horsepower,
area served and quantities of ground water pumped. A knowledge of the charac-
teristics of existing wells would allow certain of those wells to be incor-
porated into a ground water level monitoring program.
Planning and Evaluation
9.        A fundamental requirement in formulating a plan of massive ground
water development of this nature includes overall evaluation of the poten-
tial level of development which can be sustained from area to area. The most
efficient and least costly type of well, the intensity and the spacing of
wells are important elements to be considered. Investigation of special
problems, such as development of coastal aquifers threatened by saline water
intrusion, would constitute an ongoing activity.
Quality
10.       Although not originally proposed by GOO as a specific element of
the survey and monitoring program, a comprehensive surface water and ground



ANNEX 6
Page 3
water quality monitoring program would be included. Laboratory determin-
ations of all major mineral constituents, electrical conductivity and pH
would be made. A large number of existing water wells would be included
in an initial survey to establish a pre-project "threshliold cnliOOs'
lesults of this activity would be evaluated and a long range monitoring pro-
gram, reduced in scale, would he formuLated.
Basic Data and Records
11.       Data collected from implementation of survey and -mornitoring pro-
grams would be recorded and filed in a manner which is readily retrievable
and available for use.  A ceril:ral data bank at each of four regional head-
quarters *o0ti.J be established.
Implementation
12.       Though OLIC has a ground water survey cell capable of carrying out
a resource survey program, there is no organization capable of properly moni-
toring the effects of the  proposed ground water development. In Annex 23
is a detaLled itemization of equipment and staff together with estLmaied
costs, proposed for execution of a ground water survey aad monitoring
program.
Equipment and Mlaterials
13.       The major equipment required(  includes two drilling rigs with sup-
porting transport.  A conventional heavy duty rotary rig would be purchased]
i(!o construct exploratory and monitoring wells in alluvial, are-as and a second,
percussion-type, rig would be employed to install wells in weathered and
fractured crystall 1-U rock areas.
14.       Also included would be sufficient casi.ng, welding equipment and
other materials to install 30 deep wells in the coastal plain and some 35
wells in the erosiLonal plain. Other items include electrical logging
equipment, metering and recording devices, laboratory  v,hLig apparatus
for determination of the mineral quality of water, surveying instruments,
field camp facilLties and general office furniture, typewriters, calculators,
printing machines, etc.
Staff
15.       The present andl proposed organization of OLIC is shown in Chart
16240.  As indicated previously, the existing staff and facililties are
generally adequate to perform the proposed survey activity. It is pro-
posed to add two new monitoring divisions, at Balingir and Bhubaneswar,
each headed by a hydrologist, and six sub-divisions, each with an Assistant
Hydrologist.  Office and field staff, drillers and laborers numbering some
160 individuals are included in the compleoient.

Cost
16.       Financing for ground water surveys and monitoring would be provided
to cover the costs of incremental staff salaries, purchases of equipment and
materials, and installation of 65 observation and monitoring wells. Itemized
costs for each component of the project are given in Annex 23.
17.       The total cost to conduct the ground water surveys and monitoring
program during the three-year period is estimated to be Rs 16.6 M, including
approximately Rs 7.9 M for capital expenditures and Rs 8.7 M to cover pro-
jected incremental operating costs.
18.       The proposed expenditure of Rs 16.6 M represents slightly over
one percent of the estimated cost of Rs 1,180 M for ground water development
proposed. Such an expenditure for further investigation and for formulating
and implementing an ongoing monitoring program is reasonable and completely
justifiable in view of the long term benefits, and possibly adverse effects
which might otherwise occur. Early initiation of the proposed surveys
and monitoring is an urgent priority to assure the orderly and efficient
implementation of the planned ground water development.

INDIA
ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Shallow Tubewell Technology
1.        The planned development of Orissa ground water resources over the
next five years envisions installation of some 200,000 dug wells. It has
been demonstrated that, although the initial capital investment is relatively
low, the cost of producing water from dug wells is the highest of several
alternatives in terms of cost per acre and cost per ac-ft produced. Dug wells
are nevertheless valuable and economically justified for individual farmers
in many areas.
2.        A small number of successful shallow filter point wells has been
installed in Orissa and, under favorable geological conditions, this type of
well is cheaper than a dug well. A filter point well is a variation of the
conventional tubewell in that it is hand-drilled to depths of up to 80 feet,
and may utilize locally available materials. For example, a coir filter,
consisting of a hemp-type wrapping around a bamboo frame, may be used for
the screen and fabricated bamboo pipe employed in lieu of steel casing.
Objectives
3.        The basic objective of the proposed effort requires the research,
design, installation and field testing of alternative types of shallow tube-
wells which can be made available to individual farmers at savings in cost
over present methods of ground water recovery. The development of low cost,
efficient pumping equipment is also a primary goal.
4.        The activity, to be conducted over a three-year period, would in-
clude: (a) evaluation of locally available, less costly material for casing
and screens; (b) review of general design criteria for shallow tubewells;
(c) delineation of areas within the State suitable for installation of low
cost shallow tubewells; (d) investigation and review of the level of tech-
nological development in other countries; (e) design and testing of modified
pumping equipment, including engine driven pumps and hand pumps susceptible
to being driven by animals; and (f) installation and operation of some 96
test and demonstration wells incorporating the findings derived from the re-
search and evaluation program.



ANNEX 7
Page 2
Implementation
5.         The Orissa Lift Irrigation Corporation (OLIC) has been assigned the
responsibility for implementing the development of this improved shallow well
technology. OLIC has staff with the administrative and technical capability
to conduct a program of this scope with a few additions.    Equipment and
materials necessary to carry out the program would be included in the Inten-
sification Project.
6.         Annex 24, Table 2 details required equipment and materials, the
costs thereof, and a proposed Implementation Schedule over a three-year
period is shown in Annex 29. Included are tubewell components, pumps, trans-
port, hand-boring sets and miscellaneous tools and testing instruments.
Materials for fabrication of a prototype manually-operated pump are also
included.
7.         Required staffing is listed in Annex 24, Table 3, as are the other
components of operation and maintenance.   Staff would include two professional
engineers and 15 technical and general office personnel. Also included, within
the operation and maintenance function, would be activities associated with
the installation and operation of test wells and the maintenance of vehicles,
pumps and drilling equipment.
Cost
8.         The Project would include the costs of equipment,
materials, staff and operation and maintenance. The estimated costs, both
annual and total for capital and operation over a three-year period, are
detailed Annex 24 and summarized below:

INDIA
ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Input Distribution in the Cooperative Sector
I. Input Supply, Distribution and Storage
A. Introduction
1.01      Present use of inputs in Orissa is very low, fertilizer use
averaging only 9 kg/ha (all-India average 14.8), and only some 5% of kharif
paddy is under improved seeds. Agricultural cooperatives handle an estimated
60% of fertilizer distribution with the other 40% sold through private re-
tailers. It is estimated that only about half the villages are served by
private retailers so that, in many outlying areas, the agricultural coopera-
tive is the only retail outlet.
1.02      In many cases, neither cooperatives nor private retailers are well
equipped for the distribution of fertilizers (or other inputs) either in terms
of technical knowledge and training, or the storage, transport and working
capital required. GOI price controls, with fixed distribution margins, also
act as a severe disincentive to handling of fertilizer, particularly in remote
areas.
1.03      Problems arising from the inherently weak distribution system have
been compounded by a high level of credit overdues by cooperative society
members resulting in reduction of turnover as members become ineligible for
production credit to buy inputs. Rescheduling of some short term debts and a
good 1975/76 harvest should reduce this problem in 1976/77; but the real
answer is a firmer stand against overdues and a strengthening of cooperative
training as proposed under the project.
1.04      The Directorate of Agriculture and Food Production (DAFP) is pri-
marily responsible for the production and, through Block headquarters, the
distribution of improved seed. Seed processing facilities are rudimentary.
Neither cooperatives nor the private sector supply farm implements or chemi-
cals (other than fertilizer) on a large scale and most farmers do not yet
have ready access to them.



ANNEX 8
Page 2
B. Fertilizers
Present Situation
1.05      Supply.  Orissa, with a recorded output of 66,000 tons of fertilizer
nutrients (N and P) in 1974/75, is already a net exporter to other states and
capacity is being increased. Investments, to remove bottlenecks in Hindus-
tan Steel's Orissa Plant at Rourkela and increase production of ammonia, are
being made under the Bank's Fertilizer Industry Credit VII. This will raise
total capacity to 125,000 tons nitrogen (N). In addition, in the second half
of 1977, the Fertilizer Corporation of India is due to begin commercial pro-
duction of urea at a new plant at Talcher, which will have a rated capacity
of 495,000 tons of urea (220,000 tons N). The Orissa State Marketing Co-
operative Society also operates a granulating plant at Bargarh, producing
about 12,000 tons of compound fertilizer a year from imported materials.
1.06      Availability of fertilizers is not, therefore, expected to be a
constraint in Orissa. In particular, if the Talcher plant comes into produc-
tion on schedule, production of nitrogenous fertilizer, which the project
will most stimulate, will be ample within the State. However, India as a
whole is expected to be in deficit on fertilizer nitrogen, certainly into the
1980's, so that there is likely to be a ready demand for Orissa's production
from other States.
1.07      Utilization in Orissa grew rapidly between 1964/65 and 1973/74 at
about 24% per annum to reach 63,000 tons nutrients, but fell in 1974/75 due to
price increases and bad weather. Phosphate (P) and potash (K) consumption
continued to fall in 1975/76 though nitrogen (N) recovered slightly. In 1973/74
consumption averaged 8.6 kg/ha, but with a considerable variation in unit
application between districts and season (Appendix 1, Table 1). In 1975/76
consumption averaged 6.8 kg/ha comprising 5.1 kg N, 1.0 kg P, 0.7 K per
cropped hectare.
1.08      Farmgate Prices.  For all practical purposes, GOI controls fertil-
izer prices. It exercises statutory control over the prices of urea, ammo-
nium sulphate and calcium ammonium nitrate and indirectly influences phos-
phate and potash prices. A sharp rise in 1974, from Rs 2.5 to Rs 4.6 per kg
N, severely checked consumption. At present prices - Rs 4.1 per kg N, from 16
April 1976 - there is not a strong incentive for farmers to apply the recom-
mended biological levels. Assuming an average response of 10 kg paddy per
1 kg N applied, against the market price, the benefit: cost ratio fell from
3.6:1 to 2.0:1 in 1974 and in March 1976 was about 2.4:1. Against the pro-
curement price of paddy, the ratio fell to 1.5:1 in 1974 and in 1975/76 is
1.9:1. At very low rates of application, as will be the case in parts of
Orissa, response rates will be higher, but any further decline in the grain:
fertilizer price would make it difficult to realize the full potential of the
project.

1.09      Distribution.  Since 1966, the fertilizer manufacturers have built
up a system of wholesale and retail distributors in the private sector. This
network does not handle imported fertilizers, which are supplied entirely
through the cooperatives. In 1974/75, the private sector accounted for about
half the quantities manufactured within India. In Orissa, there are now
around 240 wholesale dealers and 2,100 retailers, operating as approved dis-
tributors, licensed by DAFP. The agricultural cooperative system has about
3,300 retail sale points.
1.10      The Fertilizer Corporation of India (FCI) with over 500 retailers,
has the largest private distribution network in Orissa and maintains a Re-
gional Office at Bhubaneswar. FCI efforts to increase fertilizer sales in



ANNEX 8
Page 4
the State were backed by a budget of Rs 1.8 M for extension and promotional
work in 1975/76.
1.11      GOI controlled distribution margins, a further disincentive to the
expansion of fertilizer uptake, are regarded as an important issue and are
dealt with separately in Section III.
Fertilizer Use with the Project
1.12      Demand Projections.  Projections for fertilizer use through 1983/84,
developed as a basis for estimating input storage and distribution needs, have
been derived from appraisal mission estimates of: uptake of package of
practices (Annex 2) and cropped area in 1979/80 and 1983/84; see Appendix 2.
The data base was not adequate for derivation of trends but experience from
the Rajasthan extension project indicates that reoriented and intensified
extension should have a marked impact on input demand.
1.13      Consumption of nitrogen and phosphate is projected to increase at
about 15% per annum from the average 1973/74 - 1975/76 level, potash at about
9%. After allowing for some increase in fertilizer concentration, uptake of
fertilizer in total product tons is projected to increase as follows:

1.14      Additional Distribution.  Planning for storage and distribution
would need to be one year in advance of estimated consumption and in 1982/83,
is estimated at 515,000 tons or an increment of 324,000 tons on the peak year
(1973/74) to date. It is expected that some 50% of the incremental amount
would be handled by cooperatives and would be supported under the project; in-
vestment needs are summarized in Appendix 4.
C. Seed
Present Situation
1.15      Supply.  In the absence of any organized seed industry in the State,
DAFP is the main source of improved seeds, procured mainly from departmental
farms under its direct control. With an area of 2,700 ha in 85 Departmental
Farms spread throughout the State, DAFP has produced 3/4,000 tons of seeds
per year, of which 70-80% has been paddy. DAFP also procures seed from farm-
ers, and in abnormal years, when the demand is very heavy, seeds have been
procured from outside the State through the National Seeds Corporation
(Loan No. 1273-IN) and Terai Development Corporation (Bank loan 614-IN).



ANNEX '
Page 5
1.16      Of the total quantity of paddy seed procured from all sources and
distributed for the 1975 kharif crop, 38% was supplied from DAFP farms,
42% purchased from cultivators and 20% procured from other agencies. Some
6,000 tons of paddy seed were distributed in 1975/76 - four times the previous
year, due to drought and loss of stock in 1974/75.
1.17      At present there are no proper processing facilities available in
the State under Government control, though one private producer has installed
capacity for 800 tons/year. The Government now has funds for the purchase of
six small processing plants, also with a rated capacity of 800 tons. Two
have already been procured and will be set up in Sambalpur, the main producing
district. The seeds produced in the DAFP farms and procured from State farm-
ers are generally sold uncertified, seed certification for cereals having only
been introduced in the rabi season of 1974/75 on a pilot basis.
1.18      Breeder seed for paddy is obtained from the Central Rice Research
Institute (CRRI) and Orissa University of Agriculture and Technology (OUAT).
Breeder seed for potato is obtained from Central Potato Research Institute
and for jute from the Central Jute Research Station. Pulse breeder seeds are
produced by DAFP, but arrangements for other crops have yet to be finalized.
Foundation seed production, of crops for which breeder seeds are available,
has been started by DAFP but there are no facilities for proper processing,
bagging, etc., and seed testing facilities are also inadequate.
1.19      For paddy, suitable varieties for both rainfed and irrigated con-
ditions, developed both at CRRI and OUAT and adapted to conditions in Orissa,
are available. Already about 75% of the irrigated rabi rice crop is under
improved HYV varieties. On the other hand, in the kharif season only about
5% of the land is under improved varieties. However, the main reason for
slow uptake is failure to demonstrate to farmers that improved varieties, for
both gall midge resistance and tolerance of varying water levels, are avail-
able. Support to the further development of suitable varieties will be given
through the research component (Annex 4) but enough suitable varieties are
already available to warrant gearing up the supply system.
1.20      Distribution.  Distribution of improved seeds is presently organized
through Block headquarters. Block Development Officers (BDO) in consultation
with Village Level Workers (VLW) present an indent to the District Agricul-
tural Officer (DAO) who is responsible for organizing production on the dis-
trict seed farm and arranging for procurement of supplies through Bhubaneswar
(Seeds are sold from Block headquarters or distributed direct by VLW).
Improved seeds multiplied up by farmers, but not subject to any government
control, are also distributed through interchange between farmers, often on
a barter basis.
1.21      To provide for better seed distribution in Orissa, the National
Seeds Corporation has started to build up a network of distributors in the
State and has already establfshed 150 private dealers. Under the all-India
Programme, it is the Corporation's policy to license 4 dealers per block; a
total of 1,256 for Orissa. Both cooperatives & private traders are eligible



ANNEX 8
Page 6
for appointment, provided they are competent to handle the business. The DAO
have been instructed to identify sufficient enterprises to increase the State
coverage to one distributor per block in 1976.
1.22      Pricing.  Certified and uncertified seed prices for paddy, composite
maize and jowar and for wheat are fixed by DAFP, Bhubaneswar. Other prices
are fixed by DAO and allow for full recovery of seed farm costs plus transport
and handling charges. Certified paddy seed prices have been reduced over the
last three years, falling from Rs 207 per quintal in 1974/75 to Rs 200 in
1975/76 and to Rs 150 for 1976/77, compared with a 1976/77 procurement price
for non-seed paddy of Rs 78. Seeds are subsidized to all farmers, the rate
of subsidy in 1975/76 varying from Rs 46.10 per quintal (23%) for paddy to
about 40% for maize and jowar.

not be so much on HYV varieties as on improved cultural practices, including
seed treatment and germination testing prior to sowing. Tentative estimates
for uptake of HYV paddy, composite maize, wheat and other seeds have been
made on the basis of package of practice uptake (Annex 2).  Uptake of HYV seed
is expected to be as follows:

1.24      Supply.  Orissa is expected to participate under a later phase of
the GOI/Bank assisted National Seeds Program, seed production under the Orissa
State Seeds Corporation (SSC) could begin in rabi season 1977/78. The proposed
SSC would handle only paddy, pulses and potatoes; any requirements of seeds
not covered by its own output would be procured through the National Seed
Corporation.

D. Farm Chemicals
Present Situation
1.25      Much of the technical material for farm chemicals is imported, hut
the product formulation takes place mainly within the country. Indian manu-
facturers and formulators include private, cooperative and state companies,
each with its own dealer organization. There are seven formulators in Orissa,
furnishing a small part of the State's total supplies. In India, as a whole,
however, there is excess capacity and there should be no difficulty in meeting
prospective Orissa demand. Wholesale and retail prices of pesticides and
herbicides are fixed by the manufacturers, in agreement with GOI.
1.26      In Orissa, pesticides and insecticides are sold through private
dealers, who also handle fertilizer, and through cooperatives. To encourage
use of chemicals by small and marginal farmers, GOO makes handsprayers avail-
able to them at subsidies of 25% and 33-1/3% respectively.
Demand and Supply under the Project
1.27      Demand.  Under the project, demand for pesticides and insecticidles
is expected to expand quite rapidly, with the emphasis that will be given to
seed treatment and general crop protection. Expenditure is expected to in-
crease to about Rs 60 M by 1979/80 and Rs 120 M by 1983/84, with cooperatives
and private dealers sharing the market about equally.
II. The Agricultural Cooperative System
A. Present Situation
Overall Structure
2.01      The sector of the agricultural cooperative system concerned with
input distribution and( the marketing and processing of agricultural products,
has a three-tier structure:  The Orissa State Cooperative Marketing Society
(OSCMS) - the Apex Society; 59 Regional Cooperative Marketing Societies (RCMS);
and 3,352 Primary Cooperative Societies (PCS).  Input distribution is closely
linked to provision of institutional short term credit (Annex 10). GOO
guides and controls the operatLon and development of the cooperative movement
through the Registrar of Cooperative Societies (RCS) and his staFF, respon-
sible to the Secretary for Agriculture and t.C>)peration.
Role of National Cooperative Development Corporation (NCDC)
2.02      Financial assistance and advice to the Cooperatives, particularly
in the construction of storage facilities and provision of trarisp,)rt, have



ANNEX 8
Page 8
been given by the National Co-operative Development Corporation (NCDC).
Founded in 1962 and financed by GOI and GOI guaranteed bonds, NCDC engages
throughout India in the planning and promotion of cooperative programs for
production, processing, marketing, storage, export and import of agricultural
produce and notified commodities.
2.03      For cooperative storage, NCDC provides State Governments with
75% of the approved cost, 50% as a loan (currently over 14 years @8% with
three years' grace) and 25% as a grant. The State Government, in turn,
passes on the funds either as share capital directly or as a loan and grant
through the Central Cooperative Bank to the participating cooperative. In
Orissa, GOO passes on the funds as share capital and provides the balance of
25% also as share capital.   NCDC issues detailed guidelines for construction
and the Registrar of Cooperatives, through the Central Cooperative Banks,
supervises construction and disbursement on behalf of the State Government.
For RCMS vehicles, the arrangements are generally similar except that there
are no grants for vehicles (except in Tribal areas) and the RCMS must raise
the remaining 50% (25% in Tribal areas) from its own or borrowed funds.
B. Strengthening of the Cooperative Distribution System
2.04      To achieve the full potential of project benefits, input supplies
must be readily available to farmers who will need them to respond to the more
advanced extension recommendations (see Annex 2). The necessary fertilizers,
chemicals and seeds will be available within the country, but the capacity and
efficiency of the distribution systems within the State are lacking. They
will need to be built up. One view is that farmer demand, following extension
recommendations, would bring pressure to bear on GOO and the cooperative
system to provide the necessary inputs; they may be so in the early years of
the project, but it is equally likely, and a risk to be avoided, that if no
action is taken, farmers would ultimately be frustrated by lack of inputs and
at least part of project potential benefit would be in jeopardy.
2.05      Assistance to cooperative distribution would need to cover:
-    construction of additional storage for primary
cooperative societies (PCS).
-    construction of additional storage and provision of
trucks for regional marketing societies (RCMS).
-    provision of additional training for RCMS and PCS staff.
The additional storage and trucks will be provided under a separate all-India
cooperative project which is being prepared by NCDC. The training will be
supported under the present project.



ANNEX 8
Page 9
III. Fertilizer Prices and Distribution Margins
Background
3.01      For all practical purposes the Government of India (GOI) controls
fertilizer prices. It exercises statutory control over the prices of urea,
ammonium sulphate and calcium ammonium nitrate. Phosphatic fertilizers are
not subject to this control, but since GOI recently granted a subsidy of
Rs 1,250 per ton of P205 to the manufacturers they, in turn, undertook not
to change their prices without GOI consent. For all imported fertilizers,
the Central Fertilizer Pool establishes a ceiling selling price to cul-
tivators, known as the "Pool Price." For all other fertilizers, princi-
pally compounds, prices are, in principle, not controlled. In order to be
competitive on a nutrient basis with fertilizers that have fixed prices,
however, the price freedom of local manufacturers is limited.
3.02      Since it is necessary to maintain a parity between the selling
prices of imported and domestically manufactured fertilizers, government
controlled prices are intended to reflect, not only the production costs of
the local industry, but also an equalization factor to arrive at a "pool"
price for both local and imported supplies GOI price policy also takes account
of demand factors and attempts to prevent prices becoming excessively high.
The declared pricing policy of the Pool, however, has been one of no profit
and no loss. In fact, until the rapid rise in international fertilizer prices
in 1974, the pool made a profit; but at the peak of international prices the
government subsidy on, for example, imported urea amounted to nearly Rs 2,000
per ton. Another example of this intervention is the reduction in prices of
superphosphates and potash fertilizers in December 1975 to give a special
boost to consumption of these products. The reduction in the price of single
superphosphate was achieved through a reduction in the excise duty from 15 to
7.5%, and maintained through the introduction in March 1976 of the subsidy of
Rs 1,250 per ton P205 (Rs 200 per ton SSP), as mentioned in para 3.01.
3.03      Fertilizers are substantially taxed.  There is a central excise tax
of 7.5% on superphosphates and 15% on all other fertilizers. A Central Sales
Tax of 4% is levied on fertilizers purchased interstate, and a further retail
sales tax is charged, varying from state to state. In Orissa it is 6%. There
is also a small local road tax (octroi), usually about 1%, on fertilizers
carried into a municipal area. The excise duty is included in the fixed re-
tail price of fertilizer; the central and state sales taxes are in addition to
it. Some locally produced fertilizers are also charged a pool equalization fee
by the government to arrive at the pool price (para 3.02). For urea this is
at present Rs 165 per ton. The different levies and charges by GOI, the
Government of Orissa (GOO) and municipalities can therefore add up to about
35% of the retail price for a fertilizer like urea at the present time.



ANNEX 8
Page 10
3.04      In order to fix a ceiling retail price, GOI calculates the equated
Free on Rail (FOR) Destination cost for all fertilizers it controls and makes
an allowance for the average distribution costs, or margin, from the railhead
to the farmer. The theoretical breakdown of this margin for urea, as recom-
mended by various committees between 1965 and 1976, is shown in Table 1 which
shows that, though the urea ceiling price more than doubled, as did most
costs, the margin increased relatively little.
3.05      A fixed fertilizer distribution margin creates problems and dis-
incentives at the wholesale and retail levels of the fertilizer distribution
system. These disincentives are aggravated if the distribution margin is
inadequate. Both Cooperative and private wholesalers and retailers complain
that the fertilizer margin is too low and the profit made on fertilizers not
attractive. The Orissa State Cooperative Marketing Society actually showed a
loss on its fertilizer operations in 1974/75. Since the dealer's enthusiasm
for selling fertilizers is an important force in promoting increased fertilizer
use, his complaints about the profitability of this business should not be
neglected. The major problems connected with the present distribution margins
and a few realistic solutions to them are shown below:
