Transportation
3.06      In Orissa the bulk of private fertilizer dealers are clustered
around the railheads and few serve-the farmers in more distant areas. The
apparent unwillingness of fertilizer dealers to incur more than minor trans-
portation costs is a direct result of the fixed margin and the ceiling on
retail prices. The higher the transportation cost, the more the dealer has to
take out of his profits from the fixed margin. The total transport allowance



ANNEX 8
Page 12
in the present urea margin, for example, is Rs 9.20. At present freight
rates, this is only enough to move fertilizers of about 20 km. Though some
dealers might be prepared to accept a somewhat reduced profit and move fer-
tilizers further to meet (or avoid) competition, the margin does not allow
them to move very far before the profit is reduced to zero. This makes it
difficult for farmers in remote areas to obtain fertilizers and certainly
does not promote their use.
3.07      As shown above, one solution would be to allow free pricing of
fertilizers at all levels. However, so long as India continues to run a
deficit in fertilizers, expected to last into the 1980's for nitrogen, there
may not be enough competition between local manufacturers and distributors to
assure competitive pricing, Conversely, if low cost imports were allowed to
compete freely with locally produced fertilizers, then the high cost domestic
manufacturers would not always be able to compete. A consequent substitution
of imported for locally manufactured fertilizer would lead to an economic loss
for India. It is therefore not realistic to assume that GOI would accept free
pricing for all fertilizers.
3.08      The second possible solution would be for government to subsidize
fertilizer transportation to distant areas. This is already done to some
extent. In Orissa, in 1975/76, transportation to tribal areas was subsidized.
A new request for such subsidies has been made for 1976/77. Widespread adop-
tion of this policy is not very attractive, however, for several reasons:
it is difficult and costly to administer, it invites fraudulence and it is
expensive.
3.09      The best solution is to exclude the allowance for transport in the
fixed margin and allow the dealer to charge the statutory retail price plus
his costs for transportation from the railhead to his delivery point. To
avoid unnecessary handling, and provide an incentive for cost savings in the
distribution system, the handling allowance should be kept in the fixed margin.
Although this would make fertilizers marginally (maybe up to 5%) more expensive
to remote farmers, at least it would make them available which often, now, they
are not. In fact, in certain places in India (e.g., Mysore), an addition for
transportation is already made. The way of legally getting around the inter-
diction to exceed the ceiling price is to bill transportation on a separate
invoice. GOO should investigate the possibility of a legal introduction of
such a scheme, in anticipation of a GOI change in its policy on fertilizer
margins.
3.10      A control is not necessary on the amounts charged for transportation
if the dealer is required to display the amount he bills in the same way he
displays the fertilizer prices. Competition would then help to keep transport-
ation charges to a minimum. Should this be unacceptable to the government,
the transportation cost that the dealer is allowed to bill could be annually
fixed, based on actual costs, when the dealer renews his license and he should
be required to publicly reveal what it is. All dealers in one location would
thereby charge the same amount for transportation.



ANNEX 8
Page 13
3.11      In order not to penalize distant areas, fertilizer rail-transporta-
tion costs in India are now pooled to give a uniform FOR wholesaler's.des-
tination price. This could be improved upon by pooling all transportation
costs to give a uniform price for fertilizer delivered to wholesalers. This
would allow dealers and farmers far from a railway to buy fertilizers from
wholesalers at a price similar to that close to a railway station. The Indian
Railway has indicated that the only way to meet the rapidly increasing trans-
portation requirement for fertilizers in the future would be through "single
or very limited destination single directional block rate movement." This
would considerably reduce the number of rail destinations and therefore make
a pooling of fertilizer transportation costs to the wholesaler's store even
more necessary. Such a scheme would be beneficial, whether or not dealers are
allowed to charge transportation costs in addition to the fixed ceiling price.
Under the present fixed margin system, it would allow wholesalers and retailers
to move closer to' "distant" areas and reduce'the need for government trans-
portation subsidies. The combined introduction of a price equalization to
the wholesaler's store and of a surcharge for transportation would allow the
retailer to move even farther into "distant" areas and further reduce the
need for government subsidies. The introduction of both schemes is therefore
recommended.
Storage
3.12      The fixed margin, together with the lack of off-season rebates
makes the efficient fertilizer distributor turn over his stock as-quickly
as possible. He stringently avoids storing for longer than he has to, and
place orders with manufacturers only just before the fertilizer season, which
puts an unnecessary strain on the distribution system and often results in
fertilizer arriving too late at its destination. In planning fertilizer
requirements, GOI now includes a provision of 15% of forecast consumption as
a buffer stock and 10% as a pipeline requirement. Moreover, to assure the
timely supply of imported fertilizer, plans have to be made for early arrivals.
All this will result in larger fertilizer stocks at the central level (pool,
manufacturers). As a result, GOI recently issued directives to the cooper-
atives to store six months of their requirements, but provided no extra
incentive for them to do so.
3.13      It is important that there be sufficie:it incentive down the distri-
bution chain to encourage early orders for stocks. The Fertilizer Corporation
of India (FCI) recently introduced such an incentive by providing interest free
credit, two months for cooperatives and one month for private dealers. How-
ever, though thi.s allows the dealer to keep a stock for one or two months
without cost, he can still enjoy free credit without increased stocking and has
no direct incentive to hold more than he can dispose of within this time. Off-
season rebates would more positively encourage early ordering and get locally
produced fertilizers away from the factories down the distribution channels
into dealers' stocks; similar arrangements could be made for pool fertilizer.
This would be preferable to government subsidies to cooperatives for early
stocking and is the recommended solution. However, any compensation for



ANNEX 8
Page 14
storage should be related to the amount of fertilizer handled and not a
support to the budget, the deficit on which is related to all activities of
the cooperative.
3.14      The amount of the off-season rebate discount clearly must be large
enough to cover storage, including capital, costs. In Orissa, the cost per
ton/month of renting stores is reported to be Rs 2.60 at the Central Ware-
housing Company and Rs 2.80 at the State Warehousing Company. With an interest
rate of 14.5%, the approximate monthly rebate necessary to cover storage costs
would be about Rs 25 for urea (1.3% of the retail price) and Rs 15 for ammonium
sulphate (1.6% of the retail price). By comparison, the average seasonal dis-
count offered in many European countries is between 1.0-1.5% per month for
fertilizer ordered earlier than the period of application. The discounts
should be passed on through the distribution chain down to the farmer, thus
reducing the need for large warehouses at the wholesale and retail level.
Adequate off-season rebates are therefore the recommended answer to encourage-
ment of stock holding.
Dealer Incentive and Size of the Margin
3.15      The role of the well-motivated dealer in promoting fertilizer use
is crucial. Comparative studies of fertilizer distribution in a large number
of countries, undertaken by the OECD and the FAO, stress the need for adequate
money incentive to dealers and its importance for the promotion of fertilizer
use among farmers. 1/ The fertilizer margin in India, particularly in Orissa,
clearly does not provide the required incentive. The recommendations above
would correct some of the imperfections to the present fixed margin. The ques-
tion, however, remains: what size of margin would give the required incentive
to dealers, keeping in mind that, except for a reduction in taxes, any increase
in margin would increase farm-gate price. If demand for fertilizers is price
elastic, then there clearly is a trade-off between increased dealer enthusiasm
and promotion and reduced farmer demand. Theoretically there should be an
optimum; but practically, the size of the incentive required remains a matter
of judgement.
Recommendations
3.16      Recommendations on the size of the distribution margin for urea
and ammonium sulphate are presented in Table 2. The assumptions are shown in
the table, and margins for all other fertilizers could readily be calculated,
using the same assumptions. As can be seen, an increase of the cooperative
urea margin from Rs 115 to Rs 190, and of the private urea margin from Rs 100
to Rs 150, is recommended. Actual transportation costs would be charged
separately and additionally. The new margins amount to 11% and 9% respective-
ly of the FOR destination price. The basis for the estimates is detailed
below.
1/   Eric 0 de Guia, A Comparative Study of Fertilizer Distribution Systems,
Development Centre Studies, OECD Paris 1972, and FAO Fertilizer Industry
Advisory Committee, The Scope for Improving Fertilizer Marketing and
Credit Systems in Developing Countries, Rome 1974.



ANNEX 8
Page 15
3.17      For storage costs, the average rate reported for the State and
Central Warehousing Corporations, Rs 2.80 ton month, was used. Because of
a government directive to cooperatives to store six months of their require-
ments, it was assumed that each ton would be stored for six months, as all
committees on fertilizer margins had recommended earlier. For private trade,
a storage allowance for three months should be sufficient. To the extent that
it is desirable that fertilizer be purchased by the distributors well in
advance of the season, off-season rebates should be offfered, as in para
3.14.
3.18      Reported loading and unloading costs varied from Rs 1 to Rs 3.5/
ton. Rs 2 was a commonly reported figure, and has been taken as typical.
The number of such handlings usually varies from three to five, depending
on whether the retailer picks up directly at railroad or at wholesaler's
store; four was taken as the average. Four months, interest is taken into
account for cooperatives, the remaining two months being financed through
suppliers' interest free credit. Three months' interest is taken into ac-
count for the private trade (para 3.17). The prevailing interest rate is
about 14.5% per annum. Losses of only 1% have been allowed, for though
cooperatives claim about 2%.
3.19      All Government Committees which have made recommendations on fer-
tilizer margins, have allowed 1% commission to the wholesaler and 2.5% to the
retailer. GOI accepted these margins earlier when fertilizer prices were lower,
but fixed them on a lower price basis and failed to increase them pari passu
with the increase in fertilizer prices (and other costs). The result is that
the distributor now must put up about twice as much capital to earn the same
amount from fertilizer trade as before. It is generally accepted that there
is a relationship between capital investment and risk-taking on the one hand,
and profit on the other. The margin allowed for commission is now too small,
based on pre-1973 prices, to allow for a dynamic distribution and to induce
promotional effort. Although Indian fertilizer manufacturers spend some funds
on promotion of farmer fertilizer use, for reasons of goodwill, no major
promotional effort can be expected as long as India runs a deficit in ferti-
lizers and manufacturers are assured of sales.
3.20      Based on recent (1974/75) increases in fertilizer prices, a whole-
sale commission of 1% and a retail commission of 2.5% would be more acceptable,
especically if actual transport costs were substitute for a fixed sum. At
present prices, the small distributor in distant areas would be given a
better chance of survival, on the same percentage commission, since fertilizer
price increases generally have also increased monetary turnover. The alloca-
tion of only a total 3.5 percent in commission, to distributors is acceptable,
and it is strongly recommended that GOI allows 3.5% in commission as it did
earlier; but on the actual, not an assumed lower price, to allow for monetary
costs. In the overall worldwide fertilizer shortage that prevailed in recent
years, it was understandable that GOI did not feel it was necessary to promote
the use of fertilizer and was more concerned about keeping the price down.
Now, however, in 1976 fertilizers are again coming into ample supply worldwide
and it is demand that needs stimulation.



ANNEX 8
Page 16
3.21      The distribution of the commission between the Apex, RCMS and SCS
in Table 2 is roughly according to present practice. The final split should
be left to the cooperatives themselves.
3.22      An extra incentive commission is worth considering for those ac-
tually responsible for the sale of fertilizers in the cooperative sector, at
farm level - generally the secretary of the primary cooperative. Data, from
past trials in India, verifies that the introduction of a special commission
per bag sold has increased cooperative retail sales effort. At present,
the poorly paid secretary often leaves his store to mind other more profitable
businesses, and the farmer, who may have travelled a long way to buy his
fertilizer, often finds the store closed. To induce a more active sales
effort, it is proposed that the secretary be paid 50 paisas (Rs 0.50) per bag
of urea (50 kg) or ammonium sulphate (100 kg) he sells. To avoid too strong
promotion of low grade fertilizer, the incentive commission for other fertiliz-
er materials should be related on a nutrient basis. The secretaries could be
told that such a scheme would be introduced on a trial basis and discontinued
if not successful.
The Case for Increased Margins
3.23      The case for increasing fertilizer margins in India is strong.
International comparisons are difficult, but India's are among the lowest
fertilizer margins in the world.  Out of 24 countries for which data is
available, India ranks fourth lowest for retail, wholesale and total margins,
excluding transport costs.  If transport costs are included, India has the
lowest margin of all countries compared. If costs of transportation are ex-
cluded, India's marketing margin, taking the more favorable figures for the
cooperative sector, is 6% and 7% of retail prices for urea and ammonium
sulphate respectively. The average percentage for the 22 countries studied,
is 15 and the median 13.5. The proposed new margins, 11% for urea and 9%
for ammonium sulphate, would still keep India well below these two averages.
The argument for increased margins is reinforced by the economic fact that,
should they be too high, competition will tend to result in discounts from
the retail price. This happens already near railheads where turnover is high
and retailers sell below ceiling prices to meet hard competition. They can
do this through savings in transport costs with full loads over short dis-
tances and by picking up fertilizer directly from the railhead.
3.24      If GOI desires to avoid the increase in retail prices that would
result from increased margins, small though such price increase would be,
this could be done by a reduction in the pool equalization charge, which is
still levied on domestically manufactured fertilizers, even though they are
now more expensive ex-factory than imported fertilizers ex-ship. Thus the
Rs 75 and Rs 50 increase proposed for the cooperative and private sector urea
margins could be offset by a similar reduction in the pool equalization charge
of Rs 165 on urea.

INDIA
ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Land Records and Land Survey
I. Introduction
A. General
1.01      The overall short term action program (STAP) for development of
foodgrain production in Orissa included rapid expansion of ground water
development, medium and minor surface flow irrigation and a program of command
(irrigation) area development (CAD). A large part of the ground water develop-
ment program, prepared by the Agricultural Refinance and Development Corpora-
tion (ARDC) (Annex 6), would be financed in the private sector by cooperative
Land Development Banks and by commercial banks. In granting medium and long-
term loans for irrigation and other development, the financing institution
requires confirmation that the land to be irrigated or developed is owned by
the borrower and not already mortgaged, or encumbered.
1.02      At present, both the granting of loans and command area development,
requiring an accurate record of land holdings, are constrained by:
-    lengthy procedures in the search of records for issuing
non-emcumbrance certificates;
-    outdated land records due to lack of recent survey and, in
some districts, failure to update records as transfers
take place;
-    lack of up-to-date village maps showing ownership of
individual plots.
These constraints stem in part from the historical origin of the State and
the time that has been needed to combine a multiplicity of different land
holding laws.
1.03      The Province of Orissa was formed on 1st April 1936 with territories
taken from the then provinces of Bihar, Orissa and Madras and the Central
Provinces where, in all, three different land revenue, tenancy law, and admin-
istrative systems were in operation. Furthermore, a vast tract, particularly
in the territories that came over from the province of Madras, had not been
subjected to any cadastral survey at any time earlier and no reliable land
records were available. Subsequently, with the merger of 24 feudatory States
during 1948 and 1949, the State of Orissa acquired its present territorial
jurisdiction, with a total area of about 155,000 km and, with it, a further
diversity of laws and systems of land revenue administration. In all there
were some 32 different land tenancy laws at the time of merger. Available
land records were neither uniform nor properly preserved and maintained, and
survey data was in most cases not accurate.



ANNEX 9
Page  2
1.04      After Independence, it became apparent that the extensive survey and
settlement 1/ operations needed, would be very difficult under the prevailing
multiplicity of Tenancy Laws. Accordingly, the Orissa Survey and Settlement
Act was passed in 1958 to unify land record procedures. Survey and settlement
operations are governed by the provisions of this Act and the Orissa Survey
and Settlement Rules, 1962.
B. Basic Land Record
Record of Rights
1.05      The basic land record is the record-of-rights or "patta" record-
ing all land held by the occupant. It shows: (a) name and class of each
tenant and/or occupant; (b) situation and extent of the land held by each
tenant or occupant, and the name of each proprietor or landlord; (c) rent
and charges for irrigation payable by each proprietor, landlord, tenant
or occupant; (d) use and source of water for agricultural purposes and respon-
sibility for the repair and maintenance of works for supply of water for the
cultivation of land held by each proprietor, landlord, tenant or occupant; (e)
special conditions or incidents, if any, of the tenancy; (f) any right of way
or other easement attached to the land; (g) if the land is claimed to be held
rent-free, whether or not rent is actually paid; if not paid, whether or not
the occupant is entitled to hold the land without payment of rent and if so
entitled, under what authority.
1.06      In principle, a land survey operation is conducted approximately
every thirty years to update the land records in the State.  Between such
land survey operations, if transfers of land occur, the copy of the record-of-
rights which is held in the Tahasil, or revenue sub-district office, is
updated by the revenue officer, or Tahasildar. 2/  The other copy is held
by the land owner who, on selling or mortgaging the land, registers the
transfer or mortgage, or any other transaction, e.g. gift to a relative, in
the land registration office and hands over the Ipatta' to the new owner.
Importance of up-to-date Land Records
1.07      Accurate and up-to-date land records are of considerable im-
portance to an overall development program for stepping up agricultural
production in Orissa, particularly for: providing security of tenure to
cultivators; obtaining medium and long term institutional credit; and in
implementing land reforms and development of irrigated areas.
1/   In this case settlement refers to the "Settlement of Tenancies"; that
is settlement of the land revenue that the land holder will pay to the
Government of Orissa as the ultimate owner of the land.
2/   The State is divided into 13 revenue, or administrative districts, and
105 Tahasils.



ANNEX 9
Page 3
1.08      Security of Tenure.  An authentic land record is essential to
protect the rights of cultivators, for which various land reform measures
are being implemented and in particular, to protect the rights of tribal
tenants and the weaker sections of the rural population. Cultivators'
rights are properly secured only when they are recorded in the record-of-
right and these records are used for settlement of all disputes over land.
1.09      Conversion of share-croppers to full ownership.  The records
are also required for share-croppers who, where the holding of the owner
is above 3 standard acres have the right to take over the holding on pay-
ment of five times the fair rent. Since 1965, under the Orissa Land Reforms
Act of 1960, subsequently amended in 1973, some 217,000 have availed of this
opportunity and an estimated further 100,000 share-croppers are eligible.
1.10      Acquisition of land for general infrastructure, irrigated area
development and land consolidation. Up-to-date records are clearly essential
for acquisition of land required in development schemes or in the improvement
of water use in command (irrigated) area development (CAD). The updated
village map, prepared following completion of the survey operation (see para
2.02) provides the basis for land consolidation work. The Land Consolidation
Division in the Department of Revenue and Excise started work in 1973, follow-
ing the passing of "The Orissa Consolidation of Holdings and Prevention of
Fragmentation of Land Act 1972." Surveys are under way in 1,338 villages in
six districts and, by March 1980, it is expected that 400,000 ha will have
been consolidated, mainly in irrigated areas. The complete procedure of
consolidation takes about three years, if the consolidation team also has to
update the land records. If, however, the area has already been surveyed and
land records updated within three years, the lapse time is reduced by six
months.
1.12      Land ceiling law and land for landless families.  Records are
necessary for implementation of the ceiling law for agricultural lands
under which ownership of land is limited to 10 standard acres (18 in the
case of joint families). They are also needed for the identification of
surplus land available for landless families.
1.13      Land Revenue.  As part of the survey operation, the level of
land revenue to be paid annually and the responsibility for it, are de-
termined. As a result of completing the present survey operation, land
revenue assessment is expected to be increased from Rs 14 M to Rs 50 M
per year.
C. Institutions Responsible
1.14      Two separate divisions within the Department of Revenue and Excise
are responsible for: (a) the thirty-year land survey to update records of
rights, and (b) the registration of land transfers and mortgages. Land survey
is the responsibility of the Commissioner, Land Records and Settlement. Regis-
tration is the responsibility of the Inspector-General of (Land) Registration.



ANNEX 9
Page 4
A third division, under another Commissioner, is responsible for implementation
of land reforms and land consolidation works.
II. Land Survey
A. Background
Institution
2.01      The Land Survey Division has a full-time Director responsible to
the Commissioner. Six Settlement Officers are responsible for land records
and settlement work and a Deputy Director for Survey and Map printing. Survey,
record and settlement work is carried out from camps in the area under survey,
each led by an-Assistant Settlement Officer.
Methodology
2.02      Land survey and settlement requires three distinct operations:
-    cadastral survey;
-    preparation of Record-of-Rights;
-    settlement of land revenue.
These operations may be taken up independently or in two or three stages
simultaneously. The village is the basic unit of administration for the
cadastral survey. After detailed plotting, a complete map of the village
is prepared, and the plots numbered serially for the purpose of 'Khanapuri'
that is, preparation of preliminary Record-of-Rights. Subsequently, plots
are checked, tentative land revenue assessed, and records made available for
scrutiny and objection by all concerned. The whole process takes about three
and a half years.
Progress of Work
2.03      A program for intensive operation was started in 1958 to complete
survey and settlement by the end of 1970/71. However, shortage of funds
prevented achievement, and the position on 1st May 1976 was:
No. of villages        Percent
Completed                 22,749                  44
Under Survey               23,324                 45
Not started                 5,836                 11
Total                  51,909                 100

Page 5
2.04      It is estimated that Rs 142.5 M (US$15.8 M) would be required to
complete operations by 1983/84.

B. Project Actions
2.06      In view of the wide importance of updated land records, the project
would support a program to bring forward completion to 1981/82. This would
require an increase in number of working teams (camps) as follows:

Additional Staff Requirements
2.07      Senior and Junior Supervisory Staff.  Existing Senior Supervisory
staff should be able to handle the additional program, but Additional Junior
Supervisory staff below the Survey (Settlement) Officer level would be re-
quired.  Six more officers of the State Administrative Servtio    r            vttid be
required from 1977/78 onwards to work as Charge Officers (camp supervisors) to

supervise field work and the strength of the Assistant Survey (Settlement)
Officers would need to be increased by 156. This would be met by promotion
within the organization and deputation from State Administration Cadres.
2.08      Technical and Field Staff.  There should be no difficulty in securing
the services of additional technical staff. Some 6,300 trained hands are
available, of which only 2,900 have been able to find employment under the
Settlement Organization at present. About 1,000 have been employed under the
Land Consolidation Division and other Government departments. The 1,500 addi-
tional hands required in 1977/78 and 1978/79 would be found from the balance
of 2,300 unemployed persons. Similarly, there should also be no difficulty
in securing the services of 5,000 unskilled laborers to assist the field staff.
Additional Vehicles and Equipment
2.09      Vehicles.  Six vehicles would be purchased under the project and the
best possible use would be made of the existing 21 vehicles after repairs.
2.10      Equipment and Instruments.  Available survey instruments and camp
equipment are adequate to meet the normal requirements of 260 camps but, with
an increase in the number of camps in 1977/78, additional tents, camping
equipment and tarpaulins, would need to be purchased at a cost of Rs 10,000
for each camp, together with additional survey instruments.
2.11      After winding up the survey operation, staff and operational vehicles
and equipment would be transferred to the Land Consolidation Division.
C. Project Cost
2.12      Detailed cost tables are in Annex 21.
III. Registration of Land Transfers and Mortgages
A. Background
General
3.01      In obtaining medium and long-term institutional credit, the certi-
ficate of freedom from encumbrance has to cover a minimum of 13 years. While
copies of all instruments involving land transactions (mortgage, gift, etc.)
are registered in the Sub-Registration Offices and kept in the Record Rooms of
Registration Offices, year by year, grant of a non-encumbrance certificate for
any plot of land involves search from the Copy Book Registers of 13 years.
This may take up to six days of a clerk's time and the present delay in



ANNEX 9
Page 7
issuing a non-encumbrance certificate is anywhere up to one month. With a
large ground water program to be implemented and a stepping up in the aid to
small and marginal farmers, there is an urgent need to speed things up.
3.02      A fast retrieval system for non-encumbrance certificate involves
two steps: First, modification of the present recording (index) system of
land transfers, mortgages, etc; second, transfer of the details of documents
to a simple card index unit.
Unification of Indexing Procedure
3.03      There are two systems of indexing in the State, one in all districts
except Ganjam and Koraput, and a slightly different system in those two dis-
tricts.
3.04      Unification of indexing under an improved system has been taken up
in all the districts from 1st January 1974. But the purpose of granting
non-encumbrance certificates covering 13 years cannot be served unless the
indexing is completed retrospectively and then a card index system introduced
to speed the search process. About 430,000 land transfers or other land
transactions are registered each year. To cover 13 years thus involves some
5.6 M documents.
Introduction of Improved Card Index System
3.05      An improved card index has been introduced in Dhenkanal district
as a pilot scheme, and is working well. Particulars relating to a registered
document are copied out on cards, by year; these cards are fitted into pockets
and retained in steel trays in a lockable cabinet. With this system it has
been found that time taken in 'search' relating to any plot of land involved
in the transaction for a period of 13 years has been reduced to an hour or
less.
B. Project Actions
3.06      Under the project, funds would be provided to index all previous
land transactions under the Dhenkanal system. Existing staff are fully
occupied with normal work of registration and would not be available for
this additional work. Temporary clerical help would therefore be hired.
Adequate accommodation would be provided together with storage for indexing
equipment and furniture. Before final adoption a consultant, to be recruited
under the project, would examine and report on the Dhenkanal system and
suggest any improvements. Two man-weeks of consultancy are included.
Staff Requirement
3.07      There are adequate numbers of supervisory staff, but additional
temporary clerk copyists would be required for reindexing and transferring
data to the card index system:



ANNisA Y
Page 8
Reindexing. Additional staff would be employed on a piece
work basis to undertake transfer of 5.6 M registrations
(430,000 per year x 13 years) to the index. Two pairs of
copyists can cover about 100 documents a day, so that the
work would require 56,000 days from two pairs of copyists,
or a total of 224,000 mandays.
Transferral to Card Index. For transfer of entries to cards,
one pair of copyists can make about 100 entries a day, so that
5.6 M entries would require 56,000 days from one pair of copy-
ists, or 112,000 mandays in total.
Buildings
3.08      Requirements.  Accommodation in most district registration offices
is inadequate. Shortage of funds has led to documents being retained in other
revenue department buildings and rented accommodation, which does not provide
adequate security or facility. Basic land records are divided between sub-
registration offices of origin and the central record room. New accommodation
is therefore required at three levels:
-    14 record rooms at District Headquarters or
important local centers
-    47 rural record rooms at key towns in each district
-    2 registration offices at Sambalpar and Bolangi,
where quarters are presently shared with other depart-
ments and are wholly inadequate.
3.09      Specifications.  Rural record rooms would be constructed to a
standard specification prepared by the Public Works Department. Size is
24 ft x 18 ft x 14 ft (height) with provision for a later additional storey.
Four iron racks 3 ft x 12 ft x 12 ft with seven shelves, each 20 in wide,
would be provided from floor to ceiling. Record rooms for district head-
quarters would be double the size of rural record rooms.
3.10      Location and Construction.  Buildings would be constructed, on
land allocated by GOO, either under force account by PWD or put out to tender
by contractors according to situation and availability.
3.11      Equipment.  Card index cabinets would be required for retention
of data from about 5.6 M documents. At 10,000 documents per cabinet holding
1,000 cards, and after allowing for the distribution of documents, plus a
15% margin, it is estimated that 646 cabinets would be required. Record room
furniture and stationery required for reindexing would also be required.



ANNEX 9
Page 9
C. Project Cost
3.12      Implementation of the reindexing and subsequent card indexing is
expected to take four years. Detailed cost tables are given in Annex 21.

INDIA
ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Terms of Reference for Indexing System Consultant
Introduction
An objective of the Orissa Agricultural Intensification project is
to improve the quality and reliability of land records and modernize the
system for indexing and retrieval of information. In particular, it is pro-
posed to introduce, on a statewide basis, a card indexing system which has
been tried successfully in the District of Dhenkanal. Prior to the implement-
ation of the system statewide, a consultant is to review the system and sug-
gest any improvements.
System Review
The system review would include:
(a) defining the overall objectives of the system;
(b) ascertaining that the objectives are being achieved under
present implementation methods or recommending appropriate
changes to achieve them;
(c) technical evaluation of the system, including, inter alia:
(i) evaluation of procedures for reindexing of existing
land records;
(ii) evaluation of procedures for transferral of land
record data to cards;
(iii) review of data items to be transferred to cards;
recommend possible inclusion or exclusion of data
items;
(iv) adequacy and security of data storage facilities; and
(v) time and cost saving procedures that could lead to
greater system efficiency.
(d) detailed specifications for equipment required.
Duration: Three man-weeks



ANNEX 10
Page 1
INDIA
ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agricultural Credit
1.        Successful implementation of the Agricultural Intensification Pro-
ject will indirectly depend, to a large degree, on the provision of inputs and
the credit to pay for them. Both ate a function of the extensive cooperative
system and, to some extent, the commercial banks. Input supply is referred to
in Annex 8 and agricultural credit is covered briefly in the following para-
graphs.
2.        The Orissa Fifth Plan (1974/79) relies on the institutional credit
structure to finance the agricultural sector. It places emphasis on:
(a) strengthening credit cooperatives and the commercial
banking network;
(b) reorienting lending activities so as to provide credit,
primarily to small farmers (defined as those with net
income below Rs 2,800); and
(c) remedying the existing regional credit imbalance.
3.        Farmers in Orissa have traditional and institutional sources of credit.
The former provides, through moneylenders and merchants, 82 to 85% of farmers'
requirements. Though costly, it is simple, convenient and time-honored.
4.        Institutional sources presently provide not more than 15 to 18% of
farmers' requirements and only 3% of this comes from commercial banks, though
they are making an effort to increase their exposure to agricultural credit.
The institutional credit structure comprises: the multi-purpose cooperative
societies and cooperative banks, mainly for short-term production credit; the
land development banks, exclusively for medium and long term lending; the
commercial banks, mainly for short-term, but with some medium term lending.
5.        The Cooperative Banks, constituted under the Orissa Cooperative
Societies Act of 1962 (amended up to 1975), have a three-tiered structure:
at the top the apex Orissa State Cooperative Bank (OSCB) at State level; the
Central Cooperative Banks (CCB) at district level (17, with 84 branches);
and the Primary Agricultural Credit Cooperative Societies (PCS) at village
level (3,352). These cooperatives provide only short and medium-term credit
to their members. Short-term production credit (12 months maximum) is in-
tended mainly to finance working capital, the current expenditures in raising
crops such as: wages, power, fuel and water charges. Medium-term credit (2
to 5 years) meets outlay relating to:



ANNEX 10
Page 2
(a) replacement and maintenance of existing production
assets, with the view of maintaining the earning
capacity of the farm; and
(b) capital investment designed to increase the output
from land, such as sinking of new wells, rehabili-
tation and improvement of existing wells, installa-
tion of diesel or electric pumps, purchase of farm
machinery, sprayers, draft animals, and land level-
ling.
6.        The State Land Development Bank (SLDB), with eight branches, fi-
nances and supervises its 55 affiliated Primary Land Development Banks (PLDB)
at sub-division level, and provides exclusively long-term loans to farmers.
Annual lending for the normal program approved by the Reserve Bank of India
(Rs 30.4 M in 1974/75) was mainly for wells, pumping sets and water tanks
(about 80%), followed by land reclamation (9%) and construction of warehouses
and cattle sheds (8%). Lending under special ARDC schemes (Rs 5.7 M in
1974/75) was primarily for minor irrigation in 1974/75 (60%) and, for the pre-
vious years, almost equally shared by land reclamation and tree crop planta-
tions. The PLDB are administratively independent, but their lending policy
and criteria are fixed by SLDB, which provides almost all their financial
resources. They made long-term loans of Rs 38.9 M in 1974/75, mainly for
minor irrigation, land development and soil conservation, plantations and
farm mechanization.
7.        The four major commercial banks operate in Orissa through 345
branches (half in rural areas) and provide short, medium and long term loans,
paying particular attention to small farmers' credit requirements. Under the
"Lead Bank" system the State Bank of India has adopted six districts, the
United Commercial Bank four, Bank of India two, and Andhra Bank one. As lead
banks, they are exclusively responsible for commercial bank credit expansion in
their adopted districts. Six commercial banks have also adopted 44 PCS, but
the experience is not satisfactory. Overdues have been high (76% in 1974),
because of:
(a) wilfull tendency of borrowers not to repay;
(b) inadequate coercive action;
(c) natural calamities; and
(d) misappropriation of funds by societies' staff.
8.        Two regional Rural Banks were established under the Regional Rural
Bank Ordinance and sponsored by commercial banks, in 1976 to finance small
farmers and artisans. They are intended to combine the knowledge of rural
problems possessed by cooperative societies, with the commercial organiza-
tion and banking vigor of commercial banks. They were to be set up in



ANNEX 10
Page 3
comparatively backward areas which had a relatively poor coverage by the
cooperative credit system and commercial banks.
9.        A recent development, aimed at introducing better management by
combining smaller multi-purpose societies was the establishment of 43
large-sized Rural Multipurpose Cooperative Societies (LAMPS) providing
package services to farmers in tribal areas. These have been opened
since November 1975; 177 more are phased to be open before 1977. The
main components of the package are: short, medium and long term agricul-
tural credit; consumption credit; supply of agricultural inputs, supply
of consumer goods and marketing of both agricultural and minor forest
produce.
10.       Farmers Service Societies have not yet been established but are
proposed. Intended to provide integrated credit and service to small and
marginal farmers and landless laborers of non-tribal areas, they would be
financed by the cooperative banking system or by commercial banks.
11.       All the sources of institutional credit mentioned above are finan-
cially assisted by GOI, GOO, the Reserve Bank of India and ARDC. The Orissa
Regional Office of ARDC provides medium and long-term resources to SLDB, CCB
and commercial banks for financing, through primary societies, on-farm in-
vestments complying with its approved development schemes. Such schemes
relate to minor (mainly ground water) irrigation, land reclamation and diver-
sified lending such as fruit tree plantations, farm mechanization, dairy and
fisheries. The Reserve Bank of India has statutory control over all banking
institutions, commercial as well as cooperative. It has, particularly, the
responsibility of developing the cooperative banking structure. It has a
special Agricultural Credit Department whose functions are:
(a) to study all matters related to agricultural credit;
(b) to be in consultation with GOI and State Governments;
and
(c) to coordinate Reserve Bank agricultural credit action
and its relations with the cooperative banks system
and other banks engaged in agricultural credit opera-
tion. The Department provides these institutions with
detailed, clear and precise instructions and guidelines
regarding all aspects of agricultural credit criteria,
techniques and lending as well as recovery procedure.
12.       However, despite this widespread State network of PCS, PLDB and
CCB and substantial financial support from the GOI, GOO, the Reserve Bank
of India and ARDC, institutional credit performance has been disappointing.
Causes of concern are:



ANNEX 10
Page 4
(a) unbalanced distribution of PCS, some covering
too small a cropped area to be viable, others
covering areas exceeding their administrative
and lending capacity;
(b) low membership, nowhere exceeding 50% of culti-
vating families;
(c) poor rate of loan recovery, resulting in heavy
overdues and subsequent restrictions on CCB
and PLDB borrowing from the Reserve Bank of
India or ARDC;
(d) managerial weakness due to insufficient profes-
sional expertise of most of administrative staff
and inadequate number of technical officers;
(e) unsatisfactory financial situation and poor annual
results; and
(f) lengthy administrative formalities.
13.       To remedy this situation, measures have been recommended by the
Study Team on Institutional Credit Arrangements (August 1975) and the ARDC
Banking Plan Team (January 1976). These teams urge GOO to implement a plan
which has been drawn up to redistribute PCS. Rehabilitation of weak CCB
progresses satisfactorily and the teams have suggested this be continued
for other potentially viable units. Other principal recommendations of
the teams relate to:
(a) recovery of overdues; deposit mobilization; organi-
zation of a training program for key personnel of
the PCS, PLDB and CCB; reactivation of CCB and SLDB
crash programs; reinforcement of the number of in-
spectors, supervisors and technical units at CCB,
PLDB; and
(b) steps to be taken by commercial banks to increase
their involvement in agricultural credit; and by
GOO to facilitate the extension of commercial bank
branches in rural areas.
14.       The recommendations and steps suggested by the Reserve Bank/ARDC
study teams correctly reflect the weakness of institutional credit in Orissa
and underline the essential measures to remedy it. Unfortunately, the re-
commendations are not new; they have been made many times. What is needed,
and not only in Orissa, is positive action to implement the recommendations
made.



ANNEX 10
Page 5
15.       The elimination of overdues must have absolute priority.  An
implementation procedure must be laid down without delay, a tactical plan
prepared for collection of current loans must be elaborated and clear
instructions be sent to PCS and PLDB. Emphasis must be put on keeping
borrowers under permanent supervision. Simple and effective ways of making
advances on stored crops are also desirable so that farmers are under less
pressure to sell crops on a weak market to repay loans on time. However,
the management of cooperatives involved would need to be strengthened to
take on this new responsibility.
16.       Proper provisions have to be made in order to improve the accuracy
and reliability of appraisal and supervision of loan projects throughout
their lives so as to avoid the recurrence of overdues.
17.       Staff training takes priority second only to overdues recovery
in the implementation of the strengthening measures. A training program
must be laid down and implemented as soon as possible. It must be applied
to all officers having administrative or technical responsibility. Courses
would cover administrative, financial and technical features of cooperatives
credit societies, banking business and fund management.
18.       Lending procedures are expected to be streamlined, once PCS, PLDB
and CCB staff are adequately trained. Meanwhile, intensive inspection and
systematic detection of procedural weaknesses must contribute to removing
the causes of delay. Improvement of the CCB financial position will enable
defaulters to again be eligible for interim credit and therefore eliminate
another cause of delay. CCB branch managers should be allowed to sanction
loans up to Rs 5,000. Simplified procedures should be applied to short-
term borrowers with good repayment records. CCB should be authorized to
open interim credit to PCS in good financial position.
19.       The institutional credit structure of Orissa, once strengthened
in financial and managerial capacity, would be in a position fully to use
its high operating potential and play its role of key financing instrument
for the agricultural development of the State.

INDIA
ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Cooperative Training
I. BACKGROUND
The Department of Cooperatives
1.01     The Orissa Department of Cooperatives is responsible for implementing
government policies and programs affecting the cooperative movement, administering
government assistance to the movement, and supervising the operations and
financial management of the cooperative societies in accordance with existing
legislation. Under a Registrar of Cooperatives, who reports to the Secretary
for Agriculture and Cooperation, and a number of deputy registrars, the
Department employs the following staffs
75 assistant registrars
164 sub-assistant registrars
318 senior inspectors
144 junior inspectors
A number of these are seconded to Agricultural Cooperatives.
Agricultural Cooperatives
1.02     The Agricultural Cooperatives comprise:
The Orissa State Cooperative Marketing Society;
59 Regional Cooperative Marketing Societies, and
3324 Service (Primary) Cooperative Societies.
1.03     The State Cooperative Marketing Society - the apex of all the agricultural
marketing cooperatives in Orissa - has the nominal functions of:
- procurement and distribution of fertilizers, farm chemicals and
equipment;
- procurement and milling of paddy (as an agent of the Government);
- marketing of other agricultural crops;
- processing of rice bran for oil extraction;
- mixing of fertilizers and formulation of farm chemicals, and
- procurement and distribution of cement.
These activities are in fact carried out by the 59 Regional Cooperative Marketing
Societies which are affiliated to the State Society. Of these functions, the
handling of fertilizers is the most important and in 1974/75, Regional Societies



ANNEX 11
Page 2
handled about 60% of all fertilizer sales in the State.
1.04     The Primary Cooperative Societies (PCS) have a recorded membership
of 1,728,000 (44% of the total number of farmers in the State), and their main
activities are to channel credit in cash and kind to their members from the
Central Cooperative Banks (see Annex 10) and distribute fertilizer to members
and non-members. In addition, a number of PCS have begun providing a savings
service to their members; they are increasing the range of farm inputs they
handle, and many distribute consumer goods - particularly controlled cloth.
It is intended that the PCS should also become involved in crop storage and
marketing, and that their managerial capacity be strengthened and their physical
facilities expanded to enable them to fulfill this larger role.
1.05     The average PCS has only 520 members and handles less than 40 tons of
fertilizers. A large number of societies are below this average, and
consequently have insufficient turnover, suffer from lack of funds and weak
management. This forms a vicious circle as, without funds, they cannot pay a
more able secretary which, in most cases, is their greatest need in order to
increase their membership and strengthen their business. Attention needs to
be focused, therefore, on the training of PCS secretaries to increase their
operational effectiveness and enable PCS to cope with a rapid growth in farmer
demand for the services which they should be able to offer and which the project
is expected to generate.
Training Facilities
1.06     The cooperative movement has three training institutions,
all housed in rented accommodation, comprising a Cooperative
Training College in Bhubaneswar for senior staff, and two Cooperative Training
Centers, one at Gopalpur and one at Bargarh, where PCS secretaries and cooperative
supervisors and inspectors are trained.   In addition to the staff of the
Department of Cooperatives and of the Agricultural Cooperatives described above
(numbering about 5,300), there is a total of about 4,900 staff of other sections
of the cooperative movement most of whom would normally receive some or all of
their training at the three existing training institutions,
1.07     The cooperative movement has grown rapidly in recent years and new
responsibilities continue to be assigned to it resulting in a constant need for
in-service and up-grading training. Furthermore, the development of business
and industry in Orissa creates a demand for trained managers, book-keepers, auditors
and accounting staff which syphons off numbers of the trained staff from the
cooperative movement. Thus, the training institutions must cater for a relatively
high staff wastage rate in addition to in-service training and up-grading needs;
but the present training institutions are grossly inadequate for the training
function now demanded of them.
1.08     In addition to the need to improve the existing training facilities, there
is a need to decentralize much of the training of junior staff and increase the
in-service training performed by supervisory staff. There are at present 17
Central Cooperative Banks (CCB) in Orissa (see Annex 10) employing 373 supervisors
who are concerned chiefly with the distribution and collection of credit, most of



ANNEX11
Page 3
which is channelled through PCS. The supervisors provide guidance to PCS
secretaries, who are responsible to the CCB for proper credit accounting.
However, at present, much reliance is placed on rigid formal procedures and
the secretaries receive very little instruction from the CCB staff (or from the
inspectors of the Cooperatives Department who are assigned to CCB). Yet it is
at this level where very effective on-the-job training could be carried out.
Responsibility for Training Activities
1.09    The meed for the training of staff within the cooperative movement is very
substantial and will involve the up-grading of facilities, the training of instructors,
the development of curricula, and the overcoming of considerable logistical problems
arising from the large numbers to be trained. While the Registrar has overall
responsibility for staff training policy, there is no specialized unit within the
Department of Cooperatives upon which responsibility for implementing training policy
can be focused. An intensification of training activities will require full time
training staff to plan, supervise and evaluate training programs.
II. TRAINING PROPOSALS
Up-grading of Training Facilities
2.01    Permanent facilities would be constructed for the Cooperative Training
College in Bhubaneswar and for the Cooperative Training Center in Bargarh. The
facilities at existing Training Center at Gopalpur would be renovated and additional
classroom and hostel accommodation built.   Cost estimates-are given in Annex 19.
Utilization of New Facilities
2.02    The new Cooperative  College in Bhubaneswar would have a theoretical
maximum training capacity of 1,,440 man-months per annum which would be sufficient
to accommodate the following range of courses:

This would be sufficient to provide the pre-service and in-service training
required for senior level staff of the cooperative movement.
2.03     The two expanded training centers would provide a theoretical maximum
training capacity of 2,880 man-months per annum. This is sufficient to accommodate
the following courses each year:
Course                            No.        Trainees/      Months/       Total

2,60
This would be sufficient to meet a 10% wastage from the ranks of existing
Cooperative Department inspectors, and the supervisors, junior managers and
accountants of the agricultural cooperatives, with a small margin remaining for
training of the relatively small numbers of staff of the non-agricultural
cooperatives.
Training of PCS Secretaries
2.04     The training of the 3,324 PCS secretaries has been too large a job for
the two Cooperative Training Centers and this would now become a responsibility
of the 17 Central Cooperative Banks (CCB). At each CCB simple hostel accommodation
for 24 students and a classroom unit would be constructed; only about half the
CCB have sufficient land for these buildings and land would need to be purchased
for those without any.
2.o5     A full-time instructor would be seconded from the Department of Cooperatives
to each of the CCB to organize and implement the training programs. Training
groups would be of approximately 20 and there would be four coursas per year at
every CCB. During the busy season of the year when the secretaries cannot
leave their units, instructors would carry out follow-up training and farmer
education on the role of cooperation.
2.06     CCB staff would also be involved in the training of the PCS secretaries,
together with the Department of Cooperatives' inspectors and State Cooperative
supervisors based at the CCB. The supervisors would also be given responsibility
for follow-up training of the secretaries after they have completed courses at
the CCB. As well as receiving formal classroom instruction, the trainees would
be assigned duties in the banks on an apprenticeship basis during their training
and thus would receive supervised on-the-job instruction in practical aspects
of book-keeping and simple accounting.



ANNEX 11
Page 5
2.07     The CCB would be able to use the teaching units for the instruction
of their own staff and the staff of their respective branches, and the
facilities would also be available for conferences and meetings when not in
use for formal teaching purposes.
2.08     The immediate and long term  success of the work of the agricultural
cooperatives depends upon the effectiveness of the PCS secretaries as they
are the only personnel in permanent contact with cooperative members. The
transference of their training from formal institutions to the CCB represents
a major departure from previous practice, which should benefit all those
involved with training the secretaries, as well as benefiting the secretaries
themselves. The new system needs to be given all possible assistance as it
develops, and funds are therefore included for a number of regional seminars
at which CCB managers and the instructors assigned to the CCB would be able
to meet and discuss the objectives, content and implementation of their training
programs. Staff from the Cooperative Training College and Training Centers
would attend these seminars to learn ways to assist with these training
activities.
2.09     The establishment of training facilities at the CCB would be phased
over three years so that all CCB would not be required to assume this new
responsibility at the same time, and those which start later would be able to
benefit from the experience gained by those which started first. This training
program could achieve the following throughput (assuming two CCB already have
facilities where training could be carried out in Year 1, and new facilities
would be constructed by the end of Year 1 at three other CCB):
Year 1    Year 2    Year 3    Year 4    Year 5    Total
CCB conducting courses       2         5         11         17        17        52
No. Trainees                80 *     400        880       1360      1360     4o8o
(20 trainees/course)
(* assuming two courses in the year at each CCB)
Thus, all existing PCS secretaries could receive training at the CCB during the
project disbursement period and additional capacity would be available for
training replacements.
2.10     Once a system of regular in-service training has been developed at the
CCB, they would also be able to provide training for junior accountants,
storekeepers, salesmen and others who are working in the various agencies served
by the CCB. Staff from the Training Centers would assist with this training.
Implementation of Training Program
2.11     The intensive utilization of the new training institutions and the
training facilities at the CCB described above would require able planning, good



conmunications and efficient administration of training resources.  For this
purpose a Training Officer would be appointed at the headquarters of the
Department of Cooperatives who would be responsible to the Registrar of Cooperatives
for the planning, implementation and evaluation of the training programs. In
particular, the Training Officer would be responsible for the following:
- Coordination of construction of new training facilities.
- Planning of programs of courses at training institutions in
consultation with heads of the training institutions and appropriate
senior cooperative staff.
- Advising the Registrar of Cooperatives on the appointment of teaching
staff for the posts at the new institutions, and the CCB, and arranging
for their further training as required.
-  Organizing seminars for tanagers of CCB and heads of training
institutions to plan the training of POS secretaries.
- Reviewing course curricula and planning course improvements.
- Evaluating training programs, and
-  identifying training needs.
Total Cooperatives Training Costs

INDIA
ORISSA - AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Basic Training of Extension Staff
BACKGROUND
1.01      The intensification of agricultural production in Orissa will
depend largely upon the effectiveness of the agricultural extension service
in changing farmers' attitudes and increasing their technical knowledge.
The introduction of new extension methodology (Training & Visits or T & V)
would increase the effectiveness of the large number of agricultural
extension staff already in the field throughout the State, and improve
communication between farmers and research services. The T & V program
'would provide a considerable amount of in-field, in-service training
for the field extension staff. However, levels of basic training of
many of the existing staff are rather poor and there is need to up-grade
these levels of basic knowledge so that field staff gain more from the
regular in-service training they would receive. There is also need to
strengthen existing facilities for agricultural basic or pre-service training
which were established some years ago for a much smaller training function
than they have today.
Field Staff
1.02      The Department of Agriculture employs (May 1976):
1,272 Village level workers (VLW);
1,603 Fieldman demonstrators, and
232 Agricultural sub-overseers.
3,107
1.03      In addition to the agricultural staff shown above, approximately
2,500 VLW currently employed by the Community Development Department will
be transferred to the Agriculture Department. Thus, the total number of
village level staff employed by the Agriculture Department will become
approximately 5,600 - sufficient for an extension agent to farmer ratio
of 1 : 600 throughout the State.
1.04      Fieldman demonstrators were recruited for special purposes e.g.
to work on district farms or on the jute programs; most have not completed
secondary schooling and received only brief training at farmer training
centers. Recently their pre-service training was up-graded to that of
the agricultural sub-overseers (one year at the VLW Training Centers); but
no new recruits are to be appointed to either category. Where possible,



ANNEX 12
Page 2
existing fieldman demonstrators and sub-overseers having the necessary
ability will be given up-grading training to the ecuivalent of VLW;
those judged unsuitable for further training will be retired and
replaced by fully trained VLW.
1.05      In addition to the need for up-grading training for fieldman
demonstrators and sub-overseers, many of the existing 3,800 VLW received
their basic training more than a decade ago, before major advances in
high yielding varieties and irrigation technology. Some up-grading
training of more senior VLW has been carried out at the three Training
Centers, but this will need to be continued and expanded to up-grade all
of the older VLW who are able to benefit.
Training Facilities
1.06      There are three Extension Training Centers in Orissa, at
Bhubaneswar, Gopalpur and Bolangir, which provide the agricultural training
for village level extension staff. They have been under the control of
the Department of Community Development, but a decision has been taken
recently to transfer them to the Agriculture Department.
1.07      All three training centers were built in the early 1950's and
currently provide three types of courses: a two-year pre-service course
for new VLW (though there are no pre-service trainees currently in training);
a t-wo-month refresher course - the emphasis of which may va'y to meet the
needs of a locality or agricultural situation, and a one-ye .r Higher Course
for more senior VLW with at lease ten years' field service
1.08      Each center has residential capacity for a maximum of 160 trainees,
but in practice this capacity is seldom fully utilized as all the centers
are short of teaching accommodation. Bhubaneswar has three classrooms,
Bolangir two and Gopalpur two, and each center has a small laboratory.
Each center has access to some arable land but, in general, teaching groups
are very large, instruction is very classroom oriented, and teaching staff
have insufficient contact with extension and research staff, or exposure
to field extension situations, to enable them to relate their teaching
adequately to field conditions.
1.09      All of the teaching staff of the centers are graduates and most
belong to the Agriculture Department, even though the centers have been
administered by the Community Development Department. Posting of staff to
the centers has not followed a clear policy; staff have not felt that they
could expect to make a career in agricultural teaching, and most of the
teaching staff have no training in teaching methods.
Training Needs
1.10      In addition to the existing 5,600 junior agricultural field staff
(see para. 1.03) about 1,000 will remain with the Community Development
Department. Thus, the total establishment for which the training centers



AMNEX 12
Page 3
must cater is about 6,600. Training is necessary to replace normal
wastage from this number, as well as up-grading training for the poorly
trained sub-overseers and fieldman demonstrators and older VLW. Assuming
a normal wastage rate of 5%, 330 replacements would be required annually.
1.11      Sub-overseers and fieldman demonstrators number 1835.  Assuming
approximately one third of these are nearing retirement and would not
benefit greatly from up-grading training, 1,200 would remain in service and
need training to strengthen their basic knowledge of agricultural science
and technology.
1.12      Of approximately 3,800 VLW who will be involved in agricultural
extension work, some are recently trained, some have attended the one-year
Higher Course, and others are nearing retirement. Approximately one third
of the total should receive up-grading training - say 1,300. Thus, the
imoediate training need which must be met by the training centers is:
Pre-service training         -          330 annually
Up-grading of older VLW      -        1,300
Up-grading sub-overseers    -         1,200
and fieldman demonstrators
1.13      In addition, new technology, changes in policy, and interest in
new crops or cultural practices, will call for short, specialized in-service
training courses for which the training centers would provide the most
appropriate venue. There will also be an increasing demand for short
courses for contact farmers and rural leaders, which would be held at the
training centers as well as at farmer training centers. At present the three
existing training centers lack the capacity, staff and teaching equipment
necessary to meet these training demands.
Pre-Service Courses
1.14      The largest proportion of teaching hours at the centers is currently
taken up by a two-year pre-service course. The minimum entrance qualification
for this course is matriculation (ten years of schooling), and the course
provides instruction in basic agricultural sciences, followed by applied
sciences, husbandries, farm management and extension. Most of the teaching
is heavily classroom oriented; the centers have little equipment to help them
with their teaching, and limited use is made of the agricultural land
available to the centers for practical instruction. The course includes a
three-month period of practical extension training when each trainee works
with a VLW in the field. While this provides the trainees with valuable
experience of field conditions, little supervised training is achieved during
this time, and thus the two-year course does not at present prepare the VLW
adequately for the demands that will be made upon them once they are posted
to the field. There is, therefore, a need to review the teaching of this course.



Page 4
AEO Training
1.15      Amongst the 278 AEO employed by the Agriculture Department,
roughly one third do not have degree level training. Furthermore, as
with older VLW, older AEO received their basic training before many of
the recent major technological advances took place and have had very
limited opportunities of improving their levels of technical knowledge.
The training role which the AEO would assume with the introduction of
the T & V program would quickly uncover weaknesses in their knowledge.
1.16      The Agriculture Department is discussing a four-month up-grading
course at the University for these older AEO and has funds for this training.
It is planned that accommodation at the under-utilized Plant Protection
Institute in Bhubaneswar be used for housing AEO on this course.
Subject Matter Specialists (SMS)
1.17      SMS are needed at range and district level to provide in-field
in-service training of AEO and VLW, and to provide continuous communication
between field staff and the research stations. Some SMS have been appointed
in the six existing ranges but it has recently been decided to increase the
ranges to 13 so that they coincide with revenue districts, and better
coordination and supervision of field activities can be achieved. Five
SMS are required in each range, so there is the need for a total of 65 SMS
to staff all the ranges. In addition, there is a need for SMS at district
level, because the ranges are too large to enable range level SMS to
provide all VIW in-service training. There is a need for a basic training
program for these SMS prior to their appointment to the field.
Training of Women
1.18      Most of the rural extension activities in Orissa are directed
towards the male members of the rural communities, whereas women play an
important and increasing role in family decision making (including decisions
on agricultural matters), and are becoming progressively more involved with
such things as seed selection, handling of fertilizers, rodent control,
kitchen gardens, compost making, and the keeping of small animals. At
present there is one gramsevika (women's social education officer) per block,
employed by the Department of Community Development, who covers, on an
average, five panchayats and thus has an area to cover equivalent to five
VLW. Most of the work of the gramsevikas is conducted through women's
clubs, which they initiate throughout their areas, and each gramsevika
has between 20 and 30 such clubs. Their work concentrates upon improving
nutrition, health and family planning, child care, home management, agriculture
and animal husbandry. There is a strong response from rural women to the
efforts of the gramsevikas, but their limited numbers and lack of mobility
severely restricts the impact they can make.



ANNEX 12
Page 5
1.19      There are two small Gramsevika Training Centers in Orissa,
providing pre-service and refresher training for staff and short courses
for volunteer women workers from the panchayats, as well as special
courses in support of nutrition programs.   There is a large surplus of
women who have completed secondary school and are seeking employment
(some with graduate and teacher training qualifications), so there are
abundant recruits for the gramsevika program. However, the effectiveness
of the existing field staff could be greatly increased by improving
their mobility, support and supervision.
1.20      At present, in four' of the thirteen revenue districts there
are peripatetic training teams, based at farmer training centers, which
hold "training camps" of three to five days' duration for women's clubs.
The "camps" involve local gramsevikas and are organized through block
development officers.  The teams comprise a women's training officer and
two demonstrators with a vehicle, which carries teaching aids and
demonstration materials, and the teams have proved extremely effective.
The effectiveness of all gramsevikas would be increased if they were
supported by such teams in all districts.
C .  EXTENSION TRAINING PROPOSALS
A. Up-grading of Training Facilities
2.01      The three existing VLW Training Centers would be expanded and
up-graded and a new one added, to provide the necessary in-service training
to existing staff and pre-service basic training for replacements, with
capacity for new demands which are expected to be made upon them in the
future. Cost estimates are given in Annex 20.
Pre-Service Courses
2.02      The period of formal training for VLW at the training centers would
be shortened to one year and this would be supplemented by the regular training
they would receive in the field under the T & V program in practical husbandry
techniques, economics of small farm production, and extension methods.
These aspects of the present course are not being taught well in the formal
teaching situation of the training centers, and should be much more effectively
imparted by the in-field training of the T and V program. As individuals
prove their abilities, and needs for specialization develop, VLW would be
able to return to the training centers for further short, specialized training.
2.03      During the first half of the course, trainees would concentrate
on the basic agricultural sciences, and then proceed to applied sciences
during the second half. To achieve maximum efficiency of utilization of
teaching staff and facilities, there would be two intakes per annum of
40 each.



ANNEX 12
Page 6
This would mean there would always be 'senior' and 'junior' students
in the institutions at the same time, which should have a beneficial
effect upon the student communities at the centers.
2.04      In Orissa, at present, the status of the VLW is not high and
applications for V1W training, though sufficient, do not permit rigorous
selection. The introduction of the T & V program elsewhere in India has
achieved a marked improvement in the status of the VLW, with a
corresponding improvement in the number of applicants for training.
It is anticipated that this will take place in Orissa and enable more
selection of trainees for the pre-service course. This, and the planned
increase in schooling requirement from 10 to 12 years with the introduction
of vocational schools, should provide a supply of trainees with better
science background than many now have.
VLW Higher Course
2.05      The regular in-field training of VLW under the T & V program
would enable the present one-year up-grading Higher Course for older
VLW to be shortened. To qualify for this course, VLW at present have to
have a minimum of ten years' field service which means that many of them
have limited secondary schooling and received a much simpler pre-service
training than is given to VLW now. The object of the up-grading course
would be to strengthen their basic knowledge of agricultural technology,
and an eight month course would be necessary to achieve this.
Up-grading of Sub-overseers and Fieldman Demonstrators
2.o6      Some amongst the sub-overseers and fieldman demonstrators would
qualify for the VLW Higher Course, but most have worked on a single crop
and, while their knowledge on that crop would generally be good, they lack the
broad knowledge of basic agricultural technology which they would require
to work as VLW. Also, there is considerable variation in experience within
this group and their up-grading would require a series of special courses
to suit the needs of the different categories. For the purpose of calculating
utilization of training facilities, four-month courses have been included
for sub-overseer and fieldman demonstrator up-grading.
Training Program
2.07      To provide the training outlined above, a program involving the
following training courses would be necessary:
Course                        Length       Intake        No./year  Total throughput
Pre-service                   12 months      80             1       320 in 4 centers
"Higher" VLW up-grading        8 months      40             119    24o0  " i   it
Sub-overseers and FMD          4 months      40             3       360 in 3 centers

Page 7
2.08      This training program would fully utilize the up-graded
facilities of the three existing training centers with capacity for
160 students each, and one new center with capacity for 200 students.
The up-grading training of 1,200 sub-overseers and fieldman demonstrators
would be completed in three and a half years, and the up-grading of
1,200 VLW in five years. Thereafter the facilities would be used for
refresher training for VLW and AE0 and to meet the growing need for
short courses in specialized subjects for staff and for contact farmers
and local leaders. These, together with demands for livestock training
and new development programs, justify provision of facilities at two
centers to accommodate an additional teaching stream of Lo students.
VLW Replacement Projection
2.09      The total number of VIW in the field (including the 1,000 who
will remain with the Department of Community Development) is about
6,600. The table below shows the effect of an annual wastage rate of
5% on this total, with replacements at an annual rate of 320, once new
facilities are completed.  The following assumptions are made:
(a)  Since all facilities are now being used for up-grading,
no pre-service training is in progress at any of the three
centers. There will, therefore, be no output from the
present two-year course in either 1977 or 1978.
(b)  An intake of 40 will be admitted at three centers in
1977 for a one-year pre-service course to give an output
of 120 in 1978; thereafter the three centers would turn
out a total of 240 per year.
(c)  A fourth center, to be constructed under the project,
would be ready for its first intake early in 1979 giving
an output of 80 in 1980 (see Chart, Annex 29).
