Training Facilities
2.10      The training program outlined above would require an increase in
training facilities which could be achieved by substantial expansion of the



AN. - L
Page o
three existing centers or, alternatively, improvements to existing
centers and the establishment of a new center. Of the existing centers,
the one in Bhubaneswar does not have adequate land for extensive
expansion (though its situation in relation to the university, research
stations and a large farming community is good); and the center at
Gopalpur, while having ample land for buildings, is situated on the coast
in the South-eastern corner of the State at some distance from soil types
and farming communities which are typical of most of the area it serves.
Therefore, rather than attempt substantial expansion of the three existing
centers, one new center would be constructed, the center in Bhubaneswar
would be expanded to accommodate 200 students, and the other two would
be improved to enable them to accommodate 160 students, with teaching
groups not exceeding 40.  The existing centers are all sited in the
center and south of the State. A new center, say at Sambalpur, Kheonjhar
or Baripada, would serve the north, and would increase the potential
flexibility of training within the State. Land would be made available
by Government for the new center.
2.11      The facilities to be provided are shown in the following table:
Buildings                 Bhubaneswar      Gopalpur      Bolangir     New Center
Hostel accommodation         40                              -           200
Kitchen/dining                1                              -             1
Laboratory                    1               -              -             2
Classrooms                    2               2              2             4
Teaching workshop             1               1              1             1
Hall/common room              -               -              -             1
Administration                -               -              -             1
Housing for staff             -               -              -             9
2.12      Teaching workshops and basic equipment would be provided at the
centers. These are needed to enable them to provide practical training in
farm implements, pumps, sprayers, simple engines and power tillers.  Adoption
of simple mechanization is spreading rapidly in Orissa and most VLW have
had no training in this field.
2.13      A second laboratory would be added at the center in Bhubaneswar.
Its central location, and proximity to the university and research stations,
make it the logical place to hold a variety of specialized courses for AEO
as well as VLW. Its present laboratory space is very limited and the
provision of a second laboratory would enable one to be used for chemistry,
soils and fertilizers, and the other for biological subjects.
Teaching Staff
2.14      The teaching staff, who are entrusted with the basic training of
VLW, must have a clear idea of what the VLW would be required to do in the
field and what sort of problems would confront them; they must be in touch



ANNEX 12
Page 9
with research being conducted in their own fields - this applies
particularly to staff teaching applied agricultural subjects - and
should be able to act in a subject matter specialist role for the
in-service training of VLW in their respective areas of specialization.
2.15      To achieve this, teaching staff at the training centers would
be increased; links between the centers and the field extension staffs
they serve would be strengthened, and teaching staff would be made
available to participate in in-field, in service training of VLW.
Staff would also be made available to assist the district officers with
the training of primary cooperative society secretaries in basic
agricultural principles and recommendations. An additional light vehicle
and funds for running expenses would be provided for each center
to give staff the mobility to perform these new duties. In addition,
teaching staff would be included in SMS refresher training programs at
the research stations and would be encouraged to maintain communication
with researchers working in their respective fields.
2.16      Funds are included in the project for 20 man-months of training
for teaching staff to attend training courses in India in adult education
and agricultural teaching methods. These funds would supplement a limited
number of scholarships which are available for this purpose under an
all-India program.
Trainee Stipend
2.17      The present stipend for trainees attending in-service training
courses at the centers is Rs 50/- per month. This rate has remained in
force for a number of years and is now a disincentive to prospective
trainees. This rate would be increased to Rs 75/-. For pre-service trainees
who are not yet receiving salaries, the monthly stipend would be Rs 100/-.
Estimated Cost
2.18      Details are given in Annex 20.
B. Subject Matter Specialists (SMS)
2.19      The number of Range DDA would be increased from 6 to 13 and each
DDA would be assisted by six SMS in farm machinery, agronomy, plant
protection, horticulture, farm management, water management and training.
Some of these SMS are already in post in the six existing ranges. In
addition, three S4S would be appointed at district level who would specialize
in the fields of agronomy, plant protection and training. Thus, a total
of 168 SMS would be needed, 78 at range level and 90 for the districts.



ANNEX 12
Page 10
2.20      There is a limited supply of suitably trained and experienced
men to fill the SMS positions, and the appointment of the 168 SMS would
be phased over the five-year project period to allow for their training
prior to appointment. At district level, a number of SMS in agronomy
and plant protection would be promoted from existing AEO, but the
majority would be young MSc graduates who have recently completed university
courses. These would be employed at a research station for up to a year
before being appointed as SMS to give them the necessary practical
experience.
2.21      Range level SMS would require a broader technical background in
their respective fields, and their training at research stations in
Orissa would be supplemented by courses or study visits to research
centers elsewhere in India. Funds are included in the project for 80
man-months of training outside Orissa (see para. 2.10 and Annex 16).
2.22      Training specialists would assist DDA and DAO with the
identification of training priorities; carry out in-field training of
VIW in extension methods; coordinate the activities of the SMS in the
training of AEO and VLW, and be responsible for liaison with other support
services to ensure that teaching aids and demonstration materials, needed
by VLW, were available as required. They would also liaise with research
stations and training centers in their respective areas; assist with the
planning of courses and course contents, and handle the selection of
participants to attend specific training courses. Many training SMS would
be appointed from existing AED, who have an interest in traib ing; others
would be recruited from outside the service with MSc degree Ln extension
training methods.
C. Training for Rural Women
2.23      Mobile Training Units for rural women would be provided in the
ten ranges in which no peripatetic teams are based at present. Existing
teams would then be confined to a single range. These units would comprise
a Lady Training Officer, two demonstrators and a driver. Field programs
for the mobile units would be organized in consultation with the District
Community Development Officers who are responsible for the gramsevika field
activities. The training units would concentrate on giving short, intensive,
in-field training of one or two days to "contact women" of the women's
clubs at "training camps" (as is done by existing teams at present), and
the vehicles would carry visual aid materials and demonstration equipment
for this purpose.
2.24      The training units would strengthen the impact of the existing
gramsevikas who would help to plan the programs of the units and would provide
the necessary follow-up after the training camps. The units would also be
able to distribute visual aid materials and demonstration equipment to help
the gramsevikas in their work. Training of the demonstrators to operate
equipment carried by the vehicles would be conducted in Orissa by short
attachment to one of the peripatetic teams already operating.



ANNEX 12
Page 11
2.25      The establishment of the new training units would be phased
over Years 1 and 2 to enable staff to be trained and experience to be
gained in their operation. Existing gramsevika training centers and
farmer training centers would be involved in the production of visual
aid materials for use and distribution by the units.

INDIA
ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Farm Management Data Unit
A. Background
Inadequate Data Base
1.        During the course of the Sept/Oct 1975 review and subsequent pro-
ject identification/preparation it became apparent that the reliability of the
basic production data was low and that requisite data--such as land use inven-
tories, yields of improved varieties under field conditions, input-output re-
lationships for major inputs and their prices, and farm income estimates under
different technologies--was not available. The latest available data on costs/
returns in rice production is for 1973/74 and provides only average figures
covering a wide range of situations. There is also no information on differ-
ent technologies; for example, the merits and relative costs of hand labor
against mechanical power for different operations, or hand weeding against
herbicides.
2.        Project support to the Bureau of Economics and Statistics (BSE) for
improvement of area, yield and production statistics would provide more reli-
able basic statistics. Planning of the sector, and evaluation of policy
options at both state and farm level, requires ongoing collection, analysis
and evaluation of farm cost and return data. Similarly, evaluation of the
extension and adaptive research components requires production economic data
collected from farms at different development stages.
Current Status of Farm Management Data
3.        The collection and analysis of farm production economic data has
been confined to a few cost of production studies, well carried out by the
OUAT Department of Agricultural Economics, working in cooperation with the
University of Missouri, and benchmark surveys by the Directorate of Agricul-
ture and Food Production (DAFP). At present the Department has a teaching
staff of twelve, including the Dean of the Faculty of Agriculture, but all
are fully committed to teaching and supervision of research students.
4.        One staff member supervises an ongoing full cost accounting survey
of principal crops carried out on behalf of GOI under an all-India scheme
for paddy, jute and groundnuts. Thirty-three investigators are employed to
cover a sample of about 360 cultivators. Two other major surveys have been
carried out and results published:



ANNEX 13
Page 2
(a) Economics of farming in Cuttack district carried out
between 1967 and 1972 and published in 1973, providing
data on paddy, sugarcane, jute and pulse crops collected
from some 150 holdings;
(b) a farm business analysis of 42 farmers in the Sambalpur
district carried out under the aegis of the Ford Founda-
tion in 1971-73.
5.        In addition, a number of studies have been undertaken by MSc stii-
dlents in part fulfillment of their degree. These have covered the economics
of tubewell and flow irrigation, employment and earnings of agriculture,
capital formation, etc. Special studies have also been undertaken by the
Department, covering Small Farmers Development Agencies (SFDA) and drought
prone areas. As a result of this work, the Department published a Farm
Management Handbook in 1971, of considerable value to extension workers
at all levels but needing regular updating.
6.        In the course of preparation work for the Irrigation (Command
Area) Development (CAD), DAFP project staff have undertaken some benchmark
surveys of farming in the command areas. However, whilst the quality of
the work has been high, the DAFP does not have the staff or organization
to undertake this work on anything but an ad hoc basis.
7.        Apart from collection of basic agricultural statistics, BSE has
conducted an ongoing benchmark survey in Sambalpur district, to measure
results of the intensive agricultural district program (IADP). There is
also an ongoing program on household income, but none of these activities
provide data of much value for agricultural planning.
B. Project Actions
Objectives
8.        A wide spectrum of farm management data is required to provide the
basis for:
monitoring  and evaluating performance in the
agricultural sector;
feedback on costs and returns of present farm
systems to assist in the formulation of price
policy and planning in the sector;
evaluating new techniques coming forward under
the field research program;



ANNEX 13
Page 3
--   evaluating and monitoring the take-up rate, receptiveness
or reluctance of farmers to the extension system cultural
reasons for adoption or non-adoption of recommended prac-
tices, roles of contact farmers in diffusing the received
advice.
9.        Whilst the research component (Annex 4) should fill a major gap
in linking research work with farm level needs through the extension service,
the proposed OUAT Farm Management Data Unit (FMDU) would provide a key link
in interpreting results in terms of farm profitability, and establishing the
role of new techniques in different farm systems.
10.       FMDU would provide an effective data delivery system for collecting,
analyzing and making available the necessary information for this purpose.
Such information would include input/output coefficients, factor/product price
ratios, capital/credit requirements, land/labor relationships and productivity/
income potentials, as derived for existing and alternative production systems
under various agro-ecological conditions, production structures, and organiza-
tional arrangements. With this data the impact of alternative policy measures
regarding small and large farmer development could be adequately assessed and
effective strategies devised and implemented by the Policy Planning and
Coordination Unit being created under the Agricultural Production Commissioner,
to coordinate, monitor and evaluate the project (and to prepare subsequent
agricultural projects).
11.       In summary, the main FMDU objectives would be to:
(a) mount a series of production cost studies on major
crops in the State to provide data to the subject
matter specialists (SMS) for training village level
workers (VLW), and a feedback to PPCU to assist GOO
in sector planning;
(b) undertake cost-benefit studies of new techniques
developed from the adaptive research program,
analyze results following farmer uptake;
(c) provide data for, and revise the Orissa State Farm
Management Handbook, for the guidance of extension
workers and farmers;
(d) provide a farm planning service for the information
of all GOO departments, particularly agencies such as
SFDA and the Tribal Development Agency, to ensure that
development programs are founded on sound, economically
and financially viable, farm plans;.
(e) provide benchmark data as a basis for development poli-
cies and programs for different geographical zones and
social groups;



ANNEX 13
Page 4
(f) monitor and evaluate the results of the package of
practices developed by the research and extension
services.
Organization and Staff Requirement
12.       FMDU would be set up in the OUAT Department of Agricultural
Economics as a separate unit, without teaching responsibilities. FMDU
would be run by a Director at the same level as the present Department
Head and responsible to the Dean of the Faculty of Agriculture. The
Director, Assistant Director and supporting staff, would be located at
OUAT, Bhubaneswar. One Farm Management Economist (FME) would be appointed
per two agricultural range headquarters 1/ and one Fari MIaaagement Assist-
ant (FMA) to each agricultural district, to provide subject matter advice
to extension workers and farmers and to supervise data collection. The
FME would be based at applied research stations to provide a further link be-
tween field and research and to guide planning of applied research programs.
13.       There is little expertise in farm management economics presently
available in the State, outside OUAT, and it would be necessary to recruit
outside Orissa for some posts. In training up farm management assistants,
the Department would assist inittaLly through training workshops. The
Director would be an agricultural economist with specialization in farm
management economy and the Assistant Director would be an agricultural
economist with experience in rural sociology. The job descriptions and qua-
lifications for director, assistant director and farm management assistants
are given in Appendix 1.
14.       Basic data would be collected from a sample of 1,500 farms through-
out the State and be handled by primary investigators employed at the rate of
one per 15 farms. These would be trained in the techniques of farm management
survey work through district level training workshops. Staff requirements
are projected as follows:

Selection of Farm Sample and Methodology
15.       Farm management data would be obtained through selection and ongoinv
rocIoruling of a suitably stratified sample of 1,500 farms covering the range of
farm systems in the State. The sample would he distributed and weighted to
include various farm sizes, cropping patterns, production technology and agro-
ecological situations.  Though some information wouldl be obtained for costs
and returns under various conditLoyis arid practices from these surveys, they
may not cover all available production practices that could and should be
recommended by the extension service.  In thim: evei[, Fr;tr't budgets would be
synthesized from experimental data derived from the expanded adaptive re-
search program and other available research. Finally, wherever possible
and advisable, these surveys would be integrated with those carried out
for GOI, notably by BSE.
16.       The steps in planntling and carrying out the surveys would be:
(a) derive a frequency distribution of agricultural produc-
tion units according to farm size, using available census
da ta;
(b) correlate the administrative districts, wherein these
farms are located, to identify (broad) agro-ecological
zones, thereby further stratifying these farm-size
groupings according to homogenous pedological and
climatic conditions;
(c) draw appropriate samples amongst those designated group-
ings and conduct detailed farm management surveys which
examine individual enterprises and the total farm opera-
tion, including household consumption and expenditures.
17.       It is expected that, in principle, the 1970/71 agricuLtutral census
data would be used as a basis for drawing the sample. The farms selected
would be recorded on an ongoing basis through monthly visits by primary
investigators and results of the whole farm business would be recorded. A
farm management assistant would visit at the beginning and end of the year.
Sample size would be determined following a review of the censuis data and
field research program, but it is envisaged that about 1,500 1/ farms would
be required, i.e., approximately 50 per agricultural district. A pilot run
would be carried out in two or three districts to test the methodology.
Technical assistance at no cost to GOO, would be requested from the Farm
Management and Production Economic HJnit of the Agricultural Services Division,
FAO, Rome, in selecting the sample and developing the methodology. Four

Job Descriptions and Qualifications of
Senior Staff
Director
The Director of the Unit would have had at least ten years' exper-
ience in the field of production economics in a teaching or advisory role.
He would preferably have been responsible for farm management and cost of
production studies. He would have a basic degree in agricultural economics,
preferably a Master's or Doctor's degree with specialization in farm manage-
ment economics.
It would be desirable that he was familiar with farming systems
similar to those in Orissa and had had experience of administration in a
government or university organization.
He would be responsible to the Dean of the College of Agriculture
for establishing and operating a Farm Management Data Unit and his duties
would include:
(a) control of the Unit's funds and all aspects of its
day to day planning and management;
(b) training project staff and ensuring that a proper
management training program is successfully imple-
mented for all levels of staff;
(c) selecting a suitable sample of farms and setting up
an ongoing system of data collection and analysis;
(d) supplying the Policy Planning and Coordination Unit
(PPCU) and the Extension and Research Advisory Panel
with results at regular intervals;
Assistant Director
The Assistant Director of the Unit would have had at least five
years' experience in the field of production economics, including experience
of field investigations. He would have a basic degree in agricultural econ-
omics, preferably a master's degree in rural sociology or agricultural exten-
sion. Alternatively, a basic degree in rural sociology or agricultural ex-
tension, with subsequent experience in field investigations.

Appendix 1
Page 2
He would be responsible to the Unit Director and would assist him
in all aspects of the Unit's work. In particular, his duties would include:
(a) organization of sample selection and subsequent monitor-
ing of the field work;
(b) analysis of the results and preparation of reports for the
PPCU and Extension and Research Advisory Panel;
(c) drawing up curricula and organizing training courses both
for the Unit's staff and for the subject matter specialists
of the DAFP extension service.
Farm Management Economist
A Farm Management Economist (FME) would have had at least three
years' experience in field level farm management/cost of production studies
and would have a master's degree in agricultural economics.
He would be responsible to the Director of the Unit and his duties
would include:
(a) supervision of data collection in the two agricultural
ranges for which he is responsible;
(b) supervision of preliminary aggregation of the data by
farm management assistants and transfer of this data
to the Central Analysis Unit at Bhubaneswar;
(c) assisting applied and adaptive research farms in his
ranges with production economic aspects of planning,
analysis and interpretation of trials results;
(d) running training courses for subject matter specialists
and FMA in his ranges.
Farm Management Assistant
A Farm Management Assistant (FMA) would have a bachelor's degree
in agriculture or agricultural economics and three years' experience as an
agricultural extension officer or equivalent.
He would be responsible to the Farm Management Economist for the
collection, through the primary investigators, and preliminary aggregation
of data from the sample of farms in his district. His duties would also
include the training of primary investigators in the work of farm data
collection.

INDIA
ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agricultural Statistics and Data Processing
I. Background
Agricultural Statistics in Orissa
1.        Reliable, relevant and current data, together with the capacity to
organize and present it for use of planners and policy makers, are prerequi-
sites to effective agricultural planning in Orissa. Efforts to improve the
agricultural situation and establish sound policies and investment priorities
are presently hampered by lack of such data and the human and physical
resources to acquire it.
2.        In the past, three agencies of the Government of Orissa (GOO) have
been responsible for the collection and processing of agricultural statistics:
the Directorate of Agriculture and Food Production (DAFP), the Bureau of
Statistics and Economics (BSE) and the Orissa Revenue Department. DAFP is
responsible for all crop forecasts (including rice, potato and jute) and for
final estimates of area under jute as well as area, average yield, and pro-
duction of 33 minor crops. BSE is responsible for final estimates of area,
average yield and production of rice and potatoes and for estimates of the
average yield of jute. The Orissa Revenue Department was responsible for
conducting the first large-scale census of agriculture in Orissa in early
1970/71, for presentation in 1972. Orissa proposes to conduct such a census
once every five years, and the next is now due, though preparation has not
yet begun. However, it appears that it will be replaced by a Timely Reporting
System (TRS) recommended India-wide by GOI and expected to begin in 1976 (see
para 7 below).
3.        DAFP uses an administrative reporting system based on the village
level worker (VLW) as primary reporting unit. Forecasting of all crops and
estimation of minor crops (areas and yields) is one of many functions which
VLW perform. They are also expected to estimate average yields for high-
yielding rice in the intensive agricultural district (Sambalpur) as well as
under the HYV program (Cuttack and Balangir). VLW also assess crop losses as
a result of natural calamities and disasters. Until recently, they were also
required to prepare statistical reports prescribed by the Community Develop-
ment Department (CDD) for the evaluation of CDD programs. Agricultural exten-
sion work and various other assignments are usually given a higher priority
than statistical work, which consequently suffers in quality and timelines.



ANNEX 14
Page 2
4.        Although VLW keep registers in which they record crop information,
their estimates are subjective and unreliable. They are overworked and are
naturally reluctant to prepare reports which may imply deficiencies in. their
performance. The agricultural extension officer (AEO) meets every month with
VLW in his Development Block and supervises them in the compilation of primary
data from the registers. Some of the reports are oral and the AEO may change
the data supplied by VLW without further discussion. Most AEO feel free to
correct the registers, bring the reported data more in line with targets.
They also usually adjust the data if they feel the VLW have unrealistically
exaggerated the results. Similar adjustments are also made at a more aggra-
gated level by District Agricultural Officers. Thus, the data collected under
the administrative reporting system is poorly supervised, highly subjective
and biased, subject to progressive error and unreliable for agricultural plan-
ning. Recognizing these difficulties, and following the transfer of VLW to
the extension service and reorientation of their duties, the administrative
reporting system and previous forms of agricultural survey work will be dis-
continued in favor of a comprehensive, scientifically-based system of sampling
and enumeration to be carried out by BSE.
II. Bureau of Economics and Statistics (BSE)
5.        The Bureau of Statistics and Economics (BSE) is in the Planning and
Coordination Department of Orissa. In this centralized capacity, it is well
placed to coordinate information requirements from various sources using
Departments, such as Agriculture, and avoiding unnecessary duplication of data
collection and processing activities. BSE is divided into six divisions and
a statistical training institute.
6.        The Agricultural Statistics and Crop Survey Division, headed by a
Deputy Director, will be responsible for implementation of TRS. In addition
to the Deputy Director, the Division includes 4 assistant directors, 14 statis-
tical technicians, (3 statistical investigators, 5 statistical assistants, and
6 junior statistical assistants) and 5 clerks. These 24 staff members, sta-
tioned permanently at headquarters in Bhubaneswar, are assisted by a sizable
field organization. In each of the 13 districts, there is a district statis-
tical officer (DSO) who works half time on agricultural statistics. He super-
vises the work of an assistant district statistical officer (who works exclu-
sively on agricultural statistics) and sometimes one or two statistical inves-
tigators who also work exclusively on agricultural statistics. In Orissa as a
whole, BSE presently employs 45 field inspectors and 211 specially trained
field enumerators (amins) -- all of whom work on agricultural statistics.
This staff of statistical workers will be considerably augmented and strength-
ened in the course of TRS implementation. BSE appears to be competently
managed and staffed and the expansion program associated with the implemen-
tation of TRS is within the capacity of the BSE to handle successfully.



ANNEX 14
Page 3
III. Data Collection and Processing Objectives
7.        The primary objective of TRS, being introduced nationwide, is to
provide accurate, timely and relevant information to planners, policy makers
and others such as the extension service who rely on agricultural statistics
to effectively carry out their tasks. It aims to provide:
-    Area and yield statistics
-    by irrigated and unirrigated areas
-    by high yielding and traditional varieties
-    at Block level for principal crops and at agricultural
district level for minor crops
-    land use patterns
-    Irrigation statistics
-    source of irrigation
-    net area irrigated
-    gross area irrigated
-    area irrigated more than once
-    crops under irrigation by season and by source of water.
-    Price statistics
-    retail prices
-    wholesale prices
-    farm harvest prices
-    Supplementary surveys
-    soil samples
-    labor
-    land tenure
-    production costs
-    fertilizer use
-    pesticide use
-    etc.
-    Social and Qualitative Analysis of the Projects Agricultural System
-    take-up rate
-    receptiveness of farmers
-    cultural reasons for adoption or non-adoption of recommended
packages
-    roles of contact farmers in diffusing the received advice



ANNEX 14
Page 4
8.        Under TRS, estimation of areas and average yields of rice and 11
other important crops in Orissa would be based on a predesigned selectIon of
annual samples, with a complete rotation of the sample every year. This
system is expected to be put into operation in 1976, and should produce the
results equivalent to a complete enumeration census by the early 1980s. The
advantages of this system include the relatively low cost and the timely
availability of current production statistics every year, with a gradual
build-up of an inventory of detailed land-use statistics (area planted and
average yield of 12 important crops at ar,d below the village level). These
detailed village data are needed for planning, policy decisions, and farm
management. In particular, a complete or even a partial replacement of the
random sample every year would provide detailed information on crop patterns
which could be used at and below the village level for field trials and demon-
strations to be conducted by the agricultural extension service in Orissa.
9.        TRS is designed to be responsive to the increasing need for reliable
data at lower levels of aggregation. It provides for a larger sample needed
to develop a sampling frame for collecting statistical data at the village
level. Moreover, detailed data at the village level are needed fot agricul-
tural policy and village work. Such data can only be obtained with complete
accuracy by a complete enumeration of all plots. Inasmuch as a complete
enumeration census cannot be conducted without huge expenditure and lengthy
delays of data processing and evaluation, complete enumeration of all plots
would be carried out in a sample of 3,140 selected villages (6%) during the
kharif season and in another 46 villages durinig the rabi season every year,
with sample rotation.
10.       In addition to the ongoing surveys of rice, potatoes, and jute,
the proposed TRS would cover two cereals (wheat and ragi), three pulses (biri,
mung, and kulthi), three oilseeds (sesame, groundnuts, rape and mustard), and
sugar cane. The survey of jute and mesta, would be extended to cover area as
well as yield. Total cultivated area would also be estimated (without an
estimate of average yield). In all, areas of thirteen different commodity
groups and average yields of twelve commodity groups would be estimated by
the proposed TRS surveys.
11. The present sample design used for the survey of rice would be modified
insofar as the present 180 strata would be extended to 314, where each com-
munity development block would become a separate stratum. Instead of the
present 20 villages selected at random from each of the 180 strata, the TRS
sample for this kharif season would consist of ten villages selected at ran-
dom from each of the 314 blocks (3,140 villages). The major change in the
sample design is below the village level where, instead of selecting six
clusters of ten survey numbers (from about 750 survey numbers in each village,
on the average), the TRS would enumerate all survey numbers. This would pro-
duce a complete record of the total cultivated area, showing separately the
area under each of the twelve crops in every selected village. The TRS pro-
posal envisages a gradual increase of the sample from the initial 6% (3,140
villages out of about 52,000 villages in Orissa) in 1975/76 to 10, 15, and
20% in subsequent years.



ANNEX 14
Page 5
12.       In order to distribute the workload more evenly during the year,
the 3,140 villages would be surveyed in three sub-rounds during the kharif
season. Moreover, an additional 460 villages would be selected in inten-
sively irrigated blocks during the rabi (dry) season, bring the total number
of villages to be covered during the initial year to about 7%. The rabi
survey work, in addition to that during kharif, would permit the TRS to
cover all villages within about seven years. With a complete rotation of the
sample every year, the TRS would be equivalent to a complete census enumera-
tion of all cultivated areas in Orissa over a period of several years.
IV. Staffing Requirements
13.       To implement the TRS program for improvement of agricultural
statistics, and in the absence of VLW transferred to full-time agricultural
works, a large increment of staff will be needed by BSE. The recruitment and
training of staff will be phased over several years (see Annex 26, Table 3).
14.       The major staff requirement would be for primary and supervisory
workers for collection of field data. At full development, about 1,200 Amins
(primary level data collectors) would be required, in addition to supervisory
field staff. This would provide one trained Amin for every 10 sample villages,
including a leave reserve. In addition to collecting data on yields and area,
the Amin would also collect farm harvest prices and producer prices at the
village level. Field inspectors, to supervise and assist the work of Amins,
would be required at the rate of about 1 to every 5 Amins. Additional staff
would be required at each of the 13 District statistical offices where data
are aggregated to the district level for local use as well as organized for
onward transmission to State headquarters. Staff would also be required at
State headquarters level for data quality control checking, tabulation and
analysis. Sufficient and qualified staff are available in Orissa to imple-
ment TRS and recruitment is not expected to pose a problem.
V. Training
15.       One of TRS major objectives is to provide accurate and reliable
data. This requires that the statistical methodology to be employed is under-
stood and strictly applied in the field. A training program to ensure proper
field work on the part of Amins and field inspectors would therefore be con-
ducted, under the project. Three training institutes are being established
to provide the necessary initial training. Refresher courses would be given
at BSE's Statistical Training Institute.
16.       The training courses would cover the sampling and enumeration tech-
niques to be employed in the field. Instruction would also be given on land
survey, settlement (land title) operations, identification of survey numbers



ANNEX 14
Page 6
and land boundaries, cultivation, sowing and harvest practices, general pests
and diseases, and identification of high yielding varieties. Practical field
work would be included.
VI. Equipment and Facilities
17.       Incremental equipment and facilities to implement TRS would consist
mainly of vehicles (motorcycles, bicycles, light trucks) for transportation of
field staff, measuring and weighing equipment, and crop cutting devices. Some
office calculating machines, typewriters and furniture and equipment would
also be needed. In addition, several items of tabulating machinery would
be purchased to augment the automatic tabulating facilities of the BSE (see
Annex 26, Table 2).

ANNEX 28
Page 1
INDIA
ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Financial Implications for the Government of Orissa
Cost Recovery
1.        Incremental costs of the project would be partly recovered through
taxes levied on paddy (or rice) and other foodgrains, pulses and oilseeds
entering the commercial market. Paddy purchases by millers and wholesalers
are taxed at 7% and rice that has not been taxed as paddy is taxed on sale
at 3%; other foodgrains, pulses and oilseeds are taxed on purchase or sale
at 3%. The following is a measure of the effect the project will have on
production. If it is assumed that rainfed and irrigated area were to remain
unchanged (thus eliminating change due to irrigation) and that yield increases
due to adoption of package of practices in Annex 2 would be as shown in
Annex 31 Table 1 (which are not exceptional); then increased production
(foodgrains, pulses and oilseeds) due to the project may be estimated thus:
Present     Year 4    Year 8
---- Yield in tons/hectare----
Area          '000 ha
Rainfed      5,600                 0.78        0.82      0.98
Irrigated    1,000                 1.16        1.28      1.48
6,600                0.84        0.92      1.11
Production ('000 tons)             5,500       6,100      7,300
2.        Taking the recovery rate at 60%, since recovery is confined to
the larger dealers, the annual incremental tax yield from the project would
be of the order of Rs 13 M by Year 4 and Rs 38 M by Year 8:

/a   Total production rice, other foodgrains, pulses and oilseeds less
12-1/2% for cattle feed, seed and waste.
/b   Average sale price for rice and other major commodities.
/c   Tax at 3% on sale value, recovery taken at 60%.
/d   15% of 4.8 M tons.
3.        Government revenues would be prevented from sharp decline, due to
increased local consumption and reduced export tax during the first four
years and would increase relatively and actually by the eighth year. The
annual net increase of Rs 38 M in revenue from Year 8 due to the project
compares with total cost of extension, research and allied activities of
about Rs 150 M (four times).
4.        Government revenues would be further augmented with the completion
of the present land survey operation when potential land revenue collection,
reintroduced in April 1975, is expected to rise from Rs 14 M to Rs 50 M.
September 21, 1976

INDIA
ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Proposed Terms of Reference for Policy
Planning and Coordination Unit
1.        The Policy Planning and Coordination Unit (PPU) will be directly
responsible to the Agricultural Production Commissioner (APC) in a staff
relationship. It will be headed by an Officer on Special Duty with the
rank of Additional Secretary. He will be assisted by one Economist, one
Agriculturist and a Systems Analyst. The Unit will have three cells:-
(a) Project Preparation;
(b) Monitoring and Evaluation; and
(c) Policy Planning.
2.        The PPU will be responsible not only for the Development Project
but for all future projects to be financed by the World Bank including the
Area Development and Ground Water Development Projects already appraised
and all other Projects that may be sponsored or implemented in future.
3.        The respective Secretaries and Heads of Departments will continue
to be responsible for project implementation under the general guidelines and
supervision of the APC, assisted by PPU. The PPU will not be responsible
for implementation but will serve as a contact point between implementing
Departments, GOO, GOI and IDA.
4.        The PPU's responsibilities for project preparation include, but
are not confined to:
(a) assisting the APC in the identification of suitable
priority agricultural projects;
(b) maintaining a schedule of preparation activities to be
undertaken by responsible departments and monitoring
timely preparation;
(c) coordinating and finalizing preparation of Projects
on a continuous basis consistent with available data
and in accordance with priorities as determined by
Government;



ANNEX 30
Page 2
(d) critically reviewing draft Project Reports and data
therein. Arranging inter-departmental dialogue when
necessary;
(e) consulting with the Finance and Planning Departments
of GOO - ensuring integration of Project with the
Plan and annual budget;
(f) final drafting of Project Preparation Report;
(g) securing clearance of GOO and GOI (including Planning
Commission);
(h) sponsoring projects through GOI; and
(i) obtaining such assistance as would be available from
GO1 and IDA for project preparation.
5.        The Project Preparation Cell will have two Research Assistants,
of whom one would be an Economist, and one a Statistician; also one
Stenographer and a Typist.
6.        The PPU's responsibilities for project monitoring and evaluation
include but are not confined to:
(a) establishing project objectives and time tables for
implementation;
(b) overseeing project implementation according to agreed
schedules;
(c) establishing adequate reporting procedures to enable
performance data on Projects including data provided
independently by BSE, OUAT Farm Management Data Unit
and DAFP to be completed and analyzed in a systematic
manner;
(d) assessing project performance against established objectives
and need for adjustments of findings and targets;
(e) submitting regular reports together with recommendations
for action to GOI and IDA;
(f) maintaining liaison with the Advisory Panel of Adaptive
Research and Extension;
(g) coordinating with other Sectoral activities; and



ANNEX 30
Page 3
(h) reviewing policies, identifying constraints and recommending
action; identifying constraints to project implementation and
making recommendations for appropriate action to APC.
7.        The Monitoring and Evaluation Cell will have one Research
Assistant, who will be an Economist, and a Stenographer.
8.        The PPU's responsibilities for policy Planning will include:
(a) analysing resources and potentialities;
(b) retrieving data from BSE/OUAT/DAFP;
(c) based on such data and such analysis and research findings
and on information supplied by respective Departments, advising
the APC on:
(i) long term policy, such as - cropping, land and
water use, grain prices, procurement, storage
and marketing, input pricing a distribution;
(d) assisting the APC in setting intersectoral priorities.
9.        The Cell will have two Research Assistants - of whom one Economist
and one Statistician, one Stenographer and a Typist.
10.       The Systems Analyst will be attached to Cells "B" & "C", the
Agriculturist to Cells "A" & "C" and the Economist to all three.
11.       Staff, if any, engaged in similar activity in the Department/
Directorate will be transferred to this Unit.

INDIA
ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Farm Budgets
1.        To illustrate the possible effect of the project on typical farms,
five farm models have been selected:
(a) Rainfed farm with 0.75 ha cultivable land, growing upland,
medium and lowland rice in the kharif season and rainfed pulses -
the rabi season. 0.5 ha upland rice replaced 50% in year 1 and
100% in year 2 with ragi (finger millet). Medium and lowland
rice on 0.5 ha and 0.3 ha cropped with rabi pulses.
(b)  Rainfed farm with 2 ha cultivable land with similar cropping
pattern to (a).  1 ha upland rice replaced with ragi over 2
2 years.  Medium and lowland rice on 1 ha and 0.5 ha cropped
with rabi pulses.
(c)  Rainfed farm with 5 ha cultivable land with similar
cropping pattern to (a).  2 ha in upland rice replaced
with ragi over two years. 3 ha in medium and lowland rice
and 1.3 ha of rabi rainfed pulses grown.
(d) Partially irrigated farm with 0.75 ha cultivable land,
75% of land irrigable in kharif season and, of this, 50%
irrigated in rabi. Kharif cropping comprises 0.55 ha of
irrigated local rice, partly replaced with HYV rice at
full development and 0.2 ha rainfed rice. Rabi cropping
comprising 0.2 ha rainfed pulses and 0.3 ha HYV irrigated
rice; 0.1 ha of latter replaced with wheat at full develop-
ment.
(e) Partially irrigated farm with 2 ha cultivable land with
similar cropping pattern to (d). Kharif cropping comprises
1.5 ha of irrigated local rice, partly replaced with HYV rice
at full development, and 0.5 ha rainfed rice. Rabi cropping
comprising 0.5 ha rainfed pulses and 0.75 ha HYV irrigated
rice; 0.25 ha of latter replaced with wheat at full development.
2.        The rainfed farms are assumed to take up the package of practices
(Annex 2) adopting improved cultural practices (A) in Year 1, improved
cultural practices plus 20 kg N/ha fertilizer (B) in Year 3, and moving into
HYV seeds and higher fertilizer dressings (C) on 70% of the land in Year 5
and 6. A similar assumption is made for the irrigated farm, except for the
rabi rice crop, where it is assumed that HYV seeds are already being used.



ANNEX 32
Page 2
3.        Tables 1, 2, 3, 4, and 5 summarize the total financial implications
for each farm. Other assumptions common to the five models are:
(i) working capital loan equivalent to 30% of value of inputs
(seeds, fertilizers and chemicals) at present with
incremental requirements supplied in addition until Year 6,
after which farmer assumed to supply larger share and full
requirement at Year 7. Interest on working capital, six
months at 12%;
(ii) loan for implements repaid over three years, one year grace
period; interest at 12%;
(iii) family consumption equivalent to Rs 1,400, deducted from
Balance after Debt Service to derive Cash Balance, is based
on 150 kg rice per each family member per year; or 900 kg
rice, 1.4 tons paddy per family of six members;
(iv) prices for products are paddy Rs 1,000/ton, finger millet
Rs 1,100/ton, pulses Rs 1,600/ton, wheat Rs 1,300/tons;
(v) operating costs include farm inputs (seeds, fertilizers and
chemicals), and irrigation charges, plus hired labor, bullock
costs, and implement repairs.

INDIA
ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Marketing and Prices
A. Present Situation
Marketable Surplus
1.        Most of Orissa's total production of about 4 M tons of rice is now
consumed by the farm families that produce it. Nevertheless a not insignifi-
cant amount, some 12%, or about 480,000 tons, comes on to the wholesale
market. Including trade at primary village markets, the total traded may be
as much as 25 to 30% or about 1 M tons. Even a cultivator who raises only,
say, four months' consumption from his land, may retain only three months'
consumption and sell the rest at harvest to meet immediate expenditure. Later
in the year he buys foodgrains, either from the public distribution system or
from bigger cultivators in the village.
2.        In the case of other foodgrains and pulses, the proportion of mar-
keted crop is higher, up to 50%; but total production is comparatively low,
so that approximately 150-200,000 tons each of other foodgrains and pulses
enter the market.
Market System
3.        There are about 1,400 primary or village markets in the State, so
that producers, except in the less populated parts, normally have direct
access to a weekly outlet. There are also approximately 170 assembling mar-
kets, linked with a small number of terminal markets at district centers,
such as Cuttack and Berhampur, where grain is collected in bulk for storage
and distribution in major towns or movement to another State. At village
markets, producers often sell direct to consumers. They also sell to traders
usually local, who stock for resale to wholesalers at secondary markets.
4.        Producers are generally able to deliver their small quantities to
the local market by headloads in baskets, or in bullock carts. There are
plans to improve market access of villages in isolated areas by road building
under a GOO "minimum needs program". Overall communication in Orissa, however,
is poor compared to all-India standards and large scale movement of grains
from such markets to secondary and terminal centers is often difficult.



ANNEX 33
Page 2
Rice Procurement
5.        In order to stabilize prices and to help ensure minimum food needs
are met, especially during shortages, it is Government of India policy each
year to procure a substantial proportion of marketed grain for stocking and
resale. In Orissa State, the annual procurement program applies only to paddy
and rice and now consists of three main elements:
(a) The State Government (GOO) official monopoly for the
wholesale trade in rice. Licensed millers are required
to buy paddy at officially fixed prices and deliver rice
under GOO directions. When open market prices are higher
than the official procurement prices, it is inevitably
difficult for GOO to enforce control effectively. During
the 1974/75 season, GOO used a compromise scheme whereby
millers were required to deliver 75% of their output at
official prices and were allowed to sell the remaining
25% on the free market at more attractive prices. For
1975/76, when supplies were more plentiful and open market
prices lower, GOO reverted to the system of monopoly whole-
sale trade.
(b) Because it is difficult to procure adequate tonnages
through the millers, GOO introduced, for the 1975/76
kharif season only, a levy on the larger paddy producers,
whereby every grower with more than 4 irrigated or 8
unirrigated acres of paddy was required to deliver a
portion of his output to official purchase centers.
The amount of the levy was related to his acreage
according to a sliding scale and he was paid for it
at the official procurement price plus approved
transport costs.
(c) The authorities also purchase local handpounded and huller
processed rice when the local situation required, as well
as offering to buy paddy and rice from voluntary suppliers.
In addition, stocks may be requisitioned from bigger pro-
ducers at official prices where this is considered neces-
sary to discourage hoarding.
6.        To help make these measures effective, the unlicensed movement of
paddy and rice either out of the State, or out of surplus producing areas
within the State, has to be prohibited. There are, nevertheless, great
practical difficulties in enforcement especially when producers or millers
are required to sell substantially below free market levels.
7.        In implementing its procurement and distribution program, GOO
uses a variety of agencies, including the District Collectors' administration,



ANNEX 33
Page 3
the Revenue Department, the Food Corporation of India (FCI), private millers,
cooperatives and individual wholesalers and retail merchanLs. During 1975/76,
GOO was not able to arrange for FCI procurement, and relied on its own adminis-
tration, operating primarily through private millers but also through coopera-
tives. The cooperatives which, by end of October 1976 had procured about
34,000 tons rice (15% of total procurement to that time) are not at present
equipped, either at primary or regional or Apex levels, to play a major role
in procurement, though GOO proposes to build up their responsibilities in
this field.
8.        Orissa, like other surplus producing State, is encouraged by GOI
to procure more rice than is needed for its local needs and so contribute
surpluses to the national buffer stock. Total quantity procured for all
purposes has varied from over 300,000 tons in 1969/70 to less than 75,000
tons in 1974/75 while dealings with the central pool have varied from a
235,000 tons contribution in 1968-69 to a demand for rice from the pool in
1974/75. Nevertheless procurement has never exceeded 8% of total rice pro-
duction and typically is 5-6%. In 1975/76, a very good year, procurement from
the 1975 kharif and part of the 1975/76 rabi had reached the target of 175,000
tons rice by 31st May and was expected to reach 234,000 tons by the end of
October 1/. Some 75,000 tons were expected to be offered to the central pool.
Storage
9.        In the private sector, storage available to traders and rice millers
is generally adequate for their needs, especially as the small scale buyers
turn over their purchases rather rapidly. However, in the public sector,
warehousing owned by FCI in Orissa is at present being used only for holding
foreign imported wheat. The Central Warehousing Corporation (CWC) and the
State Warehousing Corporation (SWC) also provide specialized storage for
central grain stocks, and the latter has recently been commissioned by GOO to
store 40,000 tons of rice. There is some pressure on good warehousing in the
State and further construction has been planned.
10.       The cooperatives have about 24,000 tons of storage available for
paddy/rice at the regional level and present capacity for the public sector,
including storage hired in 1975/76, is estimated at 251,000 tons comprising:
1/ Procurement year is 1st November - 31st October.

A further 76,000 tons are under construction so that, with a 70% utilization

erable pressure on storage and some paddy had to be stored under canvas on
wooden platforms.
Processing
11.       There are 2,985 rice mills officially recorded in the State, of
which the great majority are small hulling units. The larger mills can only
operate legally as official GOO agents and 177 are so licensed, 42 of which
are owned by cooperatives. Total milling capacity of the large mills is
estimated at about 1.1 M tons paddy, of which 90% is in the private sector, 7%
in the cooperative sector and 3% under FCI:
Annual Milling Capacity
FCI    Cooeratives       Private Millers       Total
---------------Thousand Tons Paddy---------------
18         77                 1,025            1,120
There are also large numbers of unregistered small hullers, estimated at
3,000-5,000, which handle a significant share of the paddy traded locally or
milled directly for producers.
12.       Though there is some scope for modernization, the capacity of the
rice milling industry in Orissa is ample for present and prospective needs,
and it is estimated that on average, the larger mills are operating at only
one-third of their full capacity.



ANNEX 33
Page 5
Prices
13.       Rice.  While the procurement system has some dampening effect on
average producer prices, the effect is offset somewhat because, when supplies
are seasonally plentiful, Government procurement reduces availability on the
open market and the procurement price acts as a support. Government pro-
curement price and average annual wholesale market prices for paddy compare
as follows:
1971     1972      1973    1974      1975      1976
------------    Rupees per ton paddy------------
Wholesale /a
market price       608       729       748    1032      1375 /b   N.A.
Procurement Ic
price              570       570      590      720       780      780
/a Annual average of prices collected from main wholesale markets;
source Food and Civil Supplies Department.
/b January to June.
/c Price for fine grade; course or superfine + Rs 40 per ton.
14.       In coastal districts there is effective competitive among traders
for available produce and producers get a fair price, but in nany areas where
communication is poor, especially in the hilly tracts, compe_ition is less
and local traders tend to pay much lower prices. This tends .o be so, not
unnaturally, in other areas also when there is a bumper crop. Small producers
are often in a weak position and local traders, acting as agents of principals
in the secondary markets, appropriate to themselves a larger share of the
consumer rupee.
15.       The good 1975 kharif crop provided an unusually large surplus of
rice an the open market price fell temporarily below the procurement price
in early 1976, subsequently recovering to around the procurement level.
B. Marketing of Project Output
Marketing Organization and Storage
16.       The pressures on the marketing system in 1975/76 emphasize the need
to look ahead and make provision for expected incremental production under
the project. With total output expected to produce an exportable surplus of
0.4 tons by 1980 and over 1 M tons by 1984, compared with a mean of 0.2 M



ANNEX 33
Page 6
tons in 1970/71 - 1973/74, the GOO procurement organization and storage
facilities will require strengthening. An all-India study of storage needs
for foodgrain over the next decade is currently being undertaken by the
Hyderabad Staff College, under an IDA credit. The results of this report
are being studied and would provide the basis for a sound contruction program.
GOI is discussing with the World Bank the possibility of finance for a grain
storage project and it is in this context that the accommodation of Orissa
surpluses should be considered, since procurement and resultant storage is
an all-India problem.
17.       With a large increase in the export surplus, the official procure-
ment price is likely frequently to be the effective support price. However,
since GOI is ready to acquire large tonnages to build up a national buffer
stock over several years, output in Orissa which is surplus to local market
needs should be readily absorbed, at least until India as a whole achieves a
reliable self sufficiency in foodgrain production. The current paddy procure-
ment prices can thus be reasonably taken as the basic prices for producers
under the project.
October 28, 1976

Page 1
INDIA
ORISSA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Economic Impact and Rate of Return
1.        Economic viability of extension and research, related training, and
farm implement demonstration, the main production oriented components of the
project, can be demonstrated by determining the yield increase necessary to
achieve an economic rate of return equal to the opportunity cost of capital
in India (thought to be about 12%). Full incremental investment and operating
costs, have therefore been discounted at 12% and the discounted incremental
net farm benefit stream (and hence crop quantity and yield) required to meet
that rate derived.
2.        In deriving the cost stream, extension, research and training costs
have been aggregated. No residual values have been placed on buildings or
equipment, since the objective is to indicate how small an incremental bene-
fit is necessary to achieve the illustrative 20% rate of return. Financial
prices have been used for all cost items. The foreign exchange component is
slight, so that shadow pricing of foreign exchange costs would have a negli-
gible effect on the result and is not attempted.
3.        The benefit stream and conversion to a yield figure to achieve the
given rate of return have been derived on the following assumptions:
(a) Benefits required have been measured against the total area
under paddy which is estimated at 4.5 million hectares. 1/
(b) An economic price for paddy of Rs 1,490 per ton is based on
Bank Commodity Price Forecasts, May 17, 1976 for milled rice
1985 at 1976 constant prices. Prices for Thailand 5% brokens
have been adjusted to allow for lower quality, that is 25-30%
brokens (Table 2).
1/   Whilst cropped area will increase, some switch to other cereals on
poor rice soils is expected so that rice area will remain fairly
constant.



ANNEX 34
Page 2
(c) The economic value (Rs 1,195 per ton) is derived by
deducting the incremental operating cost of producing
an incremental ton of paddy under the project (Rs 295)
from the economic price of paddy (Rs 1,490). Thirty
percent of all incremental labor required, 10 man days,
is considered to be hired at Rs 7/man-day.
(d) Benefits generated by the project would be 10% of
total benefit required to achieve the notional rate
of return by the end of Year 2, gradually increasing
to 30%, 60%, 80%, 90% in successive years to reach
100% by the end of Year 6; that is full benefits
required achieved in Year 7 (Table 1).
4.        Table 1 shows that, in order to realize a rate of return equal to
the opportunity cost of capital annual incremental production value from year
8 onwards, at 1976 prices, would need to reach about Rs 42 million (US$4.6
million). This is equivalent to an average yield increase over and above the
without-project situation of about 35,000 tons of paddy per year from year 8
onwards. Even if only 20% of farmers benefit at all, they would need to in-
crease their yields by an average of 40 kg/ha paddy (25 kg/ha rice). If only
contact farmers plus an additional 5% were to benefit, they would each need to
increase paddy yields by an average of only 60 kg/ha (40 kg/ha rice). Expe-
rience with reorganized extension in other States of India suggests these yields
would be exceeded by a very wide margin. The high expected rate of return is a
result of the substantial benefits of extension and research derived at rela-
tively small cost, from radical improvement in the effeciency of an existing
large extension service (with consequent sunk costs) and because improvement
in on-farm production is likewise expected to be achieved at little extra cost
to the farmer who would carry out existing operations more effeciently.
5.        Project risks are difficult to forecast being dependent on the
human response and the vagaries of the weather. However, returns on the
relatively small investment should remain satisfactory under nearly all
conceivable conditions.
